For Connor, Clara, and Calum—my brilliant grandkids—and all the
future little dreamers still to come. You are the next generation, and I
believe with all my heart that your creativity will be limitless, your
laughter will be contagious, and your joy will know no boundaries.
For all those dear souls who’ve entrusted their stories to me, for my eleven
grandkids Luke, Sadie, Teddy, Duke, Kalen, Tori, DJ, Nacho, Memo,
Kiki, and Nana, and especially for Frish and your willingness to make
meaning in this adventure together.
OceanofPDF.com



INTRODUCTION

The Greatest Design Problem of All
Allison was stuck.
She wasn’t stuck in the way you get stuck in traffic, or stuck on a logic
problem, or even stuck on a decision between two good options. It was a deeper
kind of stuck—the kind where you’re not even sure what’s wrong, let alone how
to fix it.
Allison was one of 288 accountants attending an annual conference in
Kansas City, where Dave was giving the keynote. After the talk, Allison
approached Dave. “Hi,” she said, “I read your book a couple of years ago and
just loved it! Can I ask you a question?”
Now, we’ve had this conversation more times than we can count. People like
Allison—more than a million of them at this point—have read Designing Your
Life (DYL) or taken a course based on it, and it resonated. The book’s core
message is deceptively simple: You can thrive in your life if you Get curious.
Talk to people. Try stuff. Tell your story. But those simple ten words? They
unlock something. And while we never take it for granted, we’ve seen the DYL
approach work for people again and again. The reframes and tools help people
take action, shift their mindset, and begin building a life that feels more like their
own. So, when Dave heard Allison’s opening line, he thought he knew where
this was headed: Here comes the part where she tells me how much it helped—and
now she wants her husband, boss, or kid to read it, too.
But that’s not what happened.
Instead, she sighed and said, “I made some changes after reading the book,
and those changes worked okay, but my life doesn’t seem much better. I work



hard, but it doesn’t feel rewarding. I’m feeling trapped and like I’m a failure.
What did I do wrong?”
Dave wasn’t expecting this question, so he asked about her situation and
learned that she and her husband were a working couple with two preteen kids, a
small but comfortable house, and two used but reliable cars. They both had
good jobs, and indeed Allison had been able to make some real improvements at
work and home using our design thinking tools, though they were living with
real constraints on their time and their money.
On paper, her life looked pretty good: a decent job, enough money to live
comfortably, a solid family, a good neighborhood. From the outside, there was
no reason for her to feel stuck.
But for Allison, every day felt like a version of lather, rinse, repeat. She’d wake
up, go into the office—or the kitchen table on the two days a week the firm let
her work remotely—and spend another eight to ten hours doing taxes and books
for her thirty-seven clients’ small businesses. Dinner was usually something
quick and easy for her family of four, and after they were asleep, Allison often
stayed up late, scrolling through her phone, watching everyone else’s seemingly
amazing lives. A picture of her college roommate hiking Machu Picchu. A
distant cousin announcing a big promotion.
Allison ended most of these nights by tossing her phone aside in dismay and
then feeling guilty for her discontent. At forty-two, she had the life of her
choosing, a beautiful family, and no big regrets, but she still couldn’t shake the
constant feeling of dissatisfaction. Sure, most of her work duties were a bit
boring and she hadn’t made it to Machu Picchu yet, but those small
compromises didn’t seem big enough to make her feel so lousy. She knew she
should be more grateful, but it felt like something was missing.
It wasn’t like she was miserable; she just wasn’t thrilled.
She wasn’t exactly lonely, but she also didn’t feel deeply connected.
Everything was okay, but okay didn’t feel like nearly enough.
Allison spent a lot of time during her perfectly fine day at her perfectly fine
job in her perfectly fine life, wondering, What’s missing?
Sometimes, she would jolt awake in the middle of the night with different
questions racing through her mind.



Why am I not happy?
Is this all there is?
What’s wrong with me?
Sonya, a former student of ours, knew the word for her similar discontent:
Weltschmerz. A German word (much like the French ennui) that translates
literally to “world pain” but more loosely means the dissatisfaction caused by the
difference between the world as it is and the world as it should be.
After Sonya graduated with a computer science degree, she had gone to a Big
Tech company with a large starting salary. She spent her days writing software
for some of the most popular applications on the internet, and her code was
being used by millions of people across the globe. Like Allison, Sonya thought
she had everything she had ever wanted. She had a great career, enough money,
job security, professional status. But Sonya wanted more. Not more money or
more job titles—she wanted more meaning in her life.
Sonya felt guilty for being so dissatisfied and restless; for wanting things that
felt vague and nebulous to her computer science brain. Meaning and purpose
felt squishy, embarrassing almost, but she still wanted to find it. Or create it. Or
feel it.
Like Allison, her life was fine, but she hoped that there was something more
on the other side of “fine.”
Sonya came to visit Bill in office hours a couple of years into her tech job (we
offer “office hours for life” to all our current and former Stanford students).
Sitting in Bill’s office, visibly uncomfortable, Sonya finally said the quiet thing
out loud.
“I know I should be happy, and all my friends tell me how lucky I am to have
such an awesome job, but it’s just not working for me. I’m not that happy, if I’m
being honest.”
Bill asked her one of our favorite questions to ask in office hours: “Can you
tell me more?”
Sonya let out a deep sigh. “I feel bad for even saying this—I mean, this was my
dream job—but writing code to make it easier to find a video someone wants, or
helping our AI push the latest K-pop hit, is just okay. I don’t feel like it’s enough,
and I’m not talking about money. Sure, our platform empowers the world, but



at the end of the day, it is all about harvesting people’s personal data so that we
can sell them more ads. That’s what makes us so profitable. But I keep
wondering… is there something more I could be doing? Something more I should
be doing?”
“What would that look like?” asked Bill. “Tell me more.”
We want to be clear that office hours can sometimes sound a little like talk
therapy, but it’s not. Former students usually come to office hours because they
have what we like to call a wicked problem. As designers, we love wicked
problems. They aren’t called wicked because they are bad or evil but because they
have no right or wrong answer, no clear or singular resolution. The way we
approach wicked problems, whether in office hours or in the design lab, is with
curiosity and empathy. The design thinking process always starts with a
thorough understanding and examination of the human condition that
underlies the need.
So, when we ask someone to tell us more, it isn’t therapy—it’s one of the first
steps to designing our way out of the stuck.
“At school I had friends, I was in Fleet Street [a Stanford a cappella singing
group], and I had a great social life. But now because of work, I have almost no
social life. I write code, so I spend most of my time alone. Post-COVID, we only
go into the office for bug meetings or when there’s a build, so I hardly ever get to
socialize with my coworkers in real life.
“I’m kind of lonely,” said Sonya, “but it’s more than that… it’s…”
Bill waited patiently as Sonya searched for the words.
“It feels kind of cheesy to say this out loud, but there just has to be more to
work and life than this. I want more meaning, and I have no idea where to find
it.”
Sonya looked down at the floor intently after she said this, as if maybe some
of her meaning was hiding under the rug.
Bill asked, “What do you think you want to do?”
Sonya didn’t look up from the floor at Bill’s question, and Bill could feel her
discomfort. Whatever she was going to say was hard to say out loud.
“Whatever it is, it’s okay,” said Bill.



And then he waited. Until finally, Sonya took a deep breath and said, “I think
I’m going to quit my job and start over. Some friends from school started an
organic farm in the San Juan Island in Washington…” She looked up at Bill and
smiled for the first time. “I think I’ll go there, pull some weeds, and just try to get
my life sorted out.”
All Bill could say was, “Wow, I didn’t see that coming.”
And then he smiled back at her, because with design thinking, there are no
wrong answers, no bad choices, only new things to try as you prototype your
life.
Something More
We have had a lot of students like Sonya. There’s Ergen, whose job is creating the
next-generation financial platform for the world’s fifth-largest bank, and David,
who works on advanced AR/VR systems at Apple, and Elissa, who is working
for a hot digital health start-up, trying to find a cure for diabetes.
All of them were making good salaries, working hard on objectively highimpact projects, and all had proud parents who were happy that their
investment in a college education had paid off. But Sonya and Ergen and David
and Elissa all quit their first jobs within two years. Each of them felt unfulfilled
and lonely and had no idea what to do about it. Quitting was the only solution
they could think of to their wicked problems.
But none of them needed to burn their lives down and start over.
And neither do you.
We’ve heard the same story about the elusive search for something more from
folks at all stages and ages of life and career. Recently, a venture-backed group
that was facilitating a confidential, invitation-only community of 350 top
technology product leaders in the world—chief product officers, heads of R&D,
people like that—reached out to Dave and asked if he could help them.
Dave asked Mattea, the community manager, to tell him more. These weren’t
recent grads new to the working world and finding their way. These business
leaders were at the top of their game, building leading-edge technology used by
billions and making a ton of money. What was their problem?



“What they’re telling me is they’re unfulfilled,” said Mattea. “They all
thought that once they got into these important positions and made their mark
that they’d really feel terrific about their work and themselves, but they just…
don’t. They aren’t unhappy, but they’re not nearly as satisfied as they expected.
It’s just not as meaningful as they were hoping for. Can you help them?”
Before Dave could answer, Mattea leaned in closer and corrected himself. “I
mean, can you help us?”
Dave said he could.
While Dave was talking to Mattea, Bill was in Hong Kong talking to George,
a hotel manager (you know you travel too much when you are close personal
friends with your hotel manager).
“How’s it going?” asked Bill.
“It’s tough right now,” said George. “I can’t keep staff, and my best
performers are the ones quitting. We had to let go of a lot of great people during
the pandemic, and most of them didn’t come back.”
“Tell me more,” said Bill.
“I pay well, in the top 10 percent for the hotel industry, and I’m willing to
train people and advance them quickly. When they quit and I ask why, they tell
me that they want a more meaningful job. They want more autonomy, and they
want to learn and grow, not just as a hotelier but as a person. What am I
supposed to do with that? I’m not a psychologist, I run a hotel. I can’t figure out
their lives for them! I can’t give them meaning and purpose.”
George is right—he can give people a job, but he can’t give them what they
want most: a meaningful life.
That isn’t something that can be given to you.
But you can design for it.
The Weight of the World
In office hours, our hyper-connected Gen Z students—awash in a nonstop feed
of war, climate chaos, and widening polarizing politics—keep asking different
versions of the same pressing question: How can I possibly focus on designing a



more meaningful life when people are suffering and there are so many awful
things happening in the world?
This is a great question. A hard question. A true question. And, we suspect,
one of the reasons all of us can feel a bit guilty for our own life dissatisfaction, for
wanting it all to matter more than it does on any given Tuesday.
And we do not mean to be glib or to minimize the suffering of so many
people, but our instinct when we come across problems this big, this wicked,
and this pernicious is to find a reframe that allows us to make some forward
progress on the problem. The goal of the reframe isn’t to trivialize the original
problem—not at all. The goal is to create a question that gives you the freedom
to make choices and take action.
It is also good to remember that, because of evolution, we tend to notice
things we perceive as negative more strongly than we notice the positive. So…
If you’re trying to make ends meet and groceries cost more this month than
last, you notice.
If you are looking for a job in the middle of a downturn in your industry and
it’s harder than last time, you notice.
If you have worked hard on some social issues and it seems like your
community is taking two steps back these days, you notice.
If you’re used to having four well-balanced seasons, and the last few years
have brought wild swings of rain and flooding, heat and drought, you notice.
If, like Atlas in the Greek myth, the weight of the world’s problems seems to
be fully on your shoulders, you can’t help but notice. Life is hard for everyone,
and it’s easy to fall into despair. To think that nothing matters, people suck, life
is too messy, and we are all doomed to lives of quiet misery.
It’s equally easy to not choose despair.
There’s a way to design your way forward, make choices, and take action in
the midst of chaos, despite the chaos. Even in radical opposition to the chaos.
Designing your life so it feels more meaningful is the best alternative to
despair and misery that we know.



And besides, being a miserable individual never made the world less miserable
as a whole.
Designers always start with the world the way it is, so dealing with big issues
is familiar to us. However, once you learn to think like a designer, new ideas
come fast and furious, and you find there is always something to build, to
prototype, to create hope for the future.
We promise.
All of Us
This longing for more meaning may be one of the most universal things that all
humans want: lives that are generative and joyful, fulfilling and connected, lives
that are about more than just getting through each day, paying the bills, maybe
taking a vacation now and then. And too many people are finding too few
answers for how to get what their hearts keep telling them they were made for.
The meaning and purpose that they need.
The quest for meaning can seem too big and too overwhelming, but here’s
the thing: There is something you can do—really, actually, do—to experience a
more meaningful life. Today. Right now. In this exact moment.
That’s why we wrote this book. To give you some ideas and tools you can use,
immediately, to start making real progress in finding more meaning in your life.
Finding more meaning doesn’t have to be hard. We just need better ideas, better
practices, and better guidance on this quest. Each of us deserves a life that is
meaningful every single day, even on the bad days and the hard days. Even on the
days that are boring or feel full of endless drudgery.
We’ve met a lot of Allisons. And Georges and Sonyas and Matteas. We’ve met
a lot of folks who feel overwhelmed by the “weight of the world.” In fact, we’d
wager that most of us, at some point, have felt exactly like all of them. Stuck,
frustrated, unsure why life doesn’t feel the way we thought it would. We’ve each
felt our own version of Weltschmerz—and set off on our own quiet quest for
meaning.
And some of us immediately got lost.



We are no exception to this. We’re both very much in the trenches of figuring
out how to design our lives meaningfully and joyfully in the stage of life we find
ourselves in. Writing this book in 2025, we find ourselves in a radically different
place from when we were writing our previous book in 2021. In those four short
years, Dave lost his wife, Claudia, to cancer and is now—gratefully—poised to
remarry the wonderful Frish. Bill lost his mom, his dad, and his mother-in-law.
Kids have moved out of the country. Grandkids were born. Our Stanford Life
Design Lab team completely turned over, and the university went through two
presidents. Dave turned seventy (and Bill isn’t that far behind). What used to
work for us in our forties, fifties, and sixties isn’t working anymore, and we’re
hitting the age where having plenty of time to work on the meaning problem
isn’t a luxury we can depend on.
But we know it’s not too late. Not for us, and not for you.
Luckily, we’ve both been taking a pretty intentional approach to our lives—
in very different ways—since our twenties, so we’ve got more than a century of
combined trial and error with this particular design project. One lesson that
keeps repeating is this: Life’s going to change, and you’d better change with it.
The fact that it keeps evolving is both a challenge and a delight.
And we are writing this book for ourselves as much as for you.
From a distance, we look pretty similar (well, Dave’s taller and talks faster,
but that’s about it). Up close, however, we are very different people. We had very
different childhoods. Dave believes in God and likes Jesus a lot. Bill’s an atheist
and likes Nietzsche a lot.
We have different interests, hobbies, politics—you name it. But we are in
complete agreement on what it means to be human. Our minds may have
differing perspectives, but our hearts long for exactly the same things, and we live
in exactly the same world. Our shared mission from day one back in 2007 when
we started our class at Stanford has simply been to help people become more
fully human. We like to say, “If you get the human part right, you can’t go
wrong.” So, for almost twenty years now, we’ve tried to get the human part right
—which simply means that when it comes to solving for meaning, we are
offering a way that can work for anyone and everyone.
Whether it’s for our clients or our students or ourselves.



How to live a meaningful life is one of the most wicked, wicked problems.
But before we get started, let’s figure out where we are.
The Way Forward
Before we go further, we need to make one thing perfectly clear: This is a design
book.
This is not a philosophy book, or a psychology book, or a spirituality book,
or a mindfulness manual, or an inspirational guide. Those are all wonderful
kinds of books, and we’ve read lots of them and encourage you to read some,
too. But this is not one of those books.
It’s critical to keep in mind that everything in this book is directed toward
helping you to be effective at making more of something that you want in life.
And that something we are going to make is more meaning.
This is a design book.
For designers.
To make things.
And you are a designer.
We often get asked by new students, clients, or readers, “So, what am I going
to get?” and we always give the same answer: ideas and tools. Our job is to
provide some key ideas that can reframe the problem and resolve a few critical
dysfunctional beliefs to free you up to more creatively design a better life, and
then to give you practical, doable tools to turn those ideas into results. In the Try
Stuff sections at the end of each chapter, you’ll find specific instructions for how
to use the tools.
To guide you in tackling the greatest design problem of all, we’ve divided the
book into two sections. The first part provides the big ideas and mindsets for
how to—and how not to—think about the quest for meaning-making. This
section is really an invitation to a whole new point of view about meaning.
While it mostly addresses the concepts that lay the foundation for how to live a
meaningful life, it also includes a few initial exercises and an assessment to help
you shift your perspective into this new approach. The second part of the book
provides specific tools and practices you can implement, in four key areas where



meaning is readily found. It’s the doing part of the book—packed with exercises
you can try, test, and tweak. Together, these two sections build a practice you can
understand, master, and actually live in real life.
Chapter 1 takes a look at reframing this idea of meaning and explains what
we’re talking about when we say we want a meaningful life. It identifies key
dysfunctional beliefs—concepts that are popular but untrue or ineffective—to
make sure these bad ideas don’t get you stuck before you even start. We’ll let you
in on why fulfillment is a dead end, and what the scandal of particularity means
and why celebrating it turns out to be critical to living a meaningful life.
Chapter 2 gives you an overview of design thinking and discusses how
meaning can actually be designed and not just stumbled into by the fortunate. It
lays out five design mindsets that will enable you to recognize meaning-making
opportunities and capitalize on them. Lastly, it unpacks how moment-making is
the actual work of a meaning designer and why getting that right is the difference
between joy and frustration.
Chapter 3 opens vast new territory in which to find meaning. We will
introduce you to the Tale of Two Worlds and offer a fundamental reframing of
our lived experience. We will distinguish the transactional world, which we are
constantly rushing to keep up with and where most of us spend nearly all our
time, from the flow world, which is full of potential meaning and surrounds us
constantly, but it’s a world that gets easily overshadowed by all the busyness that
comes with transactions.
The second part of the book begins with chapters exploring the four key
components of meaning-making—wonder, coherency, flow, and community.
While there are lots of other areas of experience that can contribute to meaning,
these are the four that we’ve found to be the most universal, the most accessible,
and make a big difference quickly for just about everyone.
We are big fans of both wonder and awe. People in every culture recognize
this experience of being deeply struck by something mysterious and awe-some as
being life-giving and meaningful. In chapter 4, we’ll show you how to make the
Wonder Formula work for you and practices for finding it.
In chapter 5, we define coherency as getting three elements—what you do,
what you believe, and who you are—in alignment. We will show you how to



design a meaningful life with coherency in mind.
Chapter 6 explores how to find flow—and how to step into the flow world
anytime and in almost any situation.
Chapter 7 encourages you to build formative communities. We suggest that
there are three primary reasons people gather: to have a good time together, to
get something done together, or to become better together. They’re all great, but
that last one is what we mean by a formative community. People universally
report that it’s a game changer, and we’ll give you the recipe for cooking one up.
Chapter 8 looks at how designing meaning changes (and doesn’t) at different
ages and stages of life.
In every chapter, we will give you a design exercise or two to help you build
practices so you can experience more meaning. These practical and simple
exercises are important and necessary. We want you to actually do them, not just
because they are in the book but because designers build their way forward with
a bias toward action.
In other words—we designers try stuff.
We ideate, we generate, we fail, we iterate, and we try again, over and over.
We don’t sit at our desks, or on our couches, or in our cars and think about
how someday it will all matter. Someday there will be the elusive more to it than
this. Or that there is a far-off and mystical land of meaning that exists on the
other side of a new job, a different career, a better relationship, a stunning
achievement, a bigger bank account, or after retirement.
If you design for meaning, you will find it everywhere.
So let’s get to it, because life is both far too long and way too short to spend
our time feeling stuck.
OceanofPDF.com



1

Meaning and Purpose Reframed
In good design, problem-finding always comes before problem-solving. In fact,
the reason most real-world projects fail is because they’re solving the wrong
problem. Remember Google Glass in 2013—a slick piece of tech that tried to fix
a need nobody actually had? The same misfire happens in everyday life: We
struggle to stick to a workout plan and think the solution is to shell out for a
pricey gym membership or expensive gym equipment, only to watch the
treadmill morph into an expensive coatrack. The real problem wasn’t equipment
but boredom, scheduling, or needing a workout buddy.
When people confront a tough challenge, their instinct is to jump in and do
something—anything. Designers pause instead, zoom in on the humans at the
center of the issue, and uncover what those people truly need before lifting a
finger.
Just what problem are we trying to help you solve in this book anyway? Well,
the title would imply that we’re trying to show you how to live a meaningful life,
but it’s even more accurate to say that we are trying to help you design a life with
more meaning and purpose in it. To do that, we have to first clarify what we are
talking about when we talk about meaning and purpose. And not surprisingly,
when we do that, we discover that they are both due for a reframe.
Reframing is a designer’s power tool; it is a special way of looking at a
problem from a different, more creative point of view. The result of a good
reframe is more ideas, more possibilities, and more freedom to act. (We were



doing reframing in the introduction when we talked about the Atlas problem
and finding new ways to deal with despair.)
When we step back and look at the question of meaning-making, the first
decision we need to make is which preposition to use: of or in. Are we solving for
what is the ultimate meaning of life, or are we looking for how to find more
meaning in life? Which of those innocuous two-letter words we choose to focus
on will make a huge difference.
The meaning OF life is the Big Question. Thoughtful, caring humans have
struggled with it since we stumbled out of caves. This pursuit of ultimate
meaning-making is at the heart of virtually every philosophy, religion,
spirituality, or wisdom tradition. Are we really suggesting that we have the
answer or even something important to add to this conversation?
No.
And yes.
No, we are not claiming to have a new insight on the big meaning-OF-life
question—the one that Viktor Frankl was thinking about when he wrote his
classic Man’s Search for Meaning, in which he described meaning-making as the
essence of what makes us human. While we have great regard for the various
traditions of deep thinkers like Frankl—and Freud, Black Elk, Jesus, the
Buddha, the Prophet Muhammad, the Beatles, Taylor Swift, Lao Tzu, Maya
Angelou, and all the other wisdom leaders who posed answers to the big
meaning-OF-life questions—we are not going there. The question of how, at the
end of life, we can look back and say our life meant something—in the big
cosmic sense of meaning—is a question above our pay grade.
The meaning OF life is not our question because it is not, fundamentally, a
design question—it’s a philosophical and existential question. Its answer varies
depending on your belief systems and culture. It suggests there is one right
answer, and as we know about wicked problems—there is no right or wrong
solution.
Meaning OF life? No—we can’t solve that one for you.
Meaning IN life? Yes—we have reframes, ideas, and practical design tools
ready to go.



Here’s the big difference—we can help you design and build experiences,
meaningful experiences, and those experiences occur in moments, and those
moments occur in life. We believe, along with an increasing number of
psychologists, neuroscientists, and consciousness researchers, that experiencing
meaning in life is the key to happiness, and meaning can be found in lots of
ways, in moments small and large, all along the way. And it turns out that people
of all walks, beliefs, and cultures find the same kinds of experiences meaningful,
which is to say that the experience of meaning is a profoundly human and
universal thing—and that’s something we can design for. Moreover, we think
you deserve a life full of meaningful moments that can be readily found in your
day-to-day experience. And finding more of that kind of meaning is not
dependent on correctly answering the bigger question: What is the Ultimate
Meaning of Life? Putting these tools into practice might help you develop an
awareness of and a sensitivity to the bigger questions, if finding those kinds of
answers is important to you.
Now that we’ve established what kind of meaning-making we’re designing
for here (i.e., the more meaning-in-life kind, not the ultimate meaning-of-life
kind), what about purpose? It turns out purpose has a very similar problem to
meaning. The question posed to us most often is: “How do I find my purpose?”
When we lean in and look deeper, we find the real question on people’s hearts is:
“How do I find my one, true purpose—the one I was born for and the
attainment of which will make my life meaningful?”
Now that is a loaded question.
Purpose and Passion
Finding that one true purpose is just as hard as defining the meaning of life. The
idea here is that finding your one true purpose will set the direction of your life
and achieving the desired outcome of that purpose will make your life
meaningful. According to this perspective, knowing your one true purpose is
absolutely necessary to know what you need to do to live a life of meaning, so
knowing your purpose is really important, right?
Wrong.



This longing to find the ultimate purpose that will achieve ultimate meaning
is a bad idea because it simply doesn’t work for hardly anyone. Most people
don’t ever find it, and those who do usually don’t do so until later in life. That
approach is leaving too many people out. We need to pick a better purpose
problem to solve for.
Ultimate purpose, like ultimate meaning, is a lovely aspiration—something
to grow into and live toward—but we do ourselves a huge disservice if we decide
it must be a prerequisite to the well-designed life. Or a life lived meaningfully. So
how do we reframe purpose into something we can design for?
It’s simple. While we can’t help you find your one true purpose, we can help
you design how to live purposefully. We are all for living purposeful lives—lives
that feel authentic, generative, growing, well directed, and contributory. We
want you to live with a deep sense of purpose every single day, and we’re
confident you can do so despite not having arrived at your definition of your
life’s one true purpose. You can know that what you’re doing matters to you and
is an authentic expression of who you are. As human-centered designers, we
believe that humans are always capable of growing, so we are all becoming more
human. When we take responsibility for what we’re becoming and invite others
to join us, we endow daily life with the purposefulness of the ongoing journey of
growing into our ever-better selves. Living purposefully frees us to evolve our
purpose as we and the world change over time, and it enables us to fully enjoy
the meaning of many, if not most, of our experiences along life’s way. Essentially,
we’re saying that your purpose in life—for now—is to figure out how to live as
purposefully as you can. In fact, we suggest that if you stop worrying about the
ultimate purpose and meaning of your life and start designing ways to live more
purposefully, your chance of discovering that One True Purpose is materially
enhanced.
Now let’s tackle ultimate purpose’s dysfunctional sibling—passion. The
passion question we get all the time sounds like this: “Everyone tells me to
‘follow my passion,’ but I don’t know exactly what that means. How do I find
my passion?”
The kind of passion we hear people longing for is a compelling, authentic
drive that can direct our steps and powerfully animate our lives. It’s a cause to be



passionate about, something worth living for, a noble mission that endows us
with a meaningful raison d’être that gets us up every morning and validates our
self-respect for the person looking back at us in the mirror every night. The kind
that is sure to give us a magical, dependable, nearly limitless source of energy
from within.
We have heard literally thousands of individuals tell us about their quest for
passion but have met precious few who can sincerely claim to have found it. The
problem with passion is as simple as a pronoun.
“I want to find my passion.”
That my is killing us.
Those two little letters take us off the road to meaning and joy and down a
dead-end highway to frustration. Looking for my passion implies that there is
one—and just one—solution, and finding that one, true, right, authentic,
wonderful, dependable thing to be passionate about is the prerequisite to living
a good (i.e., meaningful) life. Society has bought into the idea that there is this
one thing out there, and if it’s the real thing, it’ll be something we can’t not do
and can’t imagine not pursuing.
Few of us state this conviction so explicitly, but it is how so many people
we’ve talked to, and even more that we’ve taught, think. We may not have
confessed to ourselves that we’ll accept nothing short of a singular “can’t not”
life conviction, but it’s evident when we repeatedly denigrate or dismiss
opportunities that align with what we care about because they just aren’t good
enough. Or because we’re not bursting with passionate enthusiasm. Or we
haven’t found that magical energy source.
The way to get out of the passion dead end is to flip from my to how. It’s not
about finding my passion, it’s about finding how to live passionately. Once we’re
free of the soul-crushing presumption that we’re all on a search for our one
singular passion, we are ready to design a life full of passionate energies. And
living passionately is not only possible; it’s a whole lot of fun.
Now, if you happen to be one of those rare people who has found their one
true passion and are happily on your way to total fulfillment, we think that’s
great. Really. We wish you well and hope you’re duly grateful to be one of the



few to have discovered such clarity and confidence. But most of us aren’t there,
so we need to think differently about the passion and purpose questions.
Once you get the hang of design thinking, we expect you’ll discover that there
are oodles of untapped purpose and passion lurking in your everyday experience,
moment to moment. The key to releasing that untapped wonderfulness is to
simply ask, “How can I bring more passion to what I’m doing now?” or “How is
a noble purpose being pursued by this activity?” Recognizing that there is some
degree of purpose or passion available in nearly everything you do shifts the
entire game. It’s all about getting more out of many daily experiences, not just
waiting for that one special unicorn experience.
And it’s worth reiterating that you don’t need to prepack any purpose or
passion before you begin your travel on the road to a more meaningful life.
Three Reframes
In our work, we find that most people care deeply about having a life full of
meaning, fulfillment, and impact. Almost everyone we meet wants to make an
impact, and they are all on a quest for fulfillment, which is usually defined as
having a life in which one feels that they’ve been able to fully express themselves
in thought and action in the world. And to get it, everyone seems to be chasing
more—more experiences, more purpose, more love, more impact, more money,
more fulfilment, and so on. However, for most of us, the definitions of impact
and fulfillment are quite fuzzy. And the research says that chasing “more”
doesn’t seem to result in much long-term happiness. That said, everyone is clear
they’re not getting enough meaning or having enough impact, and they are not
fulfilled yet, but they are not sure what to do about it.
These folks, like Allison and Sonya from the introduction, are stuck. Most of
the time, what gets people stuck are what we call dysfunctional beliefs,
perceptions about their problem or its solution that are either untrue or
unhelpful. As we said earlier, a good designer knows that when you’re stuck, the
situation calls for a reframe. To be sure we’re working on the right problem, one
for which we can actually design a successful solution, we’re going to give you
three reframes. These are three different ideas about what meaning and purpose



are and where to find them—one about having an impact (and why it’s not what
you think it is), one about fulfillment (which turns out to be unattainable), and
one about particularity (which just might be the key that unlocks the whole
thing).
Let’s get ready to reframe!
Brace for Impact
Just about everybody wants to “have an impact.” They want to “make a
difference” or even “change the world.” They hope that if they can find a way to
make an Impact (with a capital I), their life will be meaningful. Sadly, an awful
lot of people are finding that impact is just out of reach—often due to no fault
of their own.
Dave graduated from Stanford in 1976 with a Master of Science in
Thermoscience—he earned the designation of an advanced energy technologist.
Dave had a passion to solve the energy crisis and had his newly minted master’s
degree to prove it (which had been paid for by the research division of Chevron
Oil Corporation, no less). Only one minor problem. In 1976, there was no
advanced energy technology industry to work for. What no one told Dave as he
was preparing to go forth and save the world was that the world wasn’t ready for
saving—and wouldn’t be for over three decades.
Dave had the right idea, but its time hadn’t come yet, so like millions of other
well-intended but unsuccessful people with bad timing, Dave was having zero
impact. He was heartbroken and thought life had no meaning because he
couldn’t make an impact. He felt like he had failed.
Eventually, Dave pivoted to a career in tech and had a big, if completely
different, kind of impact. But even as he acknowledged having an impact (he was
the world’s first mouse product manager at Apple), it still didn’t feel all that
meaningful.
Dave was making the same mistake many of us make. He was putting all his
meaning eggs into the impact basket, which is often a recipe for disappointment.
Here’s a cycle you need to watch out for. When you succeed in accomplishing
something you set out to do, and actually make an impact on something, it feels



great. But no result or impact is big enough to supply a lifetime’s worth of
meaning. Even incredibly impactful people—Nobel Prize winners, world
leaders, corporate titans, Olympic athletes, best-selling authors—often fall into
depression after the hoopla surrounding their success, their impact, has
subsided. We call it the “What have you done for me lately?” syndrome.
In fact, Dave has worked with some Olympic athletes in a program set up by
the Olympic committee for athletes who had “aged out” of their sports. The
data on the mental health of these post-Olympian athletes isn’t good—there is a
lot of depression and anxiety in this population, and no wonder. Imagine you’ve
spent your life since age six training and competing as a speed skater, and
perhaps you’ve even won Olympic medals in your time, only to find yourself at
twenty-five or twenty-seven too old to compete against the young kids coming
up in the sport. You have another sixty or seventy years ahead of you, and the
likelihood of achieving something as big as a gold medal in another career is low.
The question is: How do you handle this? How is an Olympic gold medalist
supposed to cope with having their maximum impact in the world peak as a
teenager?
This is why there is something inherently problematic about using impact as
the essential source of meaning. Impact is the result of a series of actions in the
world. When circumstances prevent you from achieving impact, you feel bereft
and useless, even though you know that some things are out of your control.
Impact is an outcome. It is produced. It is a thing. And a thing cannot have
ultimate meaning; it is limited to a specific time, place, and consequence. You,
on the other hand, are a wonderful, emotional, continually growing,
multidimensional person. Impact, which is only a thing, cannot satisfy the
deepest yearnings of your wonderful, multidimensional self.
Now, don’t get us wrong—making an impact is a good thing. We need lots of
positive impacts, and achieving them can be meaningful, but impact doesn’t
bring with it a lasting experience of meaning. As we’ll unpack further in chapter
3, impact and outcome are all part of the transactional world, the world of
getting stuff done and achieving results—and the world where nothing is ever
“enough.” Go ahead and enjoy the temporary meaning of your impact and
achievement, just don’t expect it to last (and as we’ll learn when we get to the



idea of particularity, nothing lasts, and that’s entirely okay—wonderful, even—
because it means more’s on the way).
The moment when you realize something you have designed or built had a
positive impact, you’re doing a good thing; you’re on the right track. Just be
aware that the impact you created is temporary and the experience of impact is
fleeting. We’re designing something different—a way to live where we don’t rely
on impact to create meaning for ourselves. We can enjoy what impact has to
offer, but we’re not expecting anything from it that it can’t deliver. And we can
learn how to become attentive to other kinds of meaning that are available along
the way as we’re working on making that impact.
Reframing impact as temporary is good. Reframing impact as a pointer to
more meaningful experiences is even better—like enjoying the community with
your collaborators or savoring the magic of the moment when a colleague
suddenly has the big idea. Breaking free from the never-ending quest to “make a
bigger impact” allows us to pursue other forms of meaning in life—and this is
the best approach to finding what we’re looking for.
Now let’s look at another particularly pernicious and dysfunctional belief
about meaning that is getting in our way—the quest for fulfillment.
Have You Seen My Fulfillment?
Fulfillment is a different animal. People mean different things by it, but most
often what we hear people aspiring to is a life that lets them fully express their
entire and true selves. The fulfilled life is the fully expressed life. We don’t mean
just a full life. Most of the people we encounter have completely “full” lives.
Their calendars are jammed, and they’ve not a spare minute to add something
new. Life is totally full—but it’s not fulfilling, and that’s what they really want.
It’s something we all want. And it’s something our friend Allan sure as heck
wanted, but no matter how hard he tried, he just wasn’t finding it.
Allan was about ten years into his career. He’d gotten a sociology degree from
a lovely small liberal arts college in the Southeast. Fortunately, he was pretty
savvy with spreadsheets and had a real knack for organizing things. With those
strengths, he’d managed to establish himself as a project expediter—handling



scheduling and resource allocation for a financial software company. He was
quite enjoying the opportunity to feel a part of something bigger than himself
coordinating the efforts of a large engineering team working on multimilliondollar projects. But he was disappointed that his role didn’t have much need of
his artistic creativity, his self-expression. Because the job “wasn’t big enough,” it
drained the joy from his experience. He imagined that there was some other job,
somewhere else, that would be a more perfect fit for him, that would be more
fulfilling (engaging all his talents and abilities), though he had no idea what that
might be, or even where to look.
At his core, Allan worried he was settling.
We all feel that way sometimes—that we are just settling for something less
than our ultimate best, something less than the life we deserve. Allan’s not alone.
Frustration at not achieving fulfillment voices itself in a variety of ways:
I just can’t bring my whole self to this job! It’s so unfulfilling.
My creativity needs a bigger outlet for expression—I can’t achieve what I’m
capable of!
There’s really no place I can be entirely myself. How will I fulfill my destiny?
It feels like there are big chunks of me that I never get to show to the world.
Realistically, people shouldn’t expect one job, one relationship, or one life
circumstance to deliver that fully baked, all-encompassing self-fulfillment.
Nevertheless, everyone seems to be seeking a life that includes all the creativity,
all the sex, all the love, all the accolades, all the status, and definitely all the
money that they want—a life in which they’ve got a high confidence of
fulfillment success. Too many people, even when things are pretty good, let their
joy be diminished by the worry that it’s not enough, it is not complete, and that
they’ll never reach the elusive state of fulfillment.
A person whose life is fully expressed and whose entire and true self is realized
in the world has been described as a self-actualized person. Back in 1943,
Abraham Maslow first defined self-actualization as the top level of five human
needs in his seminal paper “A Theory of Human Motivation.” The notion of
self-actualization as the highest form of human expression caught on quickly
and has persisted ever since.



Maslow posed the idea that humans are focused on evolving sets of needs.
People march up the ladder of this hierarchy, and at the apex of human existence
is self-actualization, which Maslow describes as follows:
[Self-actualization] refers to the desire to become more and more what one
is, to become everything that one is capable of becoming.
Maslow may have first articulated that idea in 1943, but it’s still as popular
and relevant today as ever.

Description 1

The National Institutes of Health describe Maslow’s hierarchy of needs as
“one of the most contagious ideas in the behavioral sciences.” With all due
respect for Maslow, we see it differently. Simply put, we think this definition of
self-actualization is wrong, because it’s unattainable and unhuman.
The problem here is that this lofty aspiration of a fulfilled life, where all of
you gets expressed and positively valued in the world, depends on three things
that we think are false, based on some fundamentally dysfunctional beliefs:
1. that there is only one of you “in there”; and
2. that one you can be “optimized” around self-actualization and thereby be
fulfilled; and
3. your value is tied up in how others view your contribution, a valuation over
which you have limited control.
This is a far-too-narrow view of who you truly are—and letting others define
your worth is always a recipe for failure.

[Embedded visual 1 on PDF page 28 (799x749 px). OCR-readable text: /\ / Self- / Actualization / Maslow’s Hierarchy of Needs]



So, let’s blow up these dysfunctional beliefs once and for all!
Walt Whitman said, in “Song of Myself,” section 51, “I am large. I contain
multitudes.” We agree—we have always said that every person contains far more
aliveness than can be expressed in one lifetime. We often run a “multiverse”
experiment in our classes and workshops where we ask people, “How many lives
would you want, if you could live in many universes at the same time (the
multiverse)? You can be the astronaut you wanted to be when you were ten, and
the chef, and the rodeo clown, and the billionaire…. You can have all these lives
at the same time and, better yet, be aware of and experience every life fully. How
many lives do you want?”
The answer, averaged from thousands of people, is… seven.
That’s how many concurrent lives people want if they could have it all. What
this means is that there are more than one of you in there. What that means is
you can’t ever express and experience every part of your personality, interests,
capabilities, passions, longings, and opportunities. It’s impossible—you are too
vast! Every person on earth contains far more life than one lifetime will permit to
be expressed—so no one can “become everything that one is capable of
becoming.” Isn’t that great?! Whitman was exactly right—we are all large. We are
all multitudes.
Which means, of course, the self-actualized “one person” model is a fallacy.
To be actualized requires that there be only one version of you, and that makes
no sense. We are all an intersection of multiple people. Bill is an engineer, a
designer, a teacher, a writer, a husband, a father, a grandfather, an artist, and far
too many other things to list. Dave is many people as well. You are also an
intersection of many, many identities, and there is no “one identity” that can be
optimized over all the others. And trying to optimize all your identities is a waste
of time and an exercise in frustration.
Finally, letting others determine the value of your life is a very bad idea, one
that, with the pervasive penetration of social media, the rise of influencers, and
the overwhelm of internet bubbles and bunkers, has been weaponized to create
the loneliest and most anxious society ever. So much so that the surgeon general
has recently called for warning labels, like the ones we put on cigarettes thirty
years ago, to warn users of the dangers of this kind of perverse and false value



setting and attention stealing and crowd bullying. You are the final judge of the
value of your actions and intentions—no one else.
The reframe is that you can’t be fulfilled (i.e., have every aspect of your
personhood fully expressed and appreciated, because you are too big to fit into a
single lifetime). However, you can be fully alive, which means completely
present for and fully experiencing the life you are in right now. Once you
understand this, your mindset shifts, and it turns out that self-actualization is no
longer your goal—it is not the apex of human experience. Even Maslow realized
this, and toward the end of his life, he became disenchanted with selfactualization. In his diaries that were unpublished at the time of his death, he
made a correction and added a new final level to his needs pyramid—a new
definition of a well-lived life, which he called self-transcendence. Selftranscendence goes beyond the ego needs of actualization and accomplishment
—you might recognize this insight if you practice a religious or spiritual
tradition, where it is most often called compassion.

Description 2

This final insight from Maslow frees us from the impossible task of attaining
a fully realized, fully optimized self and sends us on a very different journey.
With self-transcendence, the goal switches from optimizing the self to moving
toward something bigger than yourself—which leads us right back to wonder
and mystery.
We can learn to transcend ourselves by joining in the larger flow of reality
surrounding us at all times and so become fully alive. And that’s something we
can design our way into.

[Embedded visual 1 on PDF page 30 (924x770 px). OCR-readable text: Self - / Transcendence / Self - / Actualization / Self-Esteem / Love & Belonging / Safety & Security / Physiological Needs / Maslow’s Hierarchy of Needs - Revised]



So, where will we find this fully alive experience? To answer that question, we
need one more big idea to reframe meaning.
The Scandal of Particularity
Every design journey begins with reality. A sign above the Loft—Stanford’s
graduate design studio—declares, “You Are Here,” reminding students to
anchor themselves in this exact place, this moment, these constraints. Designers
don’t start with how they wish the world were; they start with the world as it is
and embrace their position at the very start of problem-finding.
There is literally no other place we can start from.
Yet many of us think that there is something missing in our lives, and that’s
why we’re less happy and less fulfilled than we think we deserve to be. Something
from our past isn’t right, or the thing we desperately want is in a future that
hasn’t happened yet. So off we go to find that one or two or ten additional
achievements, relationships, or possessions that will fill our meaning and impact
cup to the brim. The result is often a hugely overscheduled, overstuffed life, one
where the happiness we seek is still just out of reach, somewhere in an
unresolved past or a hoped-for future. Because we are not “starting in reality,” we
are no happier than before—but we might be more exhausted and more
disappointed than ever.
We think we’ve all been sold a bill of goods.
“More” is not the path to a meaningful life. You do not have to find a way to
do more, optimize more, or accomplish more to find greater meaning in your
life. You do not have to be more empathetic, meditate more, hack more of your
bad habits, or manifest your thoughts more. All you have to do is learn how to
celebrate the here and now and embrace the scandal of particularity.
The first step in doing that is to understand what we mean by particularity
and accept that it’s scandalous. Our definition of the particular is a real
something that appears in a specific time, place, and shape—something we can
observe, engage with, or build upon.
The concept of particularity shows up in many philosophies and religions—
again, it’s not a new idea. However, when you think with the mindsets of a



designer, it is a big idea and one that is core to our approach to meaning-making.
It starts with the observation that we are finite creatures living in reality, and we
can’t actually “do” or “make” the ultimate of anything.
The only things we can do are specific actions in the real world. Remember,
once you eliminate what you wish would happen or what you think should
happen, then it becomes clear that the particular is where the ultimate and the
universal have the chance to appear, in a moment of time and space, and become
concrete.
We can’t do utter beauty, but we can make a beautiful birthday cake.
We can’t know the ultimate truth, but we can design a very good cushion for
meditation.
We can’t do perfect unity, but we can facilitate the family all endorsing
whether it’s mountains or beach for the upcoming long weekend.
We can’t embody complete honesty, but we can find a way to give a colleague
some difficult news in a respectful way that helps them hear it.
The point is that we can’t do the ultimate of anything. The ultimate is always
beyond our reach. It’s a myth, because we are fundamentally finite, imperfect
humans living in an imperfect world. Accepting our finite self is another
example of designers “starting in reality.” What we can do are these little
particular things, in real time, in real space, with real people, with lots of real
constraints and limitations. But the wonderful thing is that those particular
things can become the gateway to experience the ultimate, of which they are a
small, but real and authentic, expression. That cake was indeed beautiful
(especially to the four-year-old who received it) and in so being, that particular
cake can be a portal into the ultimate reality of beauty (and relationships, and
love, and family).
Here’s a fundamental design constraint—we think that the particular is as
good as it gets. There is no reality that we encounter (in this life anyway) that
isn’t utterly particular. Once we accept that, then we are free to recognize that
it’s totally wonderful and utterly terrific—if not downright magical—that these
little cakes and weekend planning conversations actually have the power to let us
enter into experiences of huge, universal, ultimate realities—like beauty, truth,
love, unity, integrity, creativity, and joy.



That’s where the scandal comes in. It’s actually downright scandalous that
the most wonderful and mysterious things we can imagine can only come to us
through these profoundly small and even pedestrian particularities. It’s kind of
crazy, even a bit offensive, that this is how reality works. Our egos want to think
that experiencing the ultimate takes lots of hard work and can only be
accomplished with a strict regimen of meditation, or extreme yoga, or
“something” that is esoteric and difficult. Is it really possible that something as
big and profound as beauty actually fits into a four-year-old’s birthday cake?
What about a single cupcake?
Yes and yes.
And no—not all particulars are created equal. If you’d like to argue that a
Beethoven symphony holds more beauty than a child’s cupcake, we won’t argue
with you. But… that symphony, too, is utterly particular. It’s only so many notes
on so many different instruments played in just so many minutes by so many
musicians. That’s it. It might be a kind of particular that more readily releases
the experience of transcendent beauty, for you, than do other particulars—but it
is no less a particular for that. For someone else’s taste, it’s Metallica, or Yo-Yo
Ma playing Bach’s solo cello suites. No matter your particular way in, the entire
universe is a huge pile of the particular.
And yes—gaining mastery in your capacity to attend to, apprehend, and fully
experience those particularities makes a difference. Dave’s been going to a remote
monastery on silent retreat once or twice a year for thirty years. Some of the
monks he’s met there have been deeply meditating daily for fifty years. They’re
really good at it and far better than Dave will ever be. Their investment in
mastering meditation matters to them, but that particularity of their lives in no
way diminishes the meaningfulness of Dave’s (probably shallower but
nonetheless authentic) experience when he sits with them. Mastery is good, but
it’s not a prerequisite to a meaningful life; it’s just a path to a richer future. All
any of us can ever do is enter the particular moment presented to us in the life we
are actually living right now.
Stop worrying if the best is yet to come and start celebrating the good that’s
right in front of you. That’s where the best is found.



Once we accept all this, then the smart move is to embrace it. As they say in
the adventure experience training company Outward Bound, “If you can’t get
out of it—get into it!” So, let’s celebrate the scandal of particularity—and come
up with ways to more fully experience the wonderful universals and ultimates
that are lying within the dozens of particulars that populate each and every day.
Another word for the particular is moment, and we will talk even more about
moments and moment-making in the following chapters. But rest assured, as a
designer of moments, you have all the raw material, right here and right now, to
experience all the meaning you need.
Practicing this way of life allows us to access the deeply meaningful moments
that are hidden in the particular and concrete events of our lives. Moments are
inherently available, but sometimes we don’t see them, because we are looking in
the wrong place, and we need to clear away distractions to get to the good stuff.
Assembling a life with lots of those meaningful experiences and moments is a
primary way of becoming the fully alive creature you deserve to be.
Getting More Out Of… Not Cramming More Into
Once you’ve learned that impact will not, by itself, make your life meaningful,
and self-actualization is not your highest goal, then you’ve set yourself up for
success in the meaning-making business. When you are ready to embrace the
scandal of particularity, then you are ready to experience a world full of
particular moments of meaning. And that means that this search for a
meaningful life just got a whole lot easier.
It’s not about cramming more momentous experiences into your life. It’s
about getting more out of all the particular moments you already have.
Hopefully that is a big relief! You don’t have to do more, or need more, or
buy more stuff (except maybe this book, though libraries are great!). Our
meaning-in-life framework comes with the scandalous observation that
particular moments, when experienced with a designer’s mindset, can point to
the experience of the ultimate—and it’s all under your control. No one can take
your particular away from you, because your moments are unique in a universe



of billions of possible moments. And it doesn’t take years of practice—in fact, it
is available right now, in the moment you are reading this.
Right now.
It’s all about your mindset and how you reframe what’s going on in the
world, your reality, and the exquisite particular moment you are experiencing.
We can’t emphasize this too much—this is all doable, by real people, in the
real world, just like you.
We’ve reframed the idea of meaning-making, which is what we’re after here.
In the next chapter, we’re going to address how we go forward by sharing what
the designer’s mindsets are and how they can accelerate us getting what we’re
after. But first, let’s do an assessment exercise to get a better handle on where you
are and what these reframing ideas might mean to you.
TRY STUFF
This chapter introduced you to three big ideas to help you reframe your pursuit of
meaning-making:
Meaning is more than impact, much more.
You can’t be fulfilled, but you can be fully alive.
Your life is made up of meaningful, particular moments, and that’s downright
scandalous.
If you understand these three ideas, you will have accomplished a major reframe,
resulting in a new point of view, or POV. POV is an important concept in design (and
architecture, and photography, and art, and… and…). It means where you stand when
viewing something. Where you stand completely changes what you see, and what you
see determines what happens next. So, it makes sense to assess your POV on
meaning so you know where you stand, and if and how you may wish to change that
stance. This exercise is a simple reflection on your POV on these three big meaningmaking ideas.
Your POV Assessment
Simply complete the following assessment, then reflect on what you’re discovering.
Let these insights guide and support you as you continue reading this book and trying
out these ideas and tools.
Impact Assessment



You play many roles in your life, at work, at home, with family and in other
relationships. Put these roles in order of importance to you and write a short reflection
on where you think you make the most impact on others.
When you succeed in making some sort of impact—large or small—how does it
make you feel? How long does that feeling last?
Does impact seem to make you a better person or grow you into a more authentic
version of you? If so, how, and if not, why?
This chapter claims that if impact is one’s only or primary form of meaningmaking, that is a recipe for getting stuck on the treadmill of “never enough.” Does
that ring true to you? Is it encouraging or discouraging, and why?
Fully Alive vs. Fulfilled Assessment
How attractive or unattractive to you is Maslow’s definition of “fulfillment” as
“becoming all that one can be”?
If you had to pick between being “fulfilled” (i.e., having all your capacity expressed
in this life at some point, even if only briefly) versus being “fully alive” (i.e., being
vibrantly present and engaged in each and every moment of your life), which would
you pick and why?
How does the idea of “self-transcendency” free up or expand the opportunity for
you to have a meaningful life?
This chapter proposes that “fulfillment” is a myth because all of us contain far
more aliveness than one lifetime can hold, so we can’t become all that we are, but
we can be fully alive moment to moment. Do you agree or disagree, and do you
find the idea encouraging or discouraging? Why?
Particularity Assessment
How often does it seem to you that some aspect of your life “just isn’t enough”?
What do you most want, and will there ever be “enough” of it?
How good are you at noticing and appreciating “the little things” in life (i.e., the
moments of life)?
This chapter claims that we can’t ever fully experience any ultimate or universal
(like love, beauty, etc.) and only can experience fleeting aspects of those things in
moments (in the particular). Is that proposition encouraging or discouraging to
you (or both), and why?



If you fully accepted that the best of life is a series of particular moments, what
would that mean?
POV Reflection
Look over your responses. What do you notice? What jumps out at you as
important?
What questions does this raise for you? (This may take some time to answer.)
How is the whole idea of meaning-making changing or getting clearer for you, and
how does that feel?
What, if any, changes in your POV about meaning are you ready to make at this
point?

OceanofPDF.com



2

Think Like a Designer
We’ve been thinking about how designers think for quite some time. The
human-centered design (HCD) movement we are a part of started at Stanford in
1963. While it’s still correctly called HCD, this approach is now more
commonly known as design thinking—highlighting that the methodology isn’t
just about what to do but how to think about what you do. Humanity has
developed lots of different ways to think, each useful in its own method to
improve life and move civilization forward. Universities and laboratories use
research thinking to analyze their way forward to develop new knowledge.
Engineering thinking solves its way forward, using lots of data and formulas that
are tried and true, to come up with reliable answers to hard technical problems.
Business thinking optimizes its way forward to improve organizational efficiency
and effectiveness to better service customers and improve profits. Different
approaches to thinking work for different challenges.
When working on problems for which there is no prior experience and no
available data and the solutions all lie hidden in a future that hasn’t arrived yet,
none of those thinking approaches work very well. You can’t solve or analyze
something you’ve never done before. That’s where design thinking comes in.
Designers build their way forward by creatively looking at the problem,
generating lots of ideas to respond to it, then trying those ideas out quickly with
a prototype. Then they iterate that process until they reach a good solution.
Prototype iteration is the secret sauce of design thinking. And of course, we keep
iterating and prototyping design thinking itself.



Back in 1963, the group we taught in was called the Stanford Product Design
Program, and it focused on making products of all kinds but overwhelmingly
physical products—anything from spatulas to MRI machines. By the ’70s,
personal computers were popping up everywhere, and we were designing lots
and lots of software as well as physical things, but the focus was still on making
products. When user-friendly computing came on the scene in the early ’80s
(which Dave got to work on as Apple’s first mouse product manager and Bill got
to work on as project leader for Apple’s first portable computers), we started
designing not only computers and software but designing the experience of
using them. U/X (user experience) design was born as a whole new design
discipline. Design was evolving, so we changed the name to the Stanford Design
Program—dropping Product from the title, since we were by then making lots of
things beyond products. We were designing company cultures and education
systems and all manner of non-product things and experiences.
When we launched the course Designing Your Life in 2010 and the book in
2016, we expanded design thinking into yet a new arena—life design. And it
worked! We had to make some modifications and additions, because designing a
life is a very different challenge from designing a computer mouse or a spatula,
but those changes worked. Once again, the simple but powerful innovation
principles of design thinking succeeded in helping millions of people reframe a
challenging problem (What do I do with the rest of my life?!) and come up with
creative, satisfying, doable solutions.
We’re inventing yet another new category of design—meaning design. And
you are all becoming meaning designers.
When we first shared our ideas about starting up the Life Design Lab at
Stanford and teaching “life design” classes, some of our most respected colleague
designers and professors thought we were nuts (some still do). And just like life
design a dozen years ago, meaning design is a different animal—it’s a new thing.
It requires a different orientation to what it is we’re making, how to go about
making it, and the mindsets needed to do it well.
So, just what do meaning designers “make” anyway?
Good question. We’re glad you asked.
The core task of meaning design is moment-making.



Our approach to helping you find more meaning in your life centers on
moment-making, because moments are the essential element of the scandal of
particularity. When you stop stressing about getting it all figured out or finally
doing/having/making enough and fully embrace your human finitude—then
you are free to deeply engage in and enjoy lots of particular experiences, each of
which resides in a particular moment in time. Some of those moments are
fleeting—like that green flash on the horizon at sunset. And some last for a bit
longer—like that entire afternoon spent surfing when conditions were perfect
and for no explicable reason you were the only one out that day. But long or
short, the experience of the particular is momentary. Moments are all we’ve got,
and thankfully they are more than enough, far more, if we design them well.
Making It Real
The machine shop used by our design students is called the PRL—the Product
Realization Lab. We call it the PRL—not just the shop—to remind our students
what a designer does. When a student walks into that room and sees the lathes,
band saws, and mills, we don’t want them thinking that what’s going on in there
is just turning, cutting, and grinding. We want them to see the PRL as the place
where formless chunks of wood and steel get transformed into useful, beautiful,
and practical things—it’s where products get real-ized.
Real-ization is what design is all about.
The first product Dave made in the PRL as an undergraduate engineering
student was a fire hydrant wrench. (Dave put himself through Stanford as a
firefighter.) He walked in with a big chunk of brass in his backpack and hoped to
eventually walk out with a custom-designed firefighter’s tool. He made lots of
mistakes. He bent the brass. He broke the cutters. He didn’t know how to use
the tools properly, and he didn’t understand how to work with the materials.
Eventually, he learned, and the wrench finally started to emerge from the brass
billet. What Dave was doing in the PRL was learning how to make things, realize things, by carefully cooperating with the raw materials through the artful use
of the right tool.



This quote, attributed to Michelangelo, beautifully and famously describes
this idea of real-ization: “I saw the angel in the marble and carved until I set him
free.”
That’s what we’re inviting you to do in becoming a meaning designer. This
book’s job is to give you the tools and techniques to craft and real-ize
meaningful moments out of the rich raw material of the life you’re already living
every day. Our hope is to empower you to walk into the world where zillions of
moments are laid out in front of you and show you how to make them real by
artfully extracting the meaning lurking just below the surface.
A designer can make a meaningful moment two ways: by craft and by
discovery. A crafted moment is one that you’ve conceived in advance and
orchestrated to happen just as you intend in order to provide a meaningful
experience. When you rise twenty minutes earlier than usual, at an hour when
you know the house will be quiet, to sit in your favorite chair wrapped in your
favorite throw blanket for your morning meditation sit—you’ve designed a
crafted moment. When you collect affirmation cards from all the other team
members and surprise Louise on her tenth anniversary working at your store
with a carefully wrapped box full of those cards, you’ve crafted a moment.
Crafted moments are great. They have the advantage of often working just as
you want them to. However, they take effort and planning, which costs time and
energy, which are sometimes hard to come by.
A discovered moment is one that life is bringing you already, which you
transform into a meaningful moment simply by how you engage with it. When
you “make” a discovered moment, what you’re making is your experience of it—
you aren’t making the moment itself. No advance planning is needed—only
your attentiveness and readiness to seize the opportunity are required (which we
will define as the design mindset of availability).
When you notice that the car next to yours at the red light is playing the same
song on the radio as you are, and you roll down your window and flash the other
driver a thumbs-up, which she notices, then you both smile at each other as the
light turns green, and you pull away bathed in the heartwarming realization that
“Nice, we are all in this together,” you’ve done a very good piece of momentmaking.



The Never-Ending Prototype
In meaning design, as in all design, prototyping is critical, but for moments, we
do it a little differently. In every other design project, we iterate the prototyping
until we finally happen upon a solution that is worth keeping, then we stop
prototyping and implement that final design.
When we ask our Stanford teaching team to come up with a new course
module, we often try five different prototypes of an exercise before we eventually
land on a good one, are done, and move on. Most design projects are like this:
After a lot of iterations, they reach a place called “done.” But moment-making is
different—it’s never done, because the moments just keep coming.
There is no final product.
Even if you prototype a moment and live into that moment perfectly—it’s
gone in no time. In a moment. You can’t keep moments, but you can keep a
personal practice of making them.
The practice of being a moment designer is one approach to what people
often call living in the moment. It is a particular practice that allows you to make
meaning almost anywhere and at any time. Think of it as a sort of never-ending
prototype. The good news is that being a moment designer is a great way to
always be learning and improving your experience. Moment-making is a lifelong
design project.
You are never done.
The point of the meaningful life is to keep living it—so why would you want
to be “done” with that? Practices like moment design (and yoga and meditation)
are never done, because the point of the practice is the doing and refining of it.
You’ll notice that most of the exercises we offer in this book for how to make
meaningful moments, things like “putting on your wonder glasses” and
“coherency sightings,” aren’t onetime activities. They are things you can do
regularly and repeatedly, and in fact work best when they become part of a
regular meaning design practice. And that’s fabulous, because it means we will
never run out of meaning-making moments. We will never long for more,
because we will always have enough.



Once we’ve expanded our understanding of design thinking to focus on
designing moment-making and have recognized that practices are really neverending prototypes, it’s time to address the most important aspect of thinking
like a designer—your mindset.
The Designer’s Way—Five Mindsets for Meaning-Making
There are certain ways of thinking that help designers to see the world a little bit
differently from how ordinary folks do. This is the way artists and designers see
the world, and this out-of-the-box thinking tends to generate more creative
outcomes than conventional in-the-box thinking. But you don’t have to be an
artist to think this way; anyone can do it. We outlined five design mindsets in our
first book, and they are still relevant. But since we’re designing for a different
outcome in this book—not just designing one individual life but experiencing
the joy and meaning held within the moments of our lives in community with
everyone—the mindsets that get you thinking differently are a little different.
The good news is that you don’t need any previous experience with these
mindsets or to have read any of our earlier books to use these mindsets to begin
your journey.
Now, some of this might seem obvious to you because you already think this
way. A lot of this stuff is intuitive. But in our teaching and in our writing, we try
to make the intuitive explicit, because “if you can name it, you can claim it,” and
that makes it easier for you to remember it. With practice, these mindsets
become habits. They become your way of thinking and seeing the world with all
its wondrous possibilities and its delicious constraints, all the time. Collectively,
we think of them as the Designer’s Way. As you find the rhythm or pace for
adopting them into your own life, they become your Way of doing things, your
Way of being.



Wonder
In the final act of the Thornton Wilder play Our Town, the souls of townspeople
who have died appear onstage chatting with one another, and the play’s
narrator, played as the Stage Manager, about the lives of the living. Emily was a
young mother when she died, and looking back at the living, she was struck by
how beautiful and wonderful everything in life really was, but she hadn’t
noticed. After tearfully remembering ticking clocks, her mama’s sunflowers,
food and coffee, new ironed dresses, hot baths, and waking up, she exclaims:
EMILY: Oh earth, you’re too wonderful for anybody to realize you!
(turning to the Stage Manager, she continues…) Do any human beings ever
realize life while they live it?—every, every minute?
STAGE MANAGER: No. The saints and poets, maybe—they do some.
Looking back on her life from eternity, Emily could see how plentiful wonder
was. The happy news is that she’s right—earth is wonder-full! And, more happy

[Embedded visual 1 on PDF page 44 (342x413 px). Simple blue line drawing of a flower in a pot, accompanying the discussion of wonder.]



news, the Stage Manager is wrong. Catching more wonder needn’t be just the
province of saints and poets—those special kinds of people with an unusual
capacity for noticing the myriad wondrous mysteries of this world. Even in a
world full of lots to do, and an internet full of distractions, wonder is all around
us and available all the time. Anyone can notice it; all it takes is to slow down for
a moment and look for it. With some good design tools and a bit of practice, you
can catch all the wonder that Emily unfortunately missed out on.
Wonder is the first and most important mindset. It is a fundamental
viewpoint and an approach to life that helps you notice the meaningful
moments that make life wondrous! And when you go looking for wonder, you
inevitably encounter the mysteries of the universe; the things you know are real
but are beyond your understanding.
