So, what is mystery, and why is it so important?
Pretty simple, really—a mystery is something you can experience and
recognize, often together with other people, but for which you don’t (yet, if
ever) have a complete explanation. It’s an experiential reality that’s
comprehensible, but not describable. The challenge with mystery is to let it be
and not to get caught up in trying to understand it—literally trying to demystify
it.
A classic example of a wonder-full moment is being caught up in the glory of
a beautiful sunset. We’ve all had that experience—but it’s no less wondrous for
its commonality. An especially indelible sunset happened to Dave once while
driving up the coast from Santa Cruz to San Francisco on the legendary
California Highway 1. Dave drives this road all the time and has seen countless
fabulous sunsets along the way. But one particular Thursday, the sunset was
astonishing. Just south of Half Moon Bay, at four minutes before it was to set,
the sun crossed a layer of clouds, and the sky utterly exploded into a chorus of
red, orange, yellow, purple, and magenta—all laced with luminescent silver
clouds. It was like those just unbelievable pictures in photography magazines,
only far more intense. It was so gorgeous it hurt. Dave was so blown away, he
had to stop the car. He got out and just gawked. And so did all the other drivers.
In seconds, the highway came to a halt, and the shoulder was lined with people
transfixed in the moment. After a few short minutes, it faded. When the sun



finally hit the water and the spell broke, there was a short gasp of silence, then… a
roar. All the watchers started clapping and jumping up and down, shouting, “WH-O-O-O-A-A!!!” Then they all looked at one another with a strange intimacy
born of just having had the same miraculous experience. The couple nearest
Dave ran up to him, shouting, “Did you SEE THAT?! WOW, wasn’t it
AMAZING?!” After a few more flabbergasted exclamations, the guy gave Dave a
high five, the woman gave him a big hug, and everyone got back in their cars and
back on the road. That happened over twenty years ago, and it’s as clear a
memory for Dave today as it was that Thursday—and no less wonderful and
mysterious.
It’s a great example of wonder and mystery. Sure, we know why sunsets
behave the way they do—why the longer path through the atmosphere at the
horizon filters out the blues and greens and favors the red spectrum of sunlight.
But that’s not the mystery. The mystery is why does it so utterly arrest our
attention and make us feel so alive and so bonded to the total stranger next to
us? When Dave’s award-winning Highway 1 sunset ended, people weren’t
running up to one another and saying, “Huh—that was weird. Do you know
why the colors do that?” No one said, “Man, glad that’s over and we can get back
on the road!” What everyone experienced was a specific moment that activated
awe and wonder. The beauty of that sunset, while amazing and glorious, was still
just one particular moment of especially bright colors. But those particular
colors in that moment touched the deep, universally shared human experience of
beauty—and it transformed everyone who was willing to receive it. This refrain
from the Noel Paul Stookey song “Miracles” captures it well.
Now a scientist may tell you how night turns into day,
But he can never take the wonder away.
Now, some people need to have an explanation for everything because
mystery leaves them a little unsettled. But designers embrace ambiguity and
mystery. We know that if you over-rationalize your world and try to explain the
mysterious, you diminish the experience of it and risk missing the chance to let
wonder into your life. To help make the process of finding wonder a little less



mysterious, we’ve come up with an equation for it (after all—we have
engineering degrees, too):
Curiosity + Mystery = Wonder
Curiosity is a prerequisite to wonder and is never more powerful than when
it’s directed toward mystery. Mystery is amazing by itself, and it is a reliable route
to wonder and awe. The great news is that mystery is lying all over the place. All
you need is your basic human curiosity and an openness to mystery. Wonder
occurs when you let curiosity go as far as it wants to—so that you see deeply into
the mystery latent in even the simplest things right in front of you. You find it in
nature, you find it in science, music, art, and, for some people, in a spiritual or
religious experience.
However you describe the experience of wonder, once you’ve been “there,”
you want to go back as often as possible. The mindset of wonder, of being open
to real-izing this other nonrational world of mystery and experience, is
intoxicating. When you accept mystery and approach the world with wonder,
when you have the expectation that something wonderful is about to happen,
along the Way, it often does.

Availability (Especially to the Particular)
The world is full of particular opportunities and experiences that can change our
lives. These moments show up all the time, but only if you are available to them.
It’s an old adage that nothing happens until someone is ready, willing, and able.
Adopting a mindset of availability just means your starting place is to be “ready
and willing” for whatever comes next. This posture has the magical quality of
suddenly creating all sorts of new possibilities for experiences and actions in the
world. The Designer’s Way invites you to attain a state of constant readiness for

[Embedded visual 1 on PDF page 47 (499x444 px). Simple blue line drawing of a hanging sign reading “Open,” illustrating availability.]



action by adopting a mindset of availability. Now if you’re like a lot of people
we’ve worked with, when you read that, you might respond…
“Wait! Did you just say ‘a state of constant readiness for action’?”
“Yes, we did.”
“Are you kidding?! That feels impossible! The place I’m in is a state of
constantly barely being able to keep up. Just how and where am I supposed to
find all this availability?”
We hear you. All these mindsets, and certainly availability, are aspirational.
They are ways of living and being that we strive for but never fully attain—
which is totally okay. Any time we can access these mindsets, even briefly or
episodically, we have a better shot at successful moment-making. While adopting
a mindset of availability continually may seem daunting if not impossible,
accessing it momentarily can be refreshingly easy. One of our colleagues,
Annalise, related this story to us recently.
“I was working from home that day and was just logging off one Zoom
meeting and logging on to another when I heard some noise behind me. I looked
over my shoulder, and there was my ten-year-old deliriously diving into a pile of
LEGO blocks. Just as I was about to say, ‘Quiet!’ I caught myself—then I waited
and looked. There was my beautiful child in a moment of pure delight—and I
got to see it. Then I clicked into the meeting.”
That’s availability in action. If Annalise can do it, you can, too.
Total availability may take a lifetime to attain, but occasionally being just a
little bit more available is quite doable. All it takes is some intentionality and
readiness to discover a doorway to lots of lovely experiences that are just waiting
for you. Availability takes a leaning in posture that can jump-start good things
happening—often without having to initiate them yourself. Being available
doesn’t require taking initiative; it just requires a readiness. This is great news
especially for introverts who often struggle with making the first move.
Bill—who is pretty far down the line on the introvert scale—says one of his
least favorite experiences—and probably yours, too, if you are like Bill—is being
in a room with a whole bunch of strangers and being forced to make small talk.
He just wants to crawl in a hole and hide. However, Bill finds that when he can
inhabit the mindset of availability in these situations, something amazing



happens. His body language changes, his crossed arms (a defense mechanism of
introverts) drop, and his stance widens. He stops staring at the floor and instead
scans the room. He “looks” more available. Better than that, his social anxiety all
but disappears. He thinks this is because he doesn’t feel forced to initiate
anything and can just be and observe all the things that are happening around
him. Bill is often delighted by the conversations that pop up spontaneously—he
doesn’t know why this happens but figures something about his body language
and emotional state must signal that he’s in an availability mindset. If you’re like
Bill (introverts unite), try availability the next time you face a tough social
situation.
Do you know someone who just seems to have all the luck, for whom things
just seem to fall in place easily and frequently? It turns out that high-availability
people are luckier, too—and who doesn’t want more luck? Now these folks
aren’t actually luckier than you, whatever that means, but studies of people who
report themselves as lucky say they are most likely more available than the
average. Research says that people who describe themselves as being lucky are in
fact measurably more aware of the opportunities around them. They both see
the possibilities (they’re “ready”) and are more open to responding to them
(they’re “willing”) than others are—in other words, more available. This
mindset allows them to find their way forward more easily, which gives them the
appearance of “being lucky.” Being available is, therefore, an incredibly valuable
mindset for doing successful moment-making—because the only place and time
you can practice availability along the Way is in the here and now.
In the moment.

Radical Acceptance
Radical acceptance is the mindset that sets you up for the full range of
wonderful possibilities in a life lived along the Designer’s Way. Dave likes to say,
“You can’t solve a problem you’re not willing to have.” What he means is that

[Embedded visual 1 on PDF page 49 (680x345 px). OCR-readable text: Ak]



while you may wish you were in a different situation, you aren’t! To make a
change or address a problem, you have to accept that it’s your problem to solve.
People fail far too often just because they are directing all their energies into
wishing for a different reality or complaining about their current reality—none
of which helps them design something that can improve that reality.
There are countless opportunities to apply this mindset, since the
transformative power of radical acceptance always applies to reality—from the
most trivial situations to the most impactful. Here’s a common example that
most of us can relate to. You’re sitting on an airplane that has just backed away
from the gate and is beginning to taxi out to the runway for takeoff when the
plane suddenly stops and the captain announces, “Well, folks, I’ve got some bad
news…. Looks like there’s stormy weather brewing at the destination of the plane
just ahead of us in line. Air traffic control has put that plane on a hold for at least
forty minutes, so we’re going to have to wait in line here a bit longer before we
get underway. We’ll keep you apprised as the situation unfolds.” Everyone
groans. Some of the passengers calmly accept the news, recline their seat, and
open a book. But not everyone. The guy sitting two rows behind you is just not
having it. “HEY! I didn’t pay full fare for this kind of nonsense. Get this damn
plane moving! Just pull around that other plane and get us off the ground. What
kinda Mickey Mouse outfit is this airline anyway?!”
Arguing with reality doesn’t change it. No matter how loud or adamant the
guy in Seat 14B gets, he’s not going to change the weather or speed up that
plane. If he gets vociferous enough, he might earn the right to be removed from
the plane by an air marshal (both Bill and Dave have been flying long enough to
see that happen), but his noisy attempt at rejecting the situation changes
nothing. He doesn’t actually have the problem of “How can I speed up this
plane?” The only problem that reality is offering for him to solve is “How would
I like to use these extra forty minutes?” But he can’t access a solution to the real
problem (or make the most of the real opportunity) until he accepts the reality
in which he finds himself.
Dave learned the power of radical acceptance in a much more significant way
in early March 2020, when he and his wife, Claudia, suddenly learned she was
terminally ill. Claudia was diagnosed with a very aggressive breast cancer and had



as few as six but not more than twenty-four months to live. The news was
devastating, but there was no arguing with it. Claudia realized that every day she
spent in complaint or denial was at the expense of a day well lived. In less than a
week, they figured out how to make the most of what time she had left, and she
and Dave lived it to the fullest for the next nine months until her death in
December. During those nine months, Claudia gave herself exactly one day to
feel sorry for herself—to rant at what felt like a far-too-soon demise—then
returned to making the most of it. Family and friends regularly spoke of her
amazing courage and resolute approach to death. She saw it more simply: “It’s
not of my choosing, but it’s true—so why waste the time?” Her practice of
radical acceptance not only transformed her and Dave’s life but made a huge
impact on the hundreds of people connected to them. Claudia reminded
everyone that while living that year was hard, deciding how to was not. Radical
acceptance was the only path to the best of whatever life had to offer.
Accepting the reality of your world, and accepting the constraints that reality
imposes, is always the first step to designing your better future. Designers take a
view of the world that is brutally realistic because a good design can only start in
the actual real circumstances, even if those circumstances aren’t “good” or “fair.”
This is sometimes misunderstood as capitulating or settling for things as they
are, but that couldn’t be further from what we mean. Accepting reality as it is, is
not an endorsement of that reality. For instance, accepting that the world is full
of inequality is not the same thing as endorsing it—not at all. You’ll be more
effective redesigning the situation if you understand and accept the reality of the
circumstances on the ground and up front. Then you are set up for a more
powerful reframe, a way of looking at reality that offers more options and the
freedom to design solutions that are unique.
Radical acceptance is also a prerequisite to being present in the moment and
finding wonder. If we want to design more meaning in our lives by more fully
engaging and enjoying the moment we’re in (do good moment-making), we
can’t get there by arguing with it, analyzing it, negotiating with it, wishing it
were different, or complaining about it—all of which are responses that push us
out of the moment and into our heads and our negative emotions. When people



find themselves in a situation they don’t like, they often say, “It’s not fair, it’s not
what I had in mind.”
Exactly.
We (all of us) do our best to make life work. We imagine a future and try to
bring it into being. Then reality gets in the way and presents us a choice: to
accept reality and enter into what is possible—or to reject it and keep living
inside our heads, rattling around with that idea of ours that didn’t work out.
Acceptance of reality is the only choice that can result in making meaning
and finding our way to being fully alive.
To be present to a moment, which is how we can make meaning from it, we
have to accept it. Doing that regularly, committing yourself to accepting reality
as quickly as possible in every situation, is a radical act that can change your life.
Whether it’s as simple as a delayed flight or as crushing as a cancer diagnosis,
radical and relentless acceptance is always the doorway leading to the life-giving
path forward. Radical acceptance is a superpower mindset that gives you, the
designer, access to more freedom and more creativity along the Way.



Fully Engaged and Calmly Detached
This approach to life suggests that a state of mind that is fully engaged but
calmly detached is the best place from which to do design. When you are fully
engaged in any task—really buckling down and getting the work done—you
have the best chance of unlocking a great solution. However, most of us can’t
shake off the fear of a bad outcome, and this tends to stifle our creativity and
burden our actions. But when you can cultivate a mindset that is detached from
outcomes, which are ultimately out of your control, you are the freest and most
creative. When something goes as hoped for, you’re pleased. When something
unexpected or negative happens, you’re bemused and happy to learn from the
experience (which is why you come to think that just about everything is a
prototype).

[Embedded visual 1 on PDF page 53 (438x507 px). OCR-readable text: g]



One of the most influential professors Bill encountered as an undergrad at
Stanford was Professor Ron Howard (1934–2024). Professor Howard
“invented” the field of decision analysis back in 1964. He developed a complex
yet elegant way of assessing any decision as a series of quantifiable probabilities,
thus allowing you to make the best possible decision when confronted with any
question, big or small. One of his most important findings about good decisionmaking is contained in the inequality:
Quality of the Decision ≠ Quality of the Outcome
At first glance, this might seem wrong. “Of course good decisions lead to
good outcomes, and bad decisions lead to bad ones—everybody knows that!”
But actually, our lived experience tells us otherwise. Let’s say that we are all
attending one of Bill’s Stanford lectures on discernment. After the lecture, Bill
looks at his weather app and determines that it’s a warm, sunny day out on the
coast (the Pacific Ocean is only about thirty miles from the Stanford campus),
and he invites the class to join him for “office hours on the beach.” He and his
students pile into their cars, only to discover that it’s cold, foggy, and raining at
the beach when they arrive. It’s miserable, and they head home—no office hours
today.
Did Bill make a bad decision? No, he made a good decision based on the best
information available.
Clearly, something beyond his control, a sudden change in the weather,
changed the “design constraints” of the problem, and Bill got a bad outcome.
Should he be attached to this outcome? Should he hold himself responsible for
the fog and cold? Should Bill hold the “Bill who made the decision with the
information available at Stanford” responsible for the information that Bill
received later in the day, once he got to the beach? Should Bill be responsible for
information that is only available in the future?
Of course not. When you put it that way, it makes no sense to hold Bill
accountable for information about the decision to go to the beach that was only
available in the future.



Professor Howard’s observation that decisions and outcomes are only
connected by probabilities, and that you cannot control what is outside of your
control, is critical to understanding this mindset. When you are fully engaged in
your life and the decisions you make, you are exercising your agency, and when
you are calmly detached from the outcomes, you are fully acknowledging the
things that are out of your control. Here’s the key thing to keep in mind:
Engagement is all about your activity—your level of active participation in what
you’re doing—which is something over which you have total control.
Detachment is all about the outcome, the end result of your activity—which is
something over which you have very little control.
If you’re finding the concept of detachment a little fuzzy, let’s see if we can
make it clearer by looking at it in operation in something as simple as a baseball
game. Imagine your neighbor has invited you to attend his kid’s Little League
baseball game. You go and witness the following: In the final inning, the team at
bat is losing by one run. There are two outs. Suzanne is on second with Ellis on
third, and Carlos is up to bat. The crowd is going crazy. As Carlos steps up to
the plate, Suzanne’s mom screams, “Don’t blow this, Carlos—get Suzy and Ellis
home or we’ll lose!” Carlos’s dad then calls out, “Just focus on the pitch, Carlos,
and let the bat do the work. You’ve got this, buddy!” In that situation, Suzanne’s
mom is totally attached to the outcome of her daughter scoring and winning the
game. Carlos’s dad is calmly detached from those outcomes and is trying to help
Carlos fully engage with the one thing he can do—try to get his bat on the ball.
Carlos’s best shot at having a good at-bat is to tap into his dad’s calm and not
pick up Suzy’s mom’s anxiety.
This mindset of being fully engaged and calmly detached is all about how
you can direct your energies effectively with minimal wasted effort or emotion—
it’s simply smart personal resource management. By fully engaging in the design
process, you direct all your attention and intention to the problem. When you
replace anxiety about outcomes with calm detachment, you gain a big boost in
energy, focus, and flow—all of which, paradoxically, lead to better outcomes.
Fully engaged and calmly detached are the crucial ingredients for a more
meaningful life.



Create Your World
Good designers don’t ever accept the default reality or the default story, the one
where life just happens to you and you take whatever comes along. When you
are practicing the designer’s mindsets, you are actively creating and cocreating
your world, by yourself and with others. You are able to see patterns in what
looks to others like a seemingly random collection of actions and information.
You tune in to the process and the particularity of life’s moments and are
available to the possibilities of what’s coming. When you can pull those elements
together into a narrative about your life and future, you are actively involved in

[Embedded visual 1 on PDF page 56 (476x583 px). OCR-readable text: O / ]]



creating your world. Crafting that story and letting it inform and direct your
perspective on life is empowering.
There’s an old saying that “life is a story we tell ourselves,” so taking
responsibility for what story you are telling yourself and others can contribute
mightily to how you actually experience the world. We don’t mean living in a
fantasy, which isn’t helpful, but living into a thoughtful narrative that keeps you
moving forward while giving you a leg up on processing and integrating
whatever changes or surprises life brings you.
In our work at Stanford, we’ve seen a clear demonstration of the importance
of knowing your personal story in the lives of first-generation students. All the
students at Stanford are smart—really smart. But they come from a huge range
of backgrounds and situations, and we have a significant number of first-gen
students—people who are the first in their family of origin to ever attend a
university. These students, while quite capable, are often less prepared and less
resourced than other students from more college-ready circumstances. A
common framing of a first-gen student’s story is “I’m starting way behind, and
I’ll never catch up.”
Extensive research has gone into identifying how best to help these students
succeed. It turns out that a single fifteen-minute intervention with one of these
students in which (1) they learn the fact that determined hard work is a more
reliable predictor of student success than level of prior advantage and during
which (2) they are introduced to a successful student who comes from a similar
background and can assure them that they can succeed has a strong positive
impact that can last all the way through a four-year college experience and
beyond. It doesn’t matter that it was only fifteen minutes. What matters is that
the student’s personal narrative is redesigned—in a supportable and truthful
way—to empower the individual to live more meaningfully and successfully.
When “I’ll never catch up” is rewritten into “I have a lot to accomplish, and I
know how to do it,” everything changes.
And it’s not just first-gen students—it’s all of us. Every one of us is telling
ourselves a story about our lives every day. Designing that story carefully,
responsibly, and generatively makes all the difference in the world. It’s your story
—so take charge of it.



The wise designer is an adaptive storyteller capable of weaving in new threads
of the story as they appear. Creating your story, being proactive and positive
about your future, is the natural next step from the mindset of availability, being
ready and open to your present.
This mindset is how you create an intention for yourself, your family, and
your community, and then how you turn that intention into action in the
world. When you participate in the active creation of your world, you move in
flow with the world you are trying to create. Then, when you create a story out
of your journey, and radically accept the circumstances of your life, you are
operating from a sense of abundance, and it is natural to invite others to join
you. You live together, in community with other meaning designers, each
pursuing their own lives along the Designer’s Way.
Mindset Twofers
Individually, these mindsets are powerful, but they can become downright
transformative when artfully combined. There are countless complementary
ways to mix and match these five mindsets, but we want to bring your attention
to two particularly generative pairs. These are the mindset twofers you really
want working for you.
Mindset Twofer #1: Radical Acceptance and Availability
Radical acceptance and availability are the original power couple. Acceptance
working by itself is good, but it doesn’t necessarily compel you forward. A cool,
measured acceptance might have you standing back to observe carefully before
making a move prematurely. Exercising prudence is good, but prudence can
easily grow into reticence, which breeds inertia or even skepticism—and we
don’t want that. Availability adopts a leaning-in posture that is not merely open
(willing) but open with a positive inclination to generative action (ready). The
accepting and available person is poised beautifully—adopting an objective
recognition of reality and doing so with a positive readiness to respond—but not
an overreadiness that might jump the gun prematurely. A practiced designer



who can attain an accepting and available mindset is in a perfect place to make
the most of almost any situation.
Just ask Arne.
Arne is a ninety-year-old, single, wheelchair-bound, largely unsung artist
living on a fixed income in a spartan studio in an artist collective in a warehouse
in downtown Atlanta. None of those descriptors are of Arne’s choosing, except
for the studio in the artist collective. Arne used to be a globetrotting, happily
romanced, financially patroned man-about-town—a real raconteur. Much has
changed as he’s aged. At first glance, you might think Arne is stuck in a sad life.
You’d be wrong. Arne is the most vibrant, alive, engaged, and enthusiastic
person you’ve ever met. As his circumstances and his health have declined, he has
radically accepted every hit and every loss. It’s just the new reality, the new
baseline from which to keep living into what remains. He spends zero energy
complaining about or even dwelling on his losses and constraints—which are
sizable and numerous! The secret to his successful super-capacity of acceptance
is his commitment to relentless availability. Were he merely accepting, he’d be
sitting quietly in his studio apartment, calmly enduring the very little going on
and tolerating the nowhere to go. The loneliness, boredom, and emptiness
would eventually be overwhelming and claw him toward despair. But that never
happened. Regardless of what he’s lost, Arne’s focus is entirely on what he does
have and what may come of it. A lifelong painter, musician, and teacher of
filmmaking, he has a deep love of the arts. So—with all this new free time and
little distraction (being bound to his wheelchair in his apartment hour after
hour), he’s finally free to read more deeply! He reads two or three books a week,
preferably long and difficult books—to which he can finally give time and
attention. When Bill walked into Arne’s apartment a few months ago, the first
thing he said was, “Omigosh, Bill! Have you read BALZAC?! He’s amazing—sit
down, we have to talk about this book!” He isn’t just reading to pass the time,
he’s available to the fullest possible experience of reading. He’s relishing the
craft of writing, analyzing various styles, utterly reveling in and savoring the
power of story and the drama of history. He’s really into it!
That’s the difference availability makes. Rather than resist or struggle against
the world as it is and what he’s had to give up—such as being able to go out on



his own during the day—Arne takes the resource freed by his acceptance and
leans into it in a new, generative way. Arne wonderfully demonstrates the
Designer’s Way as he combines acceptance with availability (not merely
resignation—which is often as far as acceptance goes). When acceptance and
availability combine, it’s transformative. Let them work for you, too.
Mindset Twofer #2: Availability and Wonder
Availability naturally combines with wonder to allow much-improved access to
meaningful moment-making. When these mindsets are held in tandem, you are
engaging the world by saying, “Okay, life, I’m ready… for wonder!” Wonder
becomes the valence of your availability—it’s the direction that availability is
leaning toward. Arne’s story shows us how; he loves to play the harpsichord and
has one in his studio. He loves how it sounds and how the keys feel, so he’s glad
of the chance to play it more. Having tired of the pieces he knew, he started
trying something he’d never done before—improvising. All he wanted was to
play something, so he just wheeled over to the keyboard and started playing
whatever his fingers wanted to play. He was making himself available to the
instrument and the untapped skills his hands possessed. With time, he developed
a unique, free-form improv style and loved it! He was playing a kind of sound
he’d not heard before. As he let himself fall deeply into the wonder of this new
sound, he could play himself into a trancelike meditation experience that could
last for hours. A visitor asked what he was playing lately, and when Arne
demonstrated a bit of his new improv, the visitor was astonished at the sounds
and remarked, “Wow—I’ve never heard anything like that before! Can I bring
some friends over to listen to you?” And suddenly, Arne’s experience of wonder
was multiplied. Arne’s availability to the harpsichord transformed his
homebound-ness into a totally new experience of wonder that the younger, less
constrained Arne would never have discovered.
There is wonder lying right below the surface of our lives if we, like Arne, can
just be available to it. The next time you are at the farmers market, be more
available to the colors and smells of all the fresh fruits and vegetables
surrounding you. Wonder at the variety and ask yourself, Who are the amazing
humans who grew this abundance, and where did all of this amazing food come



from? Or on one of your daily walks or runs, be available to the sounds of the
environment passing by and feel how your feet propel you through space.
Wonder at the springiness of your running shoes and ask yourself, What
designer designed these shoes, and how did they make them feel so good?
Having the readiness of your availability tuned to wonder’s frequency can
reap big dividends in becoming more fully alive. Don’t miss it.
A Becoming
If these five mindsets collectively define the Designer’s Way—just where is it
we’re on our way to? Where is this way taking us?
It’s taking us on the journey of becoming.
Our favorite definition of the human person is a becoming. We frequently
hear people who get stuck arguing about whether they feel more like a human
be-ing or a human do-ing. They see this issue as a zero-sum game they can’t win.
The more you do, the less you become. These fundamental concepts about who
you really are compete with one another, and finding a balance can seem
impossible.

We don’t see it that way. We see being and doing as complementary parts of a
virtuous cycle—the Be-Do-Become cycle.

In this straightforward, generative model, you start with who you are (“Be”)
and go out into the world and do stuff (“Do”) as coherently as you can. As you
are doing stuff (engaging with the world), you reflect on your engagements and

[Embedded visual 1 on PDF page 61 (540x501 px). OCR-readable text: Be-ing | Do-ing / a” ae]

[Embedded visual 2 on PDF page 61 (599x565 px). OCR-readable text: EY / Sead]



take them to heart to grow into becoming your further evolved self (“Become”).
The new you that you’ve become (your new “Be”) starts all over doing new stuff,
and the process continues.
We are all humans in the process of becoming—becoming more human,
becoming more our authentic selves, and becoming more adept at making the
most of life along the way.
When you change the way you think, adopt new mindsets, and open yourself
up to thinking like a designer, and you invest in the cycle of becoming the next
better version of yourself, you are well on your way to designing more meaning
in life, for yourself and everyone you meet.
TRY STUFF
We’re still building up our foundation for our meaning-making project, but let’s see if
we can’t start making it work for us immediately. As an initial exercise, let’s try out one
of the Mindset Twofers: radical acceptance and availability.
Exercise: Choose Life
How to let radical acceptance and availability transform any (or every) day.
Bill has a favorite daily practice that he developed out of this work and that he uses
to start every day. While he’s shaving, he says two things to himself out loud (it’s very
important to say it out loud, because then you’re much more likely to believe yourself,
which is crucial to make this practice work).
Day Start Statement #1: “I live in the best of all possible worlds.”
Day Start Statement #2: “Everything I do today, I choose to do.”
Now Bill realizes this may sound a little delusional, because we all know the world
in general is messed up in myriad ways. And our world specifically may not even be
close to our definition of what best looks like. And each of us has had long days of
drudgery where it feels like we have no choice about how to spend our time.
We get it.
We aren’t promoting magical thinking or toxic positivity. But given the choice of
starting his day lamenting the evils of the world and his must-do list or adopting the
designer’s mindset of create your world—Bill chooses to create his world.
Because words matter.
Bill stating that he lives in the best of all possible worlds establishes a positive
availability bias—it tilts him toward all the good potential in front of him today. Bill
could just as easily say, “I live in the worst of all possible worlds,” or “I live in the
world.”
All three versions are or could be equally true, but the mindsets are profoundly
different. A lot of positive psychology research has demonstrated that by starting the



day with the true and positively biased “best of all possible worlds,” you put the power
of availability to work for you and literally increase your probability of having positive
experiences.
The second statement announces that you are in charge of what you decide to do.
It doesn’t mean that you are in control of the day—not at all. All sorts of unplanned
crap happens in Bill’s day and probably yours, too. But by “choosing into” the day, you
accept the day about to unfold, before it’s even started. It gets your acceptance out on
the table immediately, which, when combined with your availability, completely frees
you to get the most out of the day you are about to live. This practice is simply you
choosing your attitude toward your life—intentionally, explicitly.
How does this work in reality?
Imagine starting your day with these two affirming statements. Then off you go to
work. You jump on the D14 bus like always, and eighteen minutes into your forty-threeminute commute, the bus gets a flat. “What?! Buses don’t get flats, do they?” Well,
yours just did. The driver tells everyone to get off the bus and wait on the sidewalk
while he assesses the situation. While everyone else is immediately getting upset and
yelling at the driver (which helps tremendously), you have the following conversation
with yourself: Gosh, why didn’t I just drive today? Then none of this would be happening!
Oh, wait—I chose to take the bus to reduce the stress of driving every day and to buy
some extra reading time. That was a great decision; it just didn’t work out that way
today. Hmmm… So, do I really live in the best possible world? Well, yes! I guess I do if I
live in a world where buses almost never get flats, and I’ve never been stranded by a bus
before. In fact, I live in a world of incredibly reliable buses 99 percent of the time.
As this occurs to you, you notice that your fellow passengers do not seem to be
having the same realization as you are. Most of them are either arguing with one
another or yelling something into their cell phones. You order an Uber, which will still
take twelve minutes since it’s peak rush hour, and you realize you get that extra
reading time you were hoping for. The only difference between you and the dozen
unhappy people all around you is that you took the Designer’s Way of acceptance and
availability this morning by doing your choosing life practice—and living into it.
Exercise: The Got To–Get To Reversal
Here’s a very simple yet powerful way to implement the twofer of acceptance and
availability. All you have to do is replace got to with get to when you describe what
you’re already about to do. If you’re in a got to situation, there’s no room for you to
accept the task or activity, and you act like a victim of circumstance. When you get to
do something, it is easy and natural to be available for the task or activity. You choose
to want to participate.
Like we said, the words really matter.
So, instead of “I’ve got to attend another meeting,” you reframe it as “Hey, these
people want me to be part of their conversation. Isn’t it great I get to be a part of what
they’re working on?”
Instead of “I’ve got to get my information to my tax guy this week or he won’t have
time to finish by April 15, and I’m going to pay a late penalty again!” you reframe it as
“I’m sure lucky that I get to have the kind of income and investments that my taxes
require the services of an accountant. I need to remember to get him what he needs
on time so he can help me.”



You’ll find with a little practice that you can shift almost any got to into a get to.
Give it a try and see how this small move can result in a big positive shift in your day.
Eventually, you will find that all you need to do to accomplish the transformation is
change that one vowel:
“I’ve got to redo the budget” becomes “I get to redo the budget.”
“I’ve got to be the one to tell Mom that Dad has dementia” becomes “I get to be the
one who tells Mom that Dad has dementia.”
“I’ve got to get up with the sun to catch that early flight to Newark” becomes “I get to
get up with the sun to catch that early flight to Newark.”
Try it—you’re just a vowel away from a more meaningful experience.
Exercise: Reframing Ladder (Practicing Radical Acceptance)
Radical acceptance includes seeing things as they really are, naming your reality
without built-in judgment or bias, and fully embracing that reality. When all three are
accomplished, you are approaching truly radical acceptance.
This exercise is designed to get you from resistance to acceptance using a real
personal dilemma, problem, or situation to work on, and a tool we call the reframing
ladder. Bill learned this from his mentor at Stanford, Professor Bernie Roth. The goal
of the reframing ladder is to develop a practice of looking at your problems,
particularly one that you’ve been stuck on for a while, in a way that starts to generate
lots of solutions.
This exercise is supercharged by the mindset of fully engaged and calmly
detached. It works better if: 1) it’s a real problem and you have skin in the game
(engagement), and 2) you can let go of your current perspective and see the problem
afresh (detachment).
The technique is to start with a problem and see if, by moving up the reframing
ladder two or three times, you can come up with a new perspective and some new
solutions, thereby becoming unstuck!
Start by coming up with a problem that is real and juicy and alive for you right now,
but one that you’ve been stuck on for a while. Frame the problem using the sentence
stem: How might I…
How might I… get a better work-life balance?
How might I… make more money while still having time to spend with my family?
How might I… align my work with my values?
Pick one of your most interesting and perplexing personal problems, one you are
excited about but are the most stuck on. Put that problem on the bottom rung of the
ladder. In this case, let’s use a real example from a client that Bill is coaching; we will
call him Robert. Robert is a mid-career professional who is starting to burn out at
work. He wanted meaning and purpose in his job, but as he made more money and
had more people to manage, he felt further and further away from his core values.



“The money and the influence I have as a senior manager is great, but I don’t feel the
value of my job anymore—I think I’ve accidently become a bureaucrat.”
When he came to Bill, Robert had already started to work on his problem and had
drafted a problem statement that read: “How might I… align the job I do with my
values?” Bill realized that Robert was assuming that the “alignment” he was seeking
was going to come from changing something about his job. Robert wasn’t thinking
more broadly about his life. Bill suggested that, as they worked with the reframing
ladder, they change the word job to the word work. This would open up more
possibilities for what might come up. Robert agreed to try it.
Step 1
Begin by populating Rung #1. The first rung of Robert’s ladder had this slightly
modified statement: “How might I… align the work I do with my values?”
LIMIT
RUNG #3
RUNG #2
RUNG #1

How might I… align the work I do with my
values?

Step 2
Now, moving up the ladder one rung, you transform your problem to the next rung on
the ladder. The transformation question starts with this sentence stem: “What would I
get if…”
In this case, the sentence becomes:
Q: What would I get if… I aligned the work I do with my values?
Now come up with three answers to this question. In this example, three different
answers to the question might be:
1. I’d feel more energized about what I’m doing at work.
2. I could tell my friends that I’m not so bummed out all the time.
3. I’d feel like I could connect my work to something meaningful in the world.
Now you take that answer and turn it into the Rung #2 question. Robert picked
answer #3 as the one he was the most excited about, and turned it into Rung #2’s
How might I… question.
“How might I… connect my work to something meaningful in the world?”
Put this question on Rung #2.
LIMIT
RUNG #3



LIMIT
RUNG #2
RUNG #1

How might I… connect work to something
meaningful in the world?
How might I… align my work with my
values?

Step 3
Okay, we might already be to a place where we could come up with some interesting
new ideas, we are getting close to a reframe that might get us unstuck, but let’s go up
one more rung on the ladder just for fun.
Q: What would I get if… I connected work to something meaningful in the world?
Three brainstorming answers:
1. I’d be able to explain to my friends how I made my job more fulfilling.
2. I would engage with a community of people who cared about sustainability.
3. I could start a side hustle consulting on the subjects that I am an expert in.
Now, using one of these answers, construct your new problem as a How might I…
question on Rung #3.
Robert picked answer #2 and created the new Rung #3 How might I… statement.
Q: How might I… engage with a community of people who care about sustainability?
LEVEL
RUNG #3
RUNG #2

RUNG #1

How Might I Statements… for
Brainstorming
How Might I… engage with a community
of people who care about sustainability?
How Might I… connect what I’m doing at
work to something with real meaning in
the world?
How Might I… align my work with my
values?

Both Rungs #2 and #3 create a new solution space—one that is much larger than
the original problem. Brainstorming with these How might I… statements will yield new
and surprising results.
This reframing exercise led Robert to a very productive brainstorm that included
looking into some industry groups in his field (which would lead to more connection)
that promoted sustainable industry practices (one of his core values). It also opened
him up to a wide range of possible prototypes, things he could try right away. He could
connect with NGOs, governmental and nongovernmental standards groups, and
industry associations, all of which worked in areas that fit his criteria of values
alignment. Robert came up with the idea of creating an organization that matched
career mentors to students interested in sustainability and pitched it to his boss.



Robert subsequently redesigned part of his job to include running a high-impact
youth-mentoring organization, mentoring the next generation of sustainability leaders.
It’s a win-win-win.
Remember, at each step up the ladder, we came up with three ideas to “solve” the
problem statement. The outcome would have been very different if we’d taken another
path “up the ladder.” That’s okay; it’s even a feature of the technique. If for some
reason you are not getting more and more ideas as you move up the ladder—choose a
different path.
The reframing ladder is a way of practicing radical acceptance. It allows you to
unpack a problem, includes really seeing things as they are, forces you to name and
rename your reality without a built-in bias, and lets you embrace your reality fully.
Climb the ladder to radical acceptance and get unstuck.
(WARNING: Bernie also taught Bill to never go more than three rungs up the ladder.
People who go too far up the ladder end up quitting the world, shaving their heads,
and staring at the sun all day. This tool is that powerful—consider yourself warned.)

3

A Tale of Two Worlds
A statistician from Princeton, George E. P. Box, said, “All models are wrong, but some
are useful.” In this chapter, we introduce a model to reframe your worlds. That’s right
—not your world singular but your worlds plural. Because there are actually two of
them—the transactional world and the flow world—and they present two different
domains of human experience.
Now if you’re like us (and most people we know and have encountered in our
work), you are probably stuck in the day-to-day, get-the-job-done model of your
current world (i.e., the transactional world). If so, don’t feel bad—it’s not your fault.
It’s the normal outcome of the modern busy lifestyle of people like us. However, it’s a
real problem if what you’re after is more meaning, joy, and wonder, because those
things are very hard to find in that world.
But it doesn’t have to be like that.
There’s another world right in front of us all the time. This is the full-andimmediate-presence-of-all-that-is-and-is-happening-this-very-instant-from-as-near-asyour-own-heartbeat-to-as-far-as-the-edge-of-the-cosmos world. We call this ongoing
stream of totality the flow world—as it’s flowing all around us all the time—and the
flow world is where meaning, joy, and wonder are found in abundance. We’re here to
help you get more access to the good things waiting for you in the flow world while
still navigating the transactional world.
The Transactional World
Let’s be real—we all need to navigate the transactional world. It’s the world we’re most
familiar with, and we ignore it at our peril (yes, the trash does have to go out every



Thursday morning). This transactional world, where one thing causes another, and
random things happen, triggering other random things, often with unknown or
unpredictable outcomes, is the world most of us live in most of the time and the world
many people live in all the time. Your job in this world is to process all these
transactions efficiently and with as few errors as possible. Get the kids to school, go to
work, buy the groceries, please the boss, plan for retirement, solve the energy crisis
(Dave hasn’t given up on this)—all good things to do and all “transactions.” This
default reality works because, most of the time, transactions are all we need to get by.
In fact, evolution would be highly selective for a transactional intelligence. See the tiger
—run from the tiger. Have a baby—protect the baby. Catch the deer—eat the deer.
Don’t want to run out of oil—invent electric cars. Transactions work. They are
fundamental to surviving. But they can be anathema to thriving, because our focus in
the transactional world is on what’s about to come next in order to ensure we get the
right outcome (safe babies, deer-burgers, affordable e-cars) and the kind of thriving,
full aliveness we’re after is only found in the present moment. Transactions, while
necessary, continually take us out of the present and into the future. Joy, meaning, and
aliveness only exist in the immediacy of the present moment. Not what happens next
or what happened before.
We want you to have it both ways. You can have it both ways. You need not only
experience the world of transactions. We want you to regularly experience another
deeper, richer world, right here, right now, in what is almost like a parallel reality.
Flow
Whether you’ve heard of flow before or not, most of us have had the experience of
being in flow. We’ve all plunged so deeply into a task—polishing a must-win proposal,
perfecting a loved one’s favorite dish, or hammering cross-court winners—that the
world fades, our focus sharpens, and when we finally surface, we’re startled to see how
many hours have slipped away. Flow was first formally defined in 1990 in the seminal
book Flow: The Psychology of Optimal Experience by Mihaly Csikszentmihalyi (MEEhy CHEEK-sent-mee-HAH-yee), the world-renowned researcher and cofounder with
Martin Seligman of the field of positive psychology. This research describes flow as a
psychological state in which an individual experiences some or all of the following:
total immersion in what they are doing
time standing still



complete concentration and focus
full awareness of and responsiveness to other direct participants in the situation
(most commonly observed in team sports)
an incredible sense of satisfaction during the experience—even euphoria
Flow shows up when the challenge at hand matches your abilities—no harder, no
easier. The task can be simple or sophisticated; what matters is that your skills and their
demands lock into equilibrium, pulling your full attention into the moment. Hit that
sweet spot, and flow can happen anywhere. Bill goes into flow chopping onions for
dinner, Dave on his sailboat with a good breeze. Flow is perhaps best known in the
world of sports where it’s frequently referred to as “being in the zone.”
People often reported that being in a state of flow felt meaningful afterward. In his
1990 classic, Csikszentmihalyi wrote about flow and meaning-making, stating:
The flow experience… is not “good” in an absolute sense. It is good only in that
it has the potential to make life more rich, intense, and meaningful.
It’s important to note that in this definition, flow is not a place you go, it’s a
psychological state you enter when you deeply engage in some specific activity. So how
do we get from Csikszentmihalyi’s idea of the flow state to our idea of a flow world?
We suggest that the reason people find the flow state meaningful is not necessarily
because the activity is important or world-impacting (chopping onions does help get
dinner on the table, but thinner onions are never going to save the world or imbue
great meaning on an outcome). The person in flow is fully present to what they’re
doing (chopping carefully and precisely), and they’re fully present to their situation,
surroundings, and circumstances (the knife, the cutting board, the onion, the light on
the counter). That combination focuses the energies and attention of the person on
the immediate reality in which the activity is taking place, which allows magic to
happen—the magic of an intense experience of being alive—of being human. It is this
sense of full aliveness and full humanness in the moment that makes the state of flow
inherently meaningful.
As Joseph Campbell famously said in The Power of Myth film series:
People say that what we’re all seeking is a meaning for life. I don’t think that’s
what we’re really seeking. I think that what we’re seeking is… [to] actually feel
the rapture of being alive.



Remember our distinction between impact and meaning. Impact is an outcome.
Meaning is how we experience our felt response to an encounter that matters to us.
The flow state is inherently meaningful because of the purity of aliveness we
experience in it—as Campbell puts it—the rapture of being alive.
The Flow World
So, here’s the new idea—the place that the flow state comes from is the flow world.
The flow world is the process of everything happening in a particular moment. It’s
the whole river of reality that is coursing underneath, around, and within us all the
time. It’s always going on, everywhere, over there, and right here. The conversation
you’re in. The breeze blowing through the window. The quantum probabilities
bouncing around in your lap at the subatomic level. The expanding cosmos. The
physical transformation of the cookies baking in the oven. The state of flow is
experienced when a person fully participates in their particular place in the flow world
at the present moment. And that last part is important—the flow world is only
accessible in the present moment. This is a critical distinction between the flow world
and the transactional world.
In the transactional world, everything we’re doing and focused on is understood in
terms of the past or the future: How did we get here? Do we have everything we need?
Is everyone present? Is this going to work? When will we finish? Is it good enough?
Now what do I do?
In the transactional world, the outcome is everything—but the outcome typically
lives in the future, which is not where we in fact are living. We are in the present. So,
the transactional world is constantly pushing us out of the present and redirecting us
to an imagined future. We have developed incredibly sophisticated approaches to
navigating the transactional world—from AI-optimized to-do lists, to smart ovens that
know your lasagna better than you do, to team collaboration software to get more
done faster—and those techniques have been undeniably successful. So successful that
we use them all the time, but with the unfortunate side effect of never actually being a
full participant in the present moment because we’re so focused on what’s next.
This is also a hint as to why so many people feel as though their lives just aren’t
meaningful enough. When you define impact as your primary form of meaning, then
you have to get it in the transactional world, where you’re focused on that next thing
you’re working toward, and it’s always in the future. When you do have success and see
impact, it’s over as soon as it arrives. The goal has been met, but you are no longer
making that impact—it’s been made. So, you aren’t doing anything meaningful



anymore and you’ve got to get back out there and make more impact. Get back on the
wheel and run, run, run—because you’re never done, it’s never enough, and you will
never actually get anywhere, and certainly not to the mythical there you think you are
running toward.
It’s exactly the opposite in the flow world, where the present moment is all there is,
and all it offers is a chance to fully participate in it. In the flow world, there’s always
enough, because each moment is its own reward, as is the one after that and the one
after that.
One more reason tapping into the flow world is so meaningful is because it’s
transcendent. Now, we don’t want to go all woo-woo on you, and this isn’t a book
about spirituality (at least not like most of the spiritual books we know), but we have
to introduce the idea of transcendency because it’s an important—and thankfully
normal—aspect of life in the flow world. Remember Maslow’s last insight was that the
way to find meaning and experience the fullness of being human is through selftranscendence—getting beyond yourself. There are two ways that life in the flow world
contributes to transcendency: timelessness and interconnectedness.
The flow world is always encountered in the present moment, which effectively
makes it eternal—not eternal in the sense of lots and lots of time or happening on
some alternate astral plane but eternal in the sense of timelessness. It’s always now
o’clock in the flow world, so when you are fully there, fully “in the now,” your
experience has a timeless, eternal quality to it. People have a paradoxical experience of
time in the flow world. They report that time both stood still and seemed to go on
forever. The experience felt like it lasted for a long, long time and yet was over in a
flash. When in the flow world, and experiencing a flow state, we are so engaged in the
moment that we become self-forgetting. This frees us to experience the timelessness
and full aliveness of that moment—which is one common form of self-transcendence.
Flow also invites us to experience interconnectedness. A very commonly reported
aspect of being in flow is having a sense of everything all being part of one big unity.
This is easily seen when a sports team collectively goes into flow and every player feels
completely connected to and synchronized with every other player—moving
effortlessly and harmoniously together. This can happen because we are all in fact
interconnected to one another. The latest thinking in neuroscience, best characterized
in the work of Dr. Dan Siegel, founder of the Mindsight Institute and former clinical
professor of psychiatry at the UCLA School of Medicine, is that we aren’t just
autonomous, separate selves bumping into external entities and forces. Human



consciousness is not just a collection of electrical impulses inside our skulls but extends
into a network with the other people around us. So, when we experience that our
personhood, the humanity of each one of us, is in fact larger than our individual selves,
we become more human, and life gets more meaningful. The flow world is the place
where we can have encounters that make that interconnectedness real.
You may have noticed that most of what we just said about how we can attain a
degree of self-transcendency through the timelessness and interconnectedness found in
flow was in relation to being in the flow state—that special psychological state that is at
least infrequent if not elusive. Must you enter a full-blown flow state for these benefits
to unfold? Not at all. While an intense episode of being in flow allows for a more
powerful experience of meaningful transcendency, it isn’t an all-or-nothing deal. We
firmly believe that all encounters of the flow world are life-giving. We’ll develop this
idea more fully in chapter 6, but we think any time spent in the flow world is time well
spent. And you don’t have to pull out your yoga mat or book a weekend at the retreat
center to get there. The flow world is a mere glance away. Slow down when tasting that
next sip of coffee instead of just gulping through it—it won’t cost you a second of
time, and those coffee beans gave their lives to make that moment in yours. Once you
get the hang of tapping into it, you are well on your way to designing a life full of
meaning.
Playing a Different Game
Life in the flow world is quite different from life in the transactional world.
Remember, both worlds are present all the time—the difference is how you experience
them. We can illustrate this with something as simple as two versions of the same
hypothetical workday morning routine—one lived entirely in the transactional world,
and one that includes the flow world as well.
Imagine Fritz, a thirty-eight-year-old financial analyst working in a midsize regional
bank. He lives in the city in a ground-floor apartment, still loves to skateboard, and has
a girlfriend—Haley. No pets. He’s got a big presentation this afternoon. Here’s how
his morning goes, living entirely in the transactional world. At 6:30 a.m., his cell phone
alarm goes off, and after the buzzer, he hears his own voice (recorded the night before
using his custom alarm app) barking, “Dude—today’s the day, get your game on!” He
sits up, grabs his phone to check his messages, and notices an Amazon notification.
Looks like his new skateboard wheels got delivered last night—he’d better grab ’em



before they get swiped. He pops into the bathroom, hits the hot water to get it
warming up, and rushes out the front door to find the box of wheels. “Whew—they’re
still here.” He grabs the box, tosses it on the table, and makes for the bathroom. He
wipes the steam off the mirror and starts shaving—and starts rehearsing his
presentation. Again. (Hey, you can’t be too ready!) It’s a suit-and-tie day, so he just eats
an energy bar to avoid messing up the outfit, which gives him time to page through his
slides again. At 7:45, he jumps in the car and speed-dials Aron, the department admin.
No answer; it goes to voicemail. Again. “Aron, it’s Fritz—be sure the handouts are
printed and on the conference table by eleven, before the meeting starts. I’ll confirm
when I get in.” He hangs up and thinks, You can never be too careful with Aron—better
text as well to be sure the message gets through. It’s an important day, so Fritz splurges
and parks in the expensive lot in the basement of his building. En route to his desk, he
spies Aron, who sees him first and says, “Got your messages—all of them. The
handouts were ready last night. Happy now?”
“Yeah,” Fritz mutters as he heads to his office. “Thanks.”
He doesn’t see Aron shake his head in disgust behind him.
Now let’s try that again with Fritz still attending to his transactional-world
obligations but tapping into the flow world as well. At 6:30 a.m., his cell alarm goes
off, set to play his favorite song, “Perfect” by Ed Sheeran. He pauses to listen to it once
through before rolling out of bed—just long enough to relish the first time he and
Haley heard that song. Having read the research that says going outdoors first thing in
the day before going online can reduce anxiety and increase energy, he lets the song
finish, slides on his slippers, and walks out to grab a gulp of air. He takes a big sniff.
“Wow, the air tastes great at this hour.” He notices an Amazon package on the porch.
“Sweet—must be my new wheels,” he says, waving at a dog-walker as he scoops up the
box and heads inside. It’s a suit-and-tie day, so he makes breakfast before dressing, lest
he make a mess of himself. Scrambled eggs with a bit of yogurt on the side seems like
the right fuel for a big day like this. After breakfast, he heads to the bathroom and
starts shaving. He notices it’s kind of interesting how the feel of the razor changes with
each stroke as the water temperature rises. The goofy look of being covered in shaving
foam makes him smirk, which he recognizes in the mirror as the face that always made
his mom say, “That’s my boy! There’s my Fritzie!” He’ll have to call her tonight to tell
her how the big day went; she’d love that (and he would, too). After dressing, he grabs
his phone—no urgent messages—and heads to the car. He recorded his run-through
of the presentation last night, so he plays it back during the drive to groove it in his



mind one more time. It’s good. He’s got this. He parks in the usual lot and walks the
two blocks to the office, taking the path through the park. One of the trees is turning
colors, which sparks an idea for a transition metaphor he can easily add to spruce up
his talk. When he gets in, he passes Aron and asks, “We all good for today?”
“Absolutely,” Aron says. “Had it all wrapped up last night.”
“Well done again, man, thanks!”
“Hey, I’m getting a refresh on coffee—bring you one?”
“Definitely, much appreciated,” Fritz replies as he turns into his office.
The only difference between these two mornings is which world Fritz chooses to be
in and what mindsets he adopts when he gets there. These two very different morning
routines take the same amount of Fritz’s time but deliver very different experiences. In
the second, Fritz is still getting his job done—arguably, getting it done better and more
efficiently—but he’s also tapping into the flow world along the way. He’s getting more
out of his morning without having to cram more into it (and without giving anything
up in the bargain). He gets to enjoy a favorite song and a lovely memory. He starts the
day connecting to the real world and nature, not fixated on his phone, which enables
him to be fully engaged and calmly detached. He prepares carefully but not
compulsively, which leaves enough emotional availability to remember to care about
his mom (which will do them both good later that evening).
The all-transactional Fritz isn’t a bad guy. His morning was a complete success in
his eyes—it was a “normal” morning, and the feedback he got from it told him that it
worked just fine. He felt prepared, he took care of business, he made sure Aron did his
job, he avoided his wheels being stolen off the porch—all good, right? Yes. And no. Alltransaction Fritz literally doesn’t know what he’s missing, because he was never in the
flow world where what he’s missing lives. The sweet song, the mom memory, the dogwalker greeting, and most certainly Aron’s offer to get him a coffee were all entirely out
of view. That’s the reason we’re offering you this reframe and why we’re writing this
book. The transactional world is necessary and good, and we can’t live without it, but
it’s not the whole world, and it won’t evoke the whole you.
The transactional world is so self-referencing and self-contained that it will never,
on its own, remember to invite you to partake of the flow world. It just can’t. It’s so
preoccupied with getting things done that it will always direct every ounce of your
time and energy to more, better, faster outcomes. The first step to making more
meaningful moments in the flow world is recognizing that it’s there, then turning your
attention toward it.



The key difference here is you. You’re different when you orient yourself to one
world or the other. What you’re focused on, where your attention is directed, is
different. The primary task you are doing is different. The kind of action you’re taking
—whether your energies are largely outbound or inbound—is different. Your
awareness of and orientation to yourself and others is different. And how you play
your role in the world is different. This chart shows just how different they are. But
remember—as different as those lists are, these two worlds are always present. They are
very near to one another, and with time and practice, you’ll be able to move between
them easily and rather quickly.

Description 3

Another way to think about the chart above is that when you’re in the flow world,
you’re playing a different game. Which raises the question: What game is that?
We both admire the work of the modern philosopher James Carse. Carse was a
professor of history and the literature of religion at New York University, and his
seminal work was a little book titled Finite and Infinite Games. In it, he uses the
metaphor of “games” to characterize the whole set of rules, behaviors, and goals that
define and motivate different people’s approach to living life. By modelling life as a
game, Carse isn’t trivializing it; he’s merely applying a simplifying analogy to help
people understand their own lives and choose thoughtfully and intentionally how to
live into them.
Carse says there are two fundamentally different games people play: finite games
and infinite games. In finite games, you play by the rules in order to win. In infinite
games, you play with the rules in order to keep playing. We probably like his model
because it fits so well with ours. You play finite games in the transactional world, and
you play infinite games in the flow world.

[Embedded visual 1 on PDF page 76 (1115x462 px). OCR-readable text: [| transactions [Flow | / Action _ | _reaching-directing _| receiving-recognizing |]



Don’t Miss It
Dave got his first clear introduction to how different and yet how intimately
overlapping the transactional and flow worlds were when he was in his mid-twenties,
just two years out of college. Dave’s failed attempt to solve the energy crisis included a
brief stint with a start-up. Two forward-thinking oil company executives broke out of
their firm to start an “advanced energy strategy consultancy” to bring efficient power
generation innovation to heavy industry. They hired Dave as their bright, young
(meaning inexpensive) engineer to do the dirty work. The company was way ahead of
its time and ultimately failed, but they had one shining moment along the way.
Pacific Gas & Electric (PG&E), the Northern California electric utility company,
hired Dave’s firm to do a research project analyzing the potential for power
conservation at their biggest customers’ industrial sites. Dave helped prepare the
proposal and even got to be a part of the pitch presentation to the client. Minutes after
the meeting, when Dave and his boss Paul had just stepped out of PG&E headquarters
onto the sidewalk on Beale Street in downtown San Francisco, his boss got a call
informing them they had won the contract! They were both ecstatic—but not for the
same reasons.
Dave said, “Hey, this is great! If this contract goes well, then maybe PG&E will
endorse us to their industrial users, and if that happens, then maybe some of those
industrial users will ask us to overhaul their power conservation plans, and then
maybe, just maybe, we’ll get hired to actually design and install one of those new
cogeneration power units, and then we will really be doing it!”
Paul looked at him, a little confused, and replied, “Well… sure, that’d be great, Dave,
but that’s a lot of ifs and a long way off. What I got out of today was how fun it was to
be in that conversation with those guys and how gratifying it is that PG&E trusts us to
do this work. This is going to be a really fun project, and I can’t wait to get started!”
As soon as Paul said that, Dave stopped dead in his tracks on the sidewalk and just
stared straight ahead. He felt like someone had hit him in the head with a two-by-four.
It suddenly dawned on him that he and his boss had both just come out of the exact
same meeting in the exact same room with the exact same people saying the exact same
things, but they’d each had a totally different experience. Dave was stuck trying to win
the finite game of growing a bigger company. Paul wanted that, too, but his focus was
almost entirely on enjoying the chance to play the infinite game of doing some good
work with some good people. The only reason Dave was happy was because this new
contract put one little checkmark on the big project plan in his head. All that this



award meant to Dave was that the probability of hitting a strategic objective a year or
two from now (which never happened, by the way) might be a little more likely to
occur than it was a few hours ago.
Meanwhile, Paul was having a fabulous day right now. He’d had a great time giving
the presentation and enjoyed every bit of the meeting to the fullest. Even his
enthusiasm about the future was entirely different—he was looking forward to what
they were actually going to be doing, starting immediately. He wasn’t fantasizing about
some imagined far-off future. When Dave looked at Paul, he recognized that Paul was
on a different planet than he was—despite just being a few steps ahead of him on the
sidewalk. Dave didn’t have words for it at the time, but he was living in the
transactional world playing a finite game and Paul was living in the flow world playing
an infinite game—at the same time and place, and in the middle of the same
conversation.
It was clear to Dave that Paul was having a far better time in that other world than
Dave was having in his world. He wasn’t sure what to do about it then, but he knew he
had to figure out how to get more of what Paul was getting.
Mind Games
You may be asking if this construct of distinguishing between the flow world and the
transactional world is just that—a mental construct. Is it all just in our heads? Yes, of
course it is. The flow world and the transactional world are real worlds, and they are
also a perception-reframing tool that we recommend you use to give yourself a better
chance at designing a life with more meaning IN it.
As we’ve said before, design only works in reality, so good designers are realists. And
ultimately, there is just one all-inclusive world, or universe, or multiverse (we’re pretty
sure it’s a multiverse, but we digress). Whatever the whole cosmic enchilada really is,
it’s one reality. But our brains and bodies are far smaller than that totality, and we can’t
possibly perceive or understand it all. Furthermore, we have far more capacities to
think and observe and respond and move and act than we can possibly activate at one
time. So, in every moment of every day, we must pick and choose which of our many
powers to use and which little corner of reality we’re going to aim those powers at.
Neuroscience now knows that our brains are organized very efficiently to always
leverage as much of our huge but finite capacity as possible. One of the tricks the brain



uses to do that is pattern matching—noticing how things are familiar and similar to
what’s already in our memory banks so we don’t have to rethink them all over again.
Accordingly, we now know that we don’t really see what we’re looking at; we see
what we’re looking for. Let’s use that fact to our advantage for designing a more
meaningful life. By adopting practices and mindsets that enhance the frequency and
intensity of flow-world encounters, we can re-habituate our transaction-oriented
brains to be more receptive to and better recognize the flow world and the rich
possibilities that await us there. This conceptual construct of the transactional world
and the flow world is simply a tool that helps us orient our brains in such a way to
optimize access to different kinds of experiences. The transactional and flow worlds are
both part of reality, and they’re both present everywhere all the time, but we’re not
equally aware of them. Each one corresponds to a different brain mode, which we can
learn to master.
Two Brains Are Better Than One
Many neuroscientists are exploring how the brain functions and what various
networks of neurons actually do. New insights are revealing more about how our
brains and bodies work together, almost every day. Particularly relevant for our
purposes is the research of Lisa Miller, a tenured professor and director of the Clinical
Psychology Program at Teachers College, Columbia University. Dr. Miller is also the
founder and director of the Spirituality Mind Body Institute, the first Ivy League
graduate program and research institute in spirituality and psychology. In her latest
book, The Awakened Brain, Dr. Miller identifies two distinct ways or modalities in
which our brains work, which she calls the “achieving brain” and the “awakened
brain.”
The achieving brain is just that—it optimizes our ability to organize people and
resources, accomplish tasks, and achieve results. The achieving brain is running just
about everything in the modern world. But because it’s focused on trying to control
lots of moving parts and people—things not easily controlled—the achieving brain is
also anxious, restless, judgmental, and rarely, if ever, satisfied.
Sounds like a brain focused in the transactional world to us.
The awakened brain is focused more on the present moment, relationships, and
emotional well-being. It exhibits higher levels of neurotransmitters like oxytocin and
serotonin, which are linked to feelings of love, compassion, and well-being. Dr. Miller



observes that regardless of their religious or spiritual orientation, people operating out
of their awakened brains experience a sense of connectedness and a form of spirituality.
Sounds like a brain fully in the flow world to us.
We both use both our brains all the time, and wouldn’t leave home without either
of them. In fact, Dave has had this poem by Rudyard Kipling over his desk for more
than forty years.



Description 4

[Embedded visual 1 on PDF page 81 (638x866 px). OCR-readable text: Something I owe to the soil that grew, / More to the life that fed. / But most to Allah who gave me two / Separate sides of my head. / I would go without shirt or shoes, / Friends, tobacco, or bread / Sooner than for an instant lose / Either side of my head.]



What we’re observing in today’s accelerating sense of meaninglessness is that far too
many people have lost one side of their heads. They have far too little access to their
awakened brains, and it’s costing them dearly. If that’s you, please know we have
designed ways for you to get into the flow world, where you can get the awakened side
of your head back. After all—two heads are better than one, two brains are better than
one, and two worlds are definitely better than one.
How to Be in Two Places at Once
Now that we’ve introduced you to the transactional and flow worlds, the last thing we
want is for you to think that you have to choose between them. That’s a terrible idea,
but it’s what lots of highly regarded advice givers are telling you all the time. The
efficiency experts and the management consultants will give you lots of advice and
countless smartphone apps to help you be a superstar performer in the transactional
world. Meanwhile, the meditation specialists and yoga gurus and retreat center
directors can’t wait to help you disconnect from all the noise and drop deep down to
get in touch with the flow world. They all mean well, and their advice and tools and
techniques work, but unfortunately, most of their advice expects you to be totally
engaged in just one world or the other, and to stay there for extended periods of time
so that you can make progress and build momentum.
We completely understand their rationale. Many of the exercises in this book are
designed to be done just while in the flow world, and there are good reasons for that,
which we’ll explain in the coming chapters. But no one can live in just the flow world,
and while lots of people are living in just the transactional world, they’re not happy
there. So, we have to find a way to live in both worlds. And that means getting good at
moving back and forth between them, and it means learning how to be in both at the
same time (or almost). And that brings us to one of our favorite design thinking tips
and tricks: Set the bar low and clear it.
The problem with all this high-performance transaction stuff and all this superdeep flow stuff is it sets the bar too high. We’re not designing tools for Olympic
