transactionalists or Oscar-winning flow masters. We’re designing for regular people
with regular lives who have two tons of transactions to get through every day—one ton
at work and another ton at home with a nasty commute to transact in between.
Remember that prototype iteration is central to being a designer. A good prototype is
quick and easy and helps us learn something as we try it. “Set the bar low and clear it”



is just our simple way of reminding people to do good design. So, we want to help you
set the bar low and clear it when designing for meaning, too.
Sure, some of our tools are All-Flow-All-the-Time tools—like Put on Your Wonder
Glasses or the Seventh-Day Reflection. And some are quick wins that you can grab on
the fly—like the Coherency Sightings Log (chapter 5) or Take a Sniffari (chapter 6).
But in the long run, what we’re inviting you to do is simply recognize both worlds and
be attentive to tapping into a little more flow world when you can. The goal is just to
be more like the second version of Fritz than the first version of Fritz. You don’t
actually need an exercise or a technique for this at all. You just need to have both your
heads available when you need them.
For instance, during a staff meeting.
You know what a staff meeting looks like (even if you’ve never been to one, you’ve
seen scores of them in the movies). Nine people gather around a table in a conference
room. There’s a printed agenda with about 118 minutes of stuff that we’re supposed
to get through in an hour. Alice, the manager, arrives six minutes late, fuming from the
exec staff meeting she just came from where she learned the team’s budget got cut—
again. And so, the meeting starts. Some announcements, some status updates from
various members of the team. One big, serious discussion about how the new offering
from our big competitor may change things for our company (or store, or nonprofit,
or soccer team). If you just drift along with the crowd, you’ll have a totally normal
transactional experience. You’ll be interested in what matters to you, and bored and
annoyed when the meeting seems to waste your time on trivialities or things that don’t
concern you. You’ll take note of who’s gaining ground and who’s falling behind. Then
you’ll get back to work. All good transactional observations. You’re good at this. You
know how the game is played.
You can keep doing it that way, and nothing will go wrong. You’ll be an effective
member of your team and things will just keep going the way that things go. Or… you
can change it up. During those many moments in any staff meeting when not much is
happening and your full attention isn’t required, you can shift into the flow world. All
you have to do is stop and look. Now, in order to “look,” you have to know what
you’re looking for, which in this case is the flow world. But just how do we stop seeing
the thing we’re currently seeing (the transactional world) and suddenly notice this
other world (the flow world) that a minute ago was apparently invisible to us?
Good question. Here’s a shortcut to help you recognize which world is which and
make the shift between them when you want to. The transactional world is the world of



intent. It’s all about what you and others are trying to accomplish—what you intend to
have happen. Therefore, everything is seen solely through its relationship to aiding or
impeding your effort to produce that outcome. The flow world is the world of is. It’s all
about what is actually happening right now—regardless of future (or past)
implications thereof. Therefore, everything is seen just for what it is. In the
transactional world, things only have instrumental value—based on how they
contribute to your goals. In the flow world, things have intrinsic value—based on what
they are. So, the key to shifting into the flow world is to shift from seeing the world as
you’re intending it to be and seeing it just as it is (and over time, growing the capacity
to see just what is—more and more fully and deeply).
So, let’s get back to our staff meeting and see if we can pull this off in real life. As
you’re sitting there at the conference table next to your colleagues, for just a moment,
stop thinking about whatever “next thing” is currently being discussed (like the budget
change or the revised advertising campaign) and look at what’s in front of you in this
particular moment and in this particular place with these particular people. It can be as
simple as changing the question on the screen in your head from “What’s Next?” to
“What’s Happening Right Now?”
You ask yourself, What’s happening? and then, with a quick scan of the room, any
one of the following observations could instantly transport you to the flow world:
Wow—that tree in the parking lot has amazing fall colors.
Something is really bothering Susan today. She’s unhappy—no, she’s scared.
The team isn’t upset by the budget cut, but the boss doesn’t realize that—if only
she knew that we know we can deal with it—but everyone’s just complaining.
Your awakened brain can recognize these three things going on in the flow world
right in front of you in just a few seconds—far less time than it took to read them.
Each of these observations represents a different kind of invitation to engage in the
flow world. Responding to the tree is the easiest. All you have to do is receive its beauty
and enjoy it. Don’t be critical that you don’t have time to sit under it all morning and
ponder the universe—that’s transactional thinking. Just let that little ray of joy from
the tree zip right into your soul and warm you with the reminder that in nature,
beauty is plentiful and unstoppable—how great is that?
Susan is another matter. You have a number of options here. You don’t really know
what’s going on with her, and you don’t want to be presumptuous, so perhaps best to
just drop it. Even if you do only that—simply allow yourself to care about her for a



moment, to have some sympathy for whatever it is she’s in the midst of, then let it go—
you are being more human in that moment, and that’s a more meaningful way to live.
Or you could decide to speak to her later that day. You won’t make a big deal of it, but
you’ll keep an eye out for a moment when you might mention, “Hey, during staff
today, you seemed a bit down or maybe a little stressed. Everything okay with you?”
That approach leaves her plenty of room to skip it. (“Oh no, everything’s fine. I’ve just
had it with these constant budget changes.”) It also gives Susan the chance to connect
more meaningfully with someone at work if she chooses to. Either way, you’ve
designed a few more flow-world moments and done so at almost no cost to you
whatsoever.
The last observation—your sudden sense that everyone on the team is ready to step
up to dealing with the budget cut and they have the boss’s back, but the boss doesn’t
know that—is one of those situations where the flow world just might invade the
transactional world. If that was your intuition, you could hold it for a little while and
just keep scanning the room to see if the intuition persists. If it does, and the
opportunity presented itself, you could speak up and say, “You know, Alice, I agree
that this round of budget cuts isn’t necessary, but we’re stuck with it. And I, for one,
am confident we can find a way to recalibrate our projects without any devastating
impacts. I’m curious, how does everyone else feel?” Then you wait and see how people
respond. If your intuition was right, others will chime in supportively like you,
potentially resulting in a huge act of support for Alice that could change not only her
demeanor that day but how the whole team experience may feel for the next few weeks.
Yes, there’s some risk involved in speaking up this way, but it’s not a big risk. And if
you take that risk and it succeeds, your flow-world observations could become a
catalyst to improving life in the transactional world. If you’re unsure about speaking
up, then don’t take the risk. There’s no harm done by just observing carefully. But do
let yourself ask the question What’s happening? and do make those flow-world
observations if only to allow you to have some more meaningful moments during that
meeting or any meeting.
This simple, pedestrian example shows that it is quite doable to design a way to be
in two places at once, to navigate both the transactional and the flow worlds at
(almost) the same time with just a bit of well-directed attention and intention. The
staff meeting won’t take any longer. The budget cuts will still be dealt with (and very
likely dealt with more effectively). You can get more out of a staff meeting without
having to cram more into it—and it’s surprisingly easy to do.



WARNING: The Practice-to-Production Trap
Lots of perfectly good practices aimed at helping people access flow-world experiences
are being subtly co-opted by the transactional world. The two most common examples
are the twenty-minute morning meditation sit and the evening gratitude exercise. The
morning meditation practice is intended to help you get centered and at peace to help
sustain your ongoing commitment to being your better self and to enter the day
generatively. The evening gratitude exercise (listing three things you’re grateful for at
the end of each day) is intended to help you be more aware of the good things in your
life and to set your mind in a positive posture as you go to sleep, so you will rest well
and rise in a generous frame of mind. These are terrific practices, evolved from wisdom
traditions going back millennia and affirmed by modern positive psychology. They
provide great benefit to millions daily.
So, what’s the problem? The problem is that it’s incredibly easy to let them become
just another transaction—but entering the flow world should never land on your to-do
list. Since the transactional world is up and running all the time and everywhere, it
tends to be pretty imperialistic. It will volunteer to take over almost anything. It does
that with good intentions; it’s just trying to do a good job of running the world and
creating lots of good outcomes.
Transactional-world thinking looks at a flow-world experience and immediately
assesses what beneficial results or outcomes reside there. It wants to maximize those
results. Before you know it, your flow-world activity is no longer focused on fully
participating in the present moment; it’s working toward better performance and
better results. All you have to do is let your orientation shift from the present moment
experience (the actual meditation itself or the actual day of your gratitude reflection) to
the future results or performance. When your flow-world practice slides surreptitiously
into a transactional-world performance, it sounds like this:
Well, that was a good meditation, but I gotta find a way to get to that deeper place
faster.
This gratitude list is okay, but I think yesterday’s list was better.
The peace from my meditation didn’t last past lunch today—I guess I need to up it
to thirty minutes and work harder at getting more peaceful.
That last one really tells the story.



What’s happening there is what was intended to be a practice subtly morphed into a
production. The minute you start worrying about outcome is the exact moment you
have landed in the transactional world.
Earlier, we described a practice as a never-ending prototype, where the goal is the
doing of it—not the result or the outcome. Our achieving brains, spurred by the huge
momentum of the incredibly demanding transactional world, are infinitely clever at
transforming anything into an outcome-oriented production—a transaction. Flow
world–related practices (like meditation and the gratitude exercise) are intrinsically
beneficial by their very doing, and doing again, and doing again. Transactional
outcomes are instrumentally valuable by their completion contributing to a desired
goal or result. When you’re meditating just to put enough mindfulness in your tank to
get you through your stressful day, you’re turning your meditation into a transaction.
You’re just stopping at the mindfulness gas station on the way to work, or the good
dreams gas station on your way to bed.
The flow world is not the fuel to propel you through the transactional world.
Doing flow-world things in a transactional way will “work” in terms of pursuing
your transactional goals and will still provide some flow-world benefits, but it won’t
bring you the full meaning and aliveness it could if you kept those practices in the flow
world, in the particular moments where they belong. It’s a subtle struggle. Of course
we want to grow in our ability and capacity for living into the flow world more, but be
aware (and beware) of letting the transactional world steal your flow-world experiences
and practices. And when it happens to you (and it will—we all do it), be kind to
yourself and just start again.
Let’s Go
So there you have it—two different worlds in which to operate (where you can play
two different games using your two different brains) and that give you a whole bunch
of new territory in which to design more meaning. Combined with a reframed
perspective on what meaning is (far more than just making an impact) and a powerful
set of six designer’s mindsets, you are ready to start designing.
In the next four chapters, we will illustrate how to get into the flow world and make
moments rich with meaning with specific design exercises and practices you can do
immediately. Those four areas are:
Wonder



Coherence
Flow
Formative community
Designing for more meaning in our lives while learning to navigate between two
worlds doesn’t mean we aren’t going to struggle or have to work hard to pay our bills
or provide for our families. People who experience meaningful lives still get laid off.
They fall in love, get divorced, and fall in love again. They get sick, they lose friends
and family to cancer and disease, just like everyone.
But for the most part, these folks experience meaning and purpose in the day-to-day
process of life. They have found ways to access the flow world while still getting done
what needs getting done in the transactional world.
And if they can do it, so can you.
TRY STUFF
We’ll be providing more exercises in the coming chapters. The big idea in this chapter is all
about developing the ability to both recognize and engage with both the transactional and
flow worlds. Below is a simple tool that can help you to do just what those stories illustrate.
Exercise: Flip the World Switch
The idea of this exercise is simply to attune our attention to the two worlds and to improve
our facility in moving between them. It should be easy, as both the flow and transactional
worlds are present at all times. Since our goal in “flipping the switch” is just to practice our
two-world awareness, it’s best to pick a situation or a moment that’s simple and
uncomplicated.
The Steps
Select the situation in which you’re going to flip the world switch.
Enter that situation in your usual transactional orientation, but consciously ready to flip at
some point.
Notice carefully what the transaction is and how it’s going.
Partway into the experience, say FLIP! loudly in your mind (silently in the room).
Immediately look for something in the flow world in the present moment that can hold
your attention.
Attend to that and that alone for ten seconds while still doing what you’re doing, with no
visible change being noticeable to any bystander.



Then again say FLIP! loudly in your mind (and silently in the room).
Refocus on what’s next in the transactional world.
Continue on your way.
Congratulate yourself for being a world flipper.
Optional: Later—after the situation has passed—reflect on “What was that like?”
Example: Preparing Dinner
Walking into the kitchen where your partner is starting dinner, you offer, “How can I help?”
Your partner says, “Why don’t you get everything together for the salad?”
You say okay to your partner and I’m going to FLIP this to yourself.
You get the lettuce, tomatoes, and Parmesan cheese out of the fridge and start working.
You notice that you have to decide what to cut up first, where to put the pile of each salad
component, what ratio of lettuce to tomatoes, and wonder what kind of dressing will go
well with these elements.
You decide tearing up lettuce is step 1 but are so distracted by the dressing issue that
you drop the head of lettuce on the floor.
While stooping to pick up the lettuce, you say FLIP! and look for the flow world.
You see the lettuce in your hands and are amazed at how the leaves fold into one another
and how subtly the green color changes from tip to stem. “Whoa—who knew lettuce was
beautiful!?”
Your partner asks, “How’s it going over there, honey?”
You say FLIP! to yourself and say, “Great! I was just noticing how pretty lettuce really is.
It’s so pretty it deserves the right dressing, don’t you think? What should we use?”
Partner: “Huh—yeah, it is pretty. How about just oil and vinegar, then we can actually taste
the lettuce for once.”
You: “Cool. Done.”
You congratulate yourself on not just finishing the salad but enjoying its beauty as well.

OceanofPDF.com



4

The Wonder Formula
We are all born naturally curious. According to the psychologist Edward Deci,
curiosity is an intrinsic motivation—humans are curious animals just for the joy
of it. This was certainly true when we were children. The world was full of
curiosities, and curiosity is how we learned to understand and navigate the
world. We were curious about everything—about ants and worms, stars and
planets, and about all the things you notice when going for a walk in the woods.
But then school happened, and we learned that curiosity and asking too many
questions got us sent to the principal’s office. And, in our middle and high
school years, the social pressure to fit in and be like the other kids meant that we
stopped asking questions entirely.
Somehow curiosity became something only for children; something we grow
out of.
Unless we don’t.
Curiosity—one of the core mindsets we explored in Designing Your Life—
does more than just spark interest. It nourishes health, optimism, and a hopeful
stance toward the world. Push curiosity far enough and it evolves into something
more powerful. Follow its thread long enough and reality will stop you in your
tracks, astonishing you with the sheer mystery of it all.
When mystery finally breaks through, it does more than intrigue us—it fills
us with wonder. Wonder is a unique sensation of aliveness, the intuitive
knowing that we’re part of something far larger than we can grasp. Leaning into
our encounter with the mysterious (which is invariably found in the flow world)



releases the feeling of wonder. And the payoff is bigger than momentary delight:
Wonder recasts the very search for meaning, hinting that life holds possibilities
still unfolding just beyond our sight.
Exactly what those possibilities are depends on a million different factors, but
the important thing is this: You do not need to understand something to let it
transform you.
The bad news is that dismissing or denying mystery will constrain you to a
world that is smaller and less wonderful than it could be. When you let yourself
overlook mystery by getting caught up in trying to understand it—or worse yet,
by trying to demystify it into something small enough that you can explain it—
you miss out on a powerful potential source of personal discovery and meaningmaking. And while denying mystery has become a popular postmodern stance,
and some people seek to simplify their lives by simply denying that mystery
exists, we think that cultivating receptivity to mystery is a generative way to
frame our common human experience.
Years ago, Dave was having a conversation with Lawrence—one of his
consulting clients who was a self-described very smart and scientifically minded
person.
Lawrence: If it can’t be accurately quantified and empirically measured, it
doesn’t exist.
Dave: Really? That’s quite a statement. You sure about that?
Lawrence: Absolutely—anything else is just nonsense.
Dave: What about love? We can know it, and we universally value it, but
we can’t empirically measure it.
Lawrence: Doesn’t exist. It’s just your imagination.
Dave: Oh, now that you mention it, we can’t measure imagination either.
How about that?
Lawrence: Now you’re just bothering me.
Lawrence had made a choice to deny anything in his experience that he
couldn’t rationalize and understand, and because of that, he doesn’t allow
mystery to be part of his everyday life experience. He pays no attention to it. And



that is unfortunate, because Lawrence is living in a diminished world—a
disenchanted world where he has allowed his curiosity to atrophy, and mystery
has been buried under a mountain of rationalizations. And because his rational
mind, the mind that talks all the time, is the only form of intelligence that he has
access to (emotional, kinesthetic, and intuitive intelligences are some of the
others), he has cut off the possibility of recognizing any other forms of
information, rational or nonrational, that are available in this universe, all the
time. In addition to living a diminished life, Lawrence has stopped learning.
We want to be exceptionally clear here: We are not saying you have to believe
in anything supernatural or weird in order to experience mystery. Bill’s
worldview is mostly a scientific one; he was a physics major for a time in college,
and some think science is the ultimate anti-mystery point of view. Yet it doesn’t
take much study of biology before you come across the mystery of life emerging
from a primordial soup of chemicals. Or see the magic of the human stem cell—
able to self-renew and, under the right cues, give rise to every specialized cell type
that builds the organs of the human body. Modern physics and cosmology, aided
by supercolliders and the James Webb Space Telescope, has revealed a universe
even more mysterious than we ever imagined. Supermassive black holes,
quantum uncertainty, particle superposition, and quantum entanglement—
which Einstein called “spooky action at a distance”—it’s all kinds of crazy
mysterious. The essential fabric of the world is nutty and wonderful at the same
time. It turns out that science, as it generates verifiable knowledge, also creates
the next frontiers of mystery, so we are never without the next question, “Why?”
Enter with an Open Mind
To let a little mystery into your life, you just need to be honest with yourself,
trust your perception, including the perceptions and feelings you can’t explain,
and adopt the designer’s mindset of wonder when experiencing the world. Once
you have that orientation to your experiences, you will probably conclude that
you have already felt things that are mysterious. Things you know are true but
are beyond your capacity to explain.



When we encounter something we know to be real but can’t understand, as
designers, we don’t push it away—we embrace it. Rather than dismissing our
perception as “false” or “in error,” designers approach the mystery of the human
condition with an open mind. We don’t interrogate the experience or try to
define it. By definition, you can’t get a lot of information, at least rational,
verifiable information about a mystery. Instead, when you are thinking like a
designer, you learn to appreciate and engage with the mysterious, adopting an
attitude of letting the in-the-moment mystery just Be.
When we lean into mystery with curiosity, we activate the possibility of a
larger and more meaningful learning experience, and we evoke the Wonder
Formula.
Curiosity + Mystery = Wonder
Moments of wonder are everywhere once you know what to look for. They
are always found in the particular moment, scandalous as that may be, and
frequently act as a gateway from the transactional world to the flow world. And
these moments of wonder, also called awe and transcendence, have been regarded
and recorded in the human experience throughout history.
We regularly encourage our students to “pursue latent wonderfulness” as
they leave Stanford and head out into the world. We define latent wonderfulness
as “the 20 percent chance that something in a new experience might lead to
learning, joy, or wonder.” Radically accepting the world the way it is
(remembering always that accepting is not the same as endorsing) and having the
readiness of your availability tuned to wonder’s frequency can reap big
dividends. It is one of the most dependable paths to becoming more fully alive.
Many modern historians have commented on the similarities that are found
in the world’s oldest myths and religions. In most traditions, one story shows up
again and again—ordinary people report having surprisingly similar experiences
of transcendental, out-of-body moments of wonder. In these rare but powerful
moments, there is always a heightened sense of awareness, of the availability to
wonder. In this elevated state of wonder, people often describe a sensation of the
universe as one, unified, living thing in which the boundaries between



themselves and everything else dissolves into thin air. This experience of oneness
evokes a deep feeling of connection to nature and all people everywhere.
A profound feeling of wonder.
It doesn’t seem to matter what belief system or culture you come from—the
stories of experiencing awe and wonder are everywhere. Different cultures and
traditions wrap this experience in varied explanations and metaphors, but there
seems to be something in the evolution of human consciousness that allows for
these experiences. And they’re hardly rare.
In a 2023 Pew Research Center survey study of more than eleven thousand
Americans, 83 percent say they believe that we have a soul or a spirit, and 45
percent of people feel a “strong connection with something beyond this world.”
Thirty percent say they have “personally encountered” a spirit. And perhaps
even more significant is the fact that 46 percent of those surveyed feel a deep
sense of connection to an encounter of wonder at the unfathomable mystery of
the universe.
Since this data comes from a US population, steeped in the Judeo-Christian
tradition, the cultural explanation for these mysterious experiences is “spirit and
spiritual.” The same data interpreted in the Buddhist tradition might substitute
the Tao, or the Way, for the term spirit. Animistic religions, shamanism, and
other Indigenous people’s worldviews might substitute nature or Gaia for spirit.
No matter your culture or tradition, thousands of years of recorded human
experience confirm a pervasive sense that reality is far richer than what meets the
eye—and that humans have available to them the experience of transcendence
and wonder.
Recent neuroscience research highlights the benefits of experiencing awe and
wonder. Studies using fMRI show that awe promotes a sense of connection to
the world, reducing feelings of self-importance and fostering altruistic behaviors.
These findings suggest that nurturing awe can improve mental health and wellbeing. Awe also stimulates the parasympathetic nervous system, reducing stress.
Additionally, awe can increase oxytocin release, fostering social bonding and a
sense of interconnectedness.
Our conclusion is that this state of mind, in response to experiences of
wonder evoked by mystery, must somehow have evolved in our species and is a



powerful way to experience more than just the transactional world. And if
wonder/awe/transcendence (Maslow’s ultimate top of the pyramid, where
meaning is found) are good for us, the question is: How do we make more
wonder in our everyday reality? How does the wonder mindset work in real life,
and are there reliable ways to get more of it?
Remember that wonder is connected to the scandal of particularity—wonder
occurs in singular moments of intense observation. And wonder helps facilitate
the shift from the transactional world to the flow world. Therefore, in order to
experience more wonder in our lives, we must be willing to let go of the
transactions we are in the middle of, for just a moment, and pay close attention
to what is available right now.
We live in a difficult time when it comes to our attention. It feels like
everything, and particularly our devices and screens, want all our attention all
the time. Some have called this the “attention economy” because our phones and
particularly social media apps are carefully designed to keep us hooked and
staying on the screen just a little bit longer. Even worse, social media apps
monetize your attention and sell your data while they keep you scrolling,
clicking, and paying attention to their feeds, whether it is good for you or not.
These apps are insidious—who hasn’t found themselves going to sleep an hour
later than intended after scrolling through countless videos, TikToks, or Reels
that will never be remembered in the morning?
It will take some discipline and focus if we are going to find wonder in a
world of ubiquitous attention theft. But it is possible.
For instance, let’s say that you’ve decided to practice the mindset of wonder,
so you go outside to a little piece of nature, perhaps a garden. Gardens are
perfect for attentive observation. Packed with diverse life, they offer a target-rich
setting where close study of nature becomes a multidimensional, multisensory,
and truly wondrous experience. In that same Pew Research study, nearly half of
Americans said they believe nature carries its own spirits or spiritual energy. So,
sitting in a comfortable outdoor chair, you might first look at the garden with
the question “What do I need to do here?” and a list of actions pops up almost
without effort. “I need to pull those weeds over there, I need to deadhead those
Shasta daisies, it’s time to fertilize the roses,” and so on and so on. When you



look at the garden this way, what you see is a series of actions and transactions
that need doing. And notice that all the tasks are projections of yourself into a
future that hasn’t happened yet. The weeds will get pulled in the future; the roses
will get tended to in the future. Notice that you are spending very little time in
the present moment in the garden with this transactional mindset.
But let’s say we adopt a mindset of curiosity. What changes? First, our
perception of things starts to change. “Look at that California poppy over there.
I didn’t plant that; I wonder where it came from? And how does it make such an
intense color? There is just one daffodil left. I wonder why it bloomed so late?
There are spots on the rose petals; (looking closer) there are aphids all over the
leaves, so that must be the cause, (looking closer), but there are three ladybugs
on the stem. I think they like to eat aphids… and I wonder what eats ladybugs…”
And so on.
When you are curious, you don’t tend to see things to do; you just notice
things as they are. When you get really good at curiosity, the tendency to turn
your observations into tasks evaporates and you are confronted with the
intrinsic is-ness of the world. The volunteer poppy is beautiful, the color so rich
that it is astonishing, and the circle of life created by the rose-aphid-ladybug
system is amazing. And you notice that all these observations are about things in
the garden as they stand, now! The poppy is beautiful—now—the process of bug
life and death is occurring—now! Your attention has moved from tasks to be
completed in the future to the here and now. Your curiosity has brought you into
the moment, and you begin to shift from the transactional world to the flow
world.
Then let’s say you decide to go deeper, past curiosity and into the world
perceived through the lens of wonder. You notice the amazing spiral pattern of
petals on the rose you were looking at. You notice that it’s not just one color of
red, that the word red is just a stand-in for the more complete and complex
experience of the rose, the petals, and the pink-red-burgundy mélange of redness
the rose contains. You marvel at the geometric perfection of overlapping rose
petals and contemplate the mystery of the unfolding, from bud to fully deployed
rose-ness, and wonder at the energies and processes that make it happen. You are
now fully present in the infinite moment of now, all because of this simple and



infinitely complicated rose. It’s scandalous, because it’s this particular rose, on
this particular day, and no other moment will be this moment.
After a moment, you pull back to “reality” and notice that you have spent a
timeless moment in flow, marveling at the mystery of the beauty contained in a
single rose. You notice how different that in-the-moment experience of wonder
felt—how time seemed to stop, and how your attention was clear and focused
on the experience of beauty. That moment of wondrous beauty became
suddenly available to you the instant you decided to pay attention to it. And,
unlike the mindless night-scrolling in bed, you will later remember it and
perhaps even be able to re-create and savor it.
A key part of the experience is letting go of our labels—the quick words we
use as shorthand for objects and experiences—and truly seeing what’s in front of
us. Robert Irwin, one of Bill’s favorite artists, had a biography written about him
titled Seeing Is Forgetting the Name of the Things One Sees. That is how designers
look at things—they see beyond the words and labels that we commonly use to
describe things. Neuroscience has long known that our brains, to be more
efficient, are powerful pattern-matching machines. The brain quickly tries to
recognize what you’re seeing as something for which it already has a memory or
a model, so it can move on to do other things. When we recognize and apply a
name to something—“Oh, it’s a rose”—we stop attending to it, which means we
stop observing it. To see something deeply and fully in a way that will evoke its
potential wonder, we have to get past the names of things and really see them as
they are beyond their label. It is with this Designer’s Way of seeing that we can
fully enter the flow of direct experience.
That’s why we say that the mindset of wonder is always available—and the
flow world is always here, humming just beneath everyday awareness. By seeing
directly what is in front of us, without labels, we tap into the flow world. By
acknowledging the mystery of nature, we take a stance, we see and start paying
attention to a world that is more generative, more alive, and more life-giving.
Who wouldn’t want this experience of more wonder in their lives?
The painter Robert Henri in his book The Art Spirit describes this attentive
and appreciative way of engaging the world as attaining “that marvelous state
that makes art inevitable.” Agreeing with Robert and taking his idea one step



further, we believe that all of us—not just artists—are invited by wonder to that
marvelous state that makes meaning inevitable.
Bottom line: When you encounter a mystery and the chance to catch the
wonder it unleashes, when you are invited to that marvelous state that makes
meaning inevitable—whatever you do, don’t miss it.
TRY STUFF
EXERCISE: Put on Your Wonder Glasses
The American author and playwright Henry Miller said, “I have a theory that the
moment one gives close attention to anything, even a blade of grass, it becomes a
mysterious, awesome, indescribably magnificent world in itself. I have tried this
experiment a thousand times and I have never been disappointed.”
The crucial line in this quote is “I have tried this experiment a thousand times and I
have never been disappointed.” This is seeing like a designer (or a writer—who designs
with words) with an activated wonder mindset. And Miller is right—something
wondrous awaits you anytime and anyplace when you enter into the flow world with
mystery-appreciating curiosity.
It’s this Designer’s Way perspective that opens up the bevy of wonder-filled
opportunities lying in wait for us, just around the corner in the flow world. What you
want to do is train yourself to find these moments of wonder in all the little nooks and
corners of life. By the way, if we deeply practice the mindset of availability, we
sometimes experience peak moments of transcendent wonder, really big aha
moments. Combine this with fully engaged and calmly detached and you might reach
a state of alternative consciousness known in Taoism as wu wei. Wu wei is often
translated as either “nonaction,” but more accurately, it represents a state of
“effortless action” or “action without force.” This is the state of perfect balance and
harmony with the world. Christians might call this a state of grace—living in alignment
with divine purpose, not striving. Whatever we call these altered states, and they are
terrific if you have one, they’re fairly rare—even for a well-trained designer.
We’ve designed this exercise to help you see wonder-inducing mystery all over the
place. Remember that the transactional world and flow world are just versions of very
different and available modes of perception, both depending on whether we’re
operating out of our achieving brains (that see the transactional world) or our
awakened brains (that see the flow world). The truth, of course, is that there is only
one reality happening at any one time, and transactions and flow sit side by side in our
experience. But we can’t perceive everything all at once, so our brains help us out by
defaulting to the achieving, transactional brain—it makes life more manageable in the
day-to-day world. The problem is that modern life is so driven by the transactional
mind that we need tricks and tools to make the flow-world experience more available.
The point of this exercise is to provide a readily accessible route to the flow world
to allow us to enjoy the mindset of wonder whenever we want to. Therefore, this is an
exercise in noticing what is going on all around you, all the time. To do these exercises
well, you need to be in a quiet, enjoyable place, perhaps a comfortable room in your



house—or better yet, out in nature, in a place where you feel safe. We are going to use
our imagination to observe and notice the world through three different sets of
glasses, labeled NORMAL, CURIOSITY, and WONDER.
Step 0—Get Set: Pick a time and place where you can spend a few uninterrupted
minutes observing the world around you. A quiet place where you won’t be interrupted
is good; a place with a lot of stuff around, a visually complex space, is great. It can be
indoors or outside, but in our experience, this exercise works a little better somewhere
in nature. The random complexity you see in nature helps avoid distraction (no cell
phones, please) and engage your perception more fully.
Step 1—Put on Your Normal Glasses: With your Normal Glasses on, survey what’s
in front of you, holding the question “What do I need to do here?” in mind (i.e., look
with your normal transactional perspective to figure out what problems or
opportunities you notice in the scene). Don’t make any decisions; just notice what
possible actions come to mind and let a to-do list form in your mind as you do so.
After you complete your scan, close your eyes and notice the to-do list that your brain
has formed.
Spend two to three minutes with those Normal Glasses on, noticing and making
lists, and then put the list you made to the side and say to yourself, “Gosh, I sure am
good at coming up with things to do! But I don’t have to actually do any of these
things, at least not right now. So I’m going to put this list away, stop thinking about
things to do in the future, and take another look to see what I may have missed.”
Step 2—Put on Your Curiosity Glasses: With the curiosity of your three-year-old-self
mind, put on your Curiosity Glasses, open your eyes, and scan the scene again, this
time asking, “Hmmm, what more is there to see? What draws my attention and
curiosity?” When we change our focus to curiosity, we are shifting our attention to the
possibility that there is more going on than meets the eye.
In step 1, we let our achieving brains do what they love to do—make lists and
catalog actions—but we don’t want to get stuck there. Now we are using curiosity to
activate our awakened brains and our ability to perceive complexity. As you scan the
scene this time, you look for things that are interesting, things that might be more than
they appear. Don’t linger too long on any one thing—just see what little details spark
your curiosity.
It might take some practice to get your curiosity going full speed. That’s okay; we
are building a mental muscle, and that takes repetition and effort, just like working
out. After scanning the scene for a while, pick out one or two things that provoke your
curiosity, provoke a sense of “Huh, I’m curious—how does that work?” or “That’s so
beautiful—what makes it look like that?” Once you have spent three to five minutes
being curious, you are ready for the next step.
Step 3—Put on Your Wonder Glasses: Now put on your Wonder Glasses, the ones
with the bright green frames and thick lenses, and pick one of the things your curiosity
noticed. Land your gaze on that one thing and let it rest there a moment while asking,
“There’s something wonderful here—what is it?” Keep looking at it with the expectation
of wonderfulness until it appears, and when it does, hold it in appreciation and
amazement. You’ve now fully entered into the flow world, gazing into the present
moment deeply, and ready to find the wonder that is right there, waiting to be noticed
and appreciated. There is no action item or follow-up (no pending transaction) to do at
this point. Just let the experience rise and fall on its own.



If you wish to make a journal entry about the experience later, to be able to look
back on it and savor the moment and the emotional nature of the experience, that
might be helpful. Studies show that reflection practices like journaling help develop
our sensitivity to deeply emotional experiences.
That’s it! That is all you have to do. If you got through steps 1 and 2 but didn’t quite
get to step 3 and Wonder, that’s okay. Try it again twenty-four hours later or whenever
you find yourself in a better environment, a better place. Eventually, you will be able to
shift into a wonder mindset in any environment, and in any place, distracting or not.
In the Living Room with Dave
The Wonder Glasses exercise is surprisingly easy to do. Here’s how Dave does it—
putting on his Wonder Glasses while sitting in his living room.
Step 0—Set Up: Dave sits in his living room on the couch, facing the front windows.
It’s the middle of the afternoon, and the sun is streaming in. Moving around the room
clockwise, he notices an oversize couch, a glass-topped coffee table, a fireplace with
a marble hearth, two upholstered swivel rocking chairs in front of a picture window, an
upright piano, an ancient grandfather clock, and a small, round bookshelf doubling as
an end table. Dave’s two dogs, Daisy and Chico, are there, too, Chico next to him on
the couch and Daisy on one of the rockers.
Step 1—Putting on His Normal Glasses: As Dave scans the room from left to right,
this is what he notices: I should’ve lit the fireplace. Even though it’s sunny, I like having a
fire. Gosh, the slipcovers on those rockers are tired. Those patches on the armrests look
awful and really should be replaced! The piano needs tuning, too, but… nobody ever
plays it anymore, so maybe it doesn’t matter. I do love how Chico puts his head on my
lap; what a sweet dog. Then Dave closes his eyes and notices all the action items that
came to mind: light the fireplace, replace the chair covers, tune the piano, pet the dog.
Four pending transactions—all actions to be completed in some future moment. He
pats Chico on the head, then mentally puts all the other things out of his
consciousness and looks around the room again.
Step 2—Putting on His Curiosity Glasses: Now Dave thinks, Okay, now that we’ve
gotten that out of the way, what else is going on in here? What am I curious about?
When he scans the room again, this is what he notices. Well, of course there are
patches on those rockers! An awful lot of people have sat in those chairs. That
grandfather clock hasn’t run in decades. I wonder when the last time was that it actually
chimed, actually was relied on to tell people what time it is. Who was in the room for
that last chime…? Dave notices that he is now much more present in the room and
thinking about what is actually in front of him, here and now, and that’s a good thing.
Step 3—Putting on His Wonder Glasses: Now Dave holds those two curiosities—
the patched chair and the long-silent clock—in his mind and lets his attention be
drawn to… the chair. He gazes at that worn and patched chair expectantly, wondering
what else is there and notices: My, those chairs have heard a lot of stories. People don’t
just sit there; they tell their stories there—stories of joy, confusion, anger, pain, births,
deaths. That chair has heard it all.
Dave lets a cavalcade of conversations held with people in that chair roll across the
movie screen of his mind: Family. Friends. The student who drove ninety minutes just
to get one more office hour. The first-time start-up founder sweating blood over every
decision and every dollar. The neighbor whose daughter wouldn’t speak to her. All
those lives are a wonder to me. What a privilege it is to be invited into the depths of



another person’s story. What a lovely reminder those patches are of all the love and
lives that chair has held. Maybe I’ll just leave the patches as they are.
That’s it—Dave has reached a state of wonder, in the moment, and it has evoked
memories he savors as he slowly returns to the reality of his living room on the couch,
facing the front windows in the middle of the afternoon with the sun streaming in.
Exercise: Museum Magic: Curating Your Curiosity
Orientation
This exercise is taken from one we have done with many different student groups
from twelve-year-old junior high school students to eighty-year-old Stanford’s
Distinguished Careers Institute fellows. It leverages how your brain is optimized to see
what you’re looking for versus what you’re looking at in a way that can turbocharge
your curiosity. This exercise works particularly well in a museum setting, where you’ll
have easy access to a wide range of different things all in one place. We usually do it
at the Cantor Arts Center at Stanford, but any museum will work.
To get the most out of this exercise, it’s important to understand what its purpose
is and isn’t. This exercise is a practicum, focused on process to train the designer’s
mindset of curiosity (which is the doorway to wonder). This is not about finding the
most important feature or theme of the work. Rather, it is about finding what about
each work or exhibit is interesting to you right in the moment and engaging that
interest. Avoid reading gallery descriptions and posted information as you’re
searching. This is a visual/interactive/personal journey and NOT a critical/analytical
journey. Think of it as an improv game—just be ready to say yes to what grabs you at
each step of the exercise.
Materials
You and an hour of your time (preferably two)
A friend (or two or three)
It’s optional to do with another person or group, but it’s more fun if more than
one of you do it together (each person does their own observing and sketching).
That way, you can have an interactive discussion as part of the reflection at the
end, which enhances the experience substantially.
A museum of your choice
A sketch pad and pencil (most museums permit this—but pencil only, usually)
Engagement Process
Pick your first object selection criterion from the list below (choose any one):
Material and Medium



Color or Lighting
Contrast or Conflict
Emotion
Story or Tradition
Familiar or New
Now wander into the first gallery you find and enter it in search of an object that
speaks to or relates to the theme you have picked. For instance, if you selected the
theme Emotion, and you saw a statue of someone crying, that might grab your
attention. Spend no more than one to three minutes looking for “your” object. If more
than one thing seems to speak to your theme—just pick one; it won’t matter which
one.
First Artifact Observation
When you’ve selected an artifact or object, do the following:
1. Stop and pay attention to it—for a total of at least three to five minutes (likely more).
2. Sketch it (or a detail of it) from at least two different views (taking two different
vantage points of observation) to become more intimate with the object.
3. As you are sketching and observing, reflect on these questions:
a. What are you noticing?
b. What is interesting to you, and how did it attract your attention?
c. What, if anything, are you getting out of this?
4. As soon as you’ve sketched, jot down your key thoughts right by your sketches on
your sketch pad.
5. After that’s done (not before!), answer this question: “What did you notice about the
piece later during your observation that you didn’t see initially?” (It can be anything—
mounting, messaging, color, texture, scale…) For example, after sketching the statue
of the crying person a few times, you recall noticing how the different surface
textures—some rough, some smooth, some polished—caught the light differently at
your two sketch positions. You pick your next theme to be surface.
Observation Continued (Artifacts 2–5)
Make that final noticing from your sketching encounter your new theme lens and
look for a new piece or artifact that calls out to you from that perspective. You can
continue in the same gallery, or, if the museum is big enough, try another room
with different things in it. For instance, in our example, in the next room while
looking for something related to “surface,” you notice an exhibit on the history of
tattooing and are intrigued by how different cultures have thought in different
ways about what their skin—their surface—means to them. There’s an ancient
tattoo hammer on display, and you choose that to observe and draw.



Pick a new object and repeat all five steps again. In our example, after drawing the
tattoo hammer, you notice pain (it really hurts to get hit with one of those things
hundreds of times in a tattoo session)—and off you go looking for something that
connects to “pain.” And so it goes for all five or more objects.
Each object you encounter in this way will likely take about ten minutes of your
time to do thoroughly. You should be able to encounter five or six objects in an
hour.
Repeat as many times as you can in the time you’ve allotted (forty-five to ninety
minutes is recommended—per your preference). Set a firm stop time.
Try to observe/sketch at least five items.
Visit at least three galleries or rooms to ensure a variety of experiences and
artifact forms.
Maintain an attitude of focused curiosity throughout. Work hard. Pay attention.
Reflection
When you’re all done, find a place where you can sit quietly and reflect on your
experience—don’t wait until you get home, as the memories will no longer be fresh.
Most museums have a café or some benches on the grounds or in the lobby, where
you can easily do this reflection. On your sketch pad, jot down your thoughts on these
questions:
What object most engaged your curiosity—why and how?
Did you exit the museum more curious than you entered it? What seemed to
contribute to that (or not)?
What is the role of your intention and focus, and how does that help bring out your
curiosity?
Make a list of your sequence of theme lenses. Can you connect the dots to see
any common thread or meta-narrative in your tour (there may or may not be one)?
What surprised you along the way?
How does this compare to how you would normally visit a museum? (Most people
feel like they’ve had a deeper, richer experience than usual and that the time flew
by.)
How does this compare to how you go about any other day in your regular life?



Can you use anything you’ve learned at the museum in forming a competency in
getting more out of rather than cramming more into your life?
Discuss (if you did this with others)
Check in: Each share your favorite object and one surprise.
How does this compare to your typical museum visit? What was fun/different
about it?
How does this compare to how you go about any other day?
What are you learning? What questions does this raise?
What takeaways did you get related to forming a competency in getting more out
of—rather than cramming more into—your life?

OceanofPDF.com



5

Living the Coherent Life
Reg liked to introduce himself as a poet but rarely got to do so because he spent
sixty-plus hours a week at his law firm, so most people thought of Reg as a
lawyer. But he thought of himself as a poet. Those late nights in bed reading
poetry, the early Saturday mornings spent writing his poems, and the five to ten
hours a month he spent editing the small poetry magazine where he was on the
masthead under a pseudonym all confirmed what he knew in his soul. He was
really an undercover poet. He didn’t mind the lawyering. Law was a noble
profession that, like poetry, respected the power of words—even if it used them
differently. So, he was at peace with the deal he’d made. Life was good. Reg was
grateful.
Derek was a solid if quiet guy. He was a competent software engineer, a
capable handyman, a terrific skier and outdoorsman, a dependable volunteer, a
reliable dad, and a devoted husband. He never went for the promotion. Never
spent much money on things. Never made a splash. Derek was stable, but he
wasn’t static. He was continually learning new things and improving his skills;
he grew, but he never changed. He was always the same guy. He’d figured out
who he was and what he cared about (mostly God, family, and nature) early in
life and proceeded to build a life around those priorities. He just kept being
Derek, doing that Derek thing in that decidedly Derek way, right up to the day
he died. At his memorial service, people told stories of the Derek they knew, and
they all described the same man. Everyone—whether coworkers, family



members, neighbors, or ski buddies—knew the same Derek, and they all agreed
he was one terrific guy.
Steena walked into Dave’s office hours looking grim and said, “I’ve got a
problem—a big one. Can you help me?”
“Well, I’ll sure try,” Dave said. “What’s going on?”
Steena described how she had majored in chemistry as a premed student just
to make her parents proud. Her real love was the piano, and in addition to
studying chemistry, which she’d done very successfully, she’d also secretly kept
up her piano playing and had just been admitted for a master’s in fine arts at the
Juilliard School in New York.
“Oh… so you need help figuring out how to tell your parents you want to go
to Juilliard instead of medical school?” asked Dave.
“No. That’s not it,” Steena said to Dave’s surprise. “The real problem is my
boyfriend. If I’m going to commit myself to being a pianist, I don’t think I can
love the piano and a man at the same time. I don’t think I have room in my life
for both of them, and I don’t know how to tell my boyfriend. He loves me, but
the truth is, I don’t love him as much as I do the piano. I don’t want to hurt him,
but I know I have to let him go.”
Dave figured there had to be a way out of this dilemma, but after a long
discussion examining every alternative, he had to agree with Steena’s conclusion.
Her devotion to the piano was clear and unshakable, and she simply refused to
accept a life in which she gave either her instrument or her boyfriend any less
than her total devotion. She had to choose—and she chose the piano. All that
remained was breaking the news to her young man. As she walked away, Dave
marveled at both her conviction and her courage.
Despite their differences, these three people share one thing: They are living
what we call a coherent life—one that genuinely reflects their values. Their
actions align with who they are and what they believe—sometimes effortlessly,
sometimes at great cost, but always in alignment. Coherence brings clarity,
direction, and a deep sense of purpose. These people know who they are and
where they need to be.
We define coherence as the ability to live with these three key elements
aligned and interconnected:



Who you are
What you do
What you believe
When these three elements harmonize, life flows naturally and meaningfully
—you don’t just exist, you thrive.
Put simply, a coherent life is what happens when your actions are an honest
expression of who you are right now and what truly matters to you. With that
alignment, you feel purposeful and confident about where you’re headed, and
each day carries more meaning.

Description 5

We call these three interconnected dots your compass. When we can connect
the dots clearly, our compass is calibrated and we’re on course. When we can’t
connect the dots, or don’t even know where they are, we feel disoriented and
lost.
One thing to keep in mind: You can strive for perfect coherency, but you will
probably never get to 100 percent. There are constraints in the real world that
we all have to deal with, the external forces of mortgages, families, imperfect
jobs, and so on. Living coherently doesn’t mean that your problems disappear
and everything is in perfect harmony all the time. At some points in life,
maintaining a solid sense of coherency is quite a challenge. During big trials, Reg
sometimes went so long without writing a poem that he worried he’d never write
another one. At other times, it feels like life is going okay and coherence comes
naturally. Derek never ran into any serious conflict at work because he never
took a job that demanded more than he wanted to give it. And all his kids loved
doing the same outdoorsy stuff he did, so it worked for Derek. But in some rare

[Embedded visual 1 on PDF page 107 (722x575 px). OCR-readable text: Who You / a Are : / What You What You / Believe Do]



cases, there just isn’t an available compromise, and some hard choices need to be
made. Steena accepted the huge compromise of giving up her boyfriend because
of her uncompromising commitment to the piano. By the way, Steena told Dave
that had she not been admitted by Juilliard, she was ready to accept that their
rejection meant she was not a good enough pianist to dedicate her life to music
and she would have gone to medical school and very likely have married her
boyfriend. Circumstances matter, and there isn’t just one right answer for any of
us. How you navigate these challenges to make your way to coherence may
comprise some of the most important decisions you ever make.
No matter the circumstances, the design challenge is universal: Begin with
radical acceptance—embrace your life as it is, acknowledge what cannot be
changed, and keep returning to the values and pursuits that give it meaning and
purpose. What’s encouraging is that for almost everyone we’ve spoken to, living
coherently was always within reach. Reg, Derek, and Steena (who are all real
people we have worked with, though their names and stories have been
anonymized) found their way to living coherently. For the people we’ve met who
failed to attain coherency, the real challenge wasn’t an impossible circumstance;
it was usually a reluctance to embrace necessary changes or to engage in the deep
reflection needed to find some clarity. Reg could have chosen to just languish
bitterly about having to make a living as a lawyer, but he found a workable
solution by creatively practicing the mindset of radical acceptance.
We know this isn’t always easy, but we also know you are capable of creating a
life that aligns with your values and brings greater meaning to each day. By
taking small, intentional steps, you can cultivate more coherence, more purpose,
and more fulfillment—starting right now.
So, let’s get to work. There are four tasks required to design and live a more
coherent life:
Build your compass and calibrate it
Make coherency sightings (i.e., take compass readings)
Reflect on those sightings
Recalibrate your compass



Compass Building
Living the coherent life is done by recognizing moments of coherency, then
capturing and reflecting on those experiences to make the most of them. That
only works when you have articulated what coherency is for you so that you’ll
recognize it when it’s happening. That’s where building your compass comes in.
The compass comprises defining and connecting the three dots of who you are,
what you believe, and what you do. We’ll build your compass with these three
writings:
My Current Story (who you are)
My Workview (what you do)
My Lifeview (what you believe)
My Current Story
My Current Story is just that—a short description of what’s currently going on
for you, what life is all about at this time. It’s not your whole story or your
autobiography, nor is it just what you’re currently reading or had for lunch. It’s a
snapshot (typically fifty to two hundred words) of the important things that
describe who you are and what’s going on right now. It will reflect both your
current circumstances and what’s most important to you in them.
We have a close friend, Lena, an accomplished creative in her fifties who just
went through a divorce, the sale of her home, and moving her youngest son off
to college. She was also recently diagnosed with Lyme disease, so it’s been
eighteen months of radical change. Here’s Lena’s current story:
I’m in a season of unraveling—and reinvention. After years of relentless
achievement, I’ve hit pause—not because I wanted to but because my
body asked me to. The Lyme diagnosis has forced me to slow down.
Slowing down has never been my thing, and it’s uncomfortable. I’m not
taking on as many projects anymore, and saying no to people is also
uncomfortable. And weird. I’ve had to close myself off to so many things,
but at the same time I feel like I’ve never been more open—to my own
healing, to different kinds of creativity, even to the possibility of falling in



love again. I live near the beach now, a place I’ve wanted to live since I was
twenty-one years old. The slow rhythm is teaching me something my
professional sphere never could—how to move at the speed of trust. I still
work, just differently. I make fewer time commitments, and that’s left
room for more clarity. Less proving and more presence. My kids are
growing up. My work is evolving. I’m lifting weights, doing hot yoga, and
learning what it means to live in a body I want to care for instead of one I
want to punish and push. My current story is about alignment. Letting go
of roles that no longer fit. Listening more than I speak. And asking not
“What can I build next?” but “What wants to be born through me now?”
It sounds a little woo-woo, I know, but I don’t have all the answers. Some
days, honestly, I’m not even sure I know who I am anymore. It’s
terrifying, but also kind of exciting.
So, what’s your current story?
Yours will sound different, but you get the idea. What’s your current story?
Let your mind run down the list of things that are going on: your work life, your
love life, your family, your recreation, your reading and learning, your challenges,
your transitions, your adventures, the people, the ideas, your health, your hopes
and worries, your goals and dreams. Let your mind scan through the whole list
of things that comprise your life and jot down a short list of what the important
things are now. Then just tell your story. Don’t overthink it. Keep it handy; we’ll
put it to use shortly.
My Workview and Lifeview
My Current Story tells who you are and locates where you’re standing in your
life at this time. The next question is: In what direction are you going? To
answer that question, you need two things: a Workview—to help you make
sense of the role work plays in your life, since for most people it’s their single
biggest activity—and a Lifeview—to guide your broader sense of purpose and
keep you aligned over the long arc of your life. The job of the Workview is to
define what work means to you. What is work for? Why do you do it? What
makes good work good? By discovering and being able to articulate your



philosophy of work (what it’s for and why you do it), you will be better able to
extract meaning from your experiences at work and find a sense of purpose in it.
Lifeview is a little different. It may sound a bit lofty, but it’s really not. Everyone
has a Lifeview, but you may not have articulated it before. A Lifeview is simply
your ideas about the world and how it works. What gives life meaning? What
makes your life worthwhile or valuable? How does your life relate to others in
your family, your community, and the world? What does money, recognition, or
personal accomplishment have to do with a satisfying life? How important are
experience, growth, and fulfillment in your life?
Don’t worry, we know that your Workview and Lifeview will change. It’s
obvious that the Workview and Lifeview you have as a teenager, as a young
college grad, or as an empty nester will all be substantially different. The point is,
you don’t have to have it all figured out for the rest of your life; you just have to
create the compass for what life’s about right now. The most important thing
about your Workview and Lifeview is that they are yours.
Parker Palmer, a renowned educational reformer and author of Let Your Life
Speak, writes that at one point he had to confess to himself that he was simply
doing a noble job of living someone else’s life. Parker was emulating his heroes—
Dr. Martin Luther King Jr., Gandhi, and Dorothy Day—the great social justice
icons of the 1950s and ’60s. Out of admiration for their leadership and sacrifice,
he set his path in the world by their compass, not his own, and aspired to bring
reform to higher education as an exemplary leader. He earned a PhD in
education at UC Berkeley and was on track to reach his goal of becoming a
respected university president. That was all fine and good, but Parker hated it.
He came to the realization that he could be inspired by others, but that didn’t
mean he had to walk their same path. He abandoned trying to be what he wasn’t
—an institutional chief executive—and redesigned his life as a thought leader
and writer. He was still working for the same goals but in a way that was less
imitative and more authentic.
There are lots of powerful voices in the world and lots of powerful voices in
our heads, all telling us what to do or who to be. And because there are many
models for how life is supposed to be lived—we all run the risk, like Parker, of
accidentally using someone else’s compass and living someone else’s life. The



best way to avoid this is to clearly articulate your own Workview and Lifeview to
build your own unique and trustworthy compass.
And remember, for most of us, some trade-offs and compromises have to be
made along the way, including some you might not like. If your Lifeview is that
art is the only thing worth pursuing, and your Workview tells you that it’s
critical to make enough money so your kids have what they need for a good start
in life, you may need to make a compromise in your Lifeview while your
children are dependent and at home. But that will be okay, because that’s your
conscious choice of an acceptable trade-off. Living coherently doesn’t mean
everything is in perfect order all the time. It simply means you are living in
alignment with your values and have not sacrificed your integrity along the way.
When you have a good compass guiding you, you have the power to cut these
kinds of deals with yourself. If you can see the connections between who you
are, what you believe, and what you are doing, you will know when you are on
course, when there is tolerable tension, when there might need to be some
careful compromises, and when you are in need of a major course correction.
You may be thinking, Hey, guys, just how do we know when the tension
between our real life and our values is fine (like Derek), tolerable (like Reg), in
need of some adjustment, or requires a major correction (like Steena)? Discerning
what is an acceptable compromise or when one is needed is difficult and
intensely personal. It’s also unpredictable because discernment isn’t about
simply applying rules of thumb. It arises within you as an awareness that’s
reached maturity at a particular moment of your life. You can’t see it until you’re
ready to see it, so you have to be patient and have a little faith in yourself. We
won’t attempt a whole treatise on discernment here, but we can say this: If you
pay attention. If you do the work of defining your Compass Values (which we’ll
explain shortly). If you notice your experience of your life (we’ll show you how).
If you adopt the designer’s mindset of create your world and keep fine-tuning
and better articulating your story. If you take coherency sightings as outlined
later this chapter. If you do these sorts of things… then when the time has come
for you to make a change, it will be evident to you. Now, we know how
aggravating it is when someone says something like, “Oh… when the time is right
for a change, you’ll know. You’ll just know.” We hate being patronized like that



and expect you do, too. That’s not what we’re saying. What we’re saying is that
by actively adopting these mindsets and doing the sometimes hard, sometimes
delightful work of paying attention to such things, you will empower your
capacity for discernment and increasingly be able to hear your own best wisdom
at the right time.
So now it’s time for you to write your Workview and Lifeview to build out
your compass.
Workview Reflection
Write a short reflection about your Workview. We’re not looking for a term
paper here, but we do want you to really write this down. Don’t just do it in
your head. Once you’ve thought through your ideas, the writing should take
about thirty to sixty minutes. Target length is 250 to 350 words—less than a
page of typed writing.
A Workview should address the critical issues related to what work is and
what it means to you. It is not just a list of what you want from or out of work
but a general statement of your view of work. It’s your definition for what good
work deserves to be. A Workview may address such questions as:
Why work?
What’s work for?
What does work mean?
How does it relate to the individual, others, society?
What defines good or worthwhile work?
What does money have to do with it?
What do experience, growth, and fulfillment have to do with it?
Here’s Lena’s Workview:
For years, my work was about survival—hustling to prove I was useful and
worthy to the world and to myself. Work used to be exhausting, but now
that I’m in a pause, it’s revealed something vital: my work is not my
identity, but it is an extension of my love. Work has become sacred—not
in the sense of martyrdom or sacrifice but as a living, breathing expression



of purpose. Work is how I pray with my hands. It’s where meaning takes
form and gets messy, complicated, and real. It’s not just what I do to earn
a living—it’s how I stay alive in the truest sense. Work, when it’s good, is a
place where I can tell the truth—about shame, about joy, about the
thousand lives we live in one body. Whether I’m writing, mentoring,
speaking, or simply showing up with presence, my work must align with
my soul. If it doesn’t nourish me, stretch me, or serve something beyond
my ego, it’s not mine to do. I believe good work is connective. It says: I see
you. I’ve been there, too. It invites others into dignity. It ripples beyond
one life. Money matters—it buys stability, safety, time—but for me,
money is not the measure of good work. Integrity is. Impact is. Joy is. At
this stage, my work is about making beauty out of brokenness, creating
spaces—on the page, in community, in conversation—where people can
tell the truth and still be loved. Work is where I transmute pain into power
and where hope can become contagious. That’s what I’m here for.
In the years we’ve been helping people with this exercise, we’ve noticed that a
Workview is a pretty new idea for most people. And we’ve noticed that when
people get stuck on this exercise, it is because they just write what they’re looking
for in a job or an employment situation, which is a job description. For this
exercise, we’re not interested in what work you want to do but why you work.
What we’re after is your philosophy of work—what it’s for, what it means.
It’s essentially your work manifesto. When using the term work, we mean the
broadest definition—not just what you do to make money or for “a job.” Work
is often the largest single component of most people’s waking lives, and over a
lifetime, it occupies more of our attention and energy than anything else we do.
Accordingly, we’re suggesting you take the time to reflect and articulate what
work and vocation mean to you (and perhaps what you hope it means for others
as well).
Workviews can and do range widely in what they address and how they
incorporate different issues, such as service to others and the world, money and
standard of living, and growth, learning, skills, and talents. All of these can be
part of the equation. We want you to address what you think is important. You



do not have to address the question of service to others or any explicit
connection to social issues. However, the positive psychologist Martin Seligman
found that the people who can make an explicit connection between their work
and something socially meaningful to them are more likely to find satisfaction
and are more able to adapt to the inevitable stresses and compromises that come
with working in the world. Since most people tell us they long for satisfying and
meaningful work, we encourage you to explore the questions above and write
down your Workview. Your compass won’t be complete without it.
Lifeview Reflection
Just as you did with the Workview, write a reflection on your Lifeview. While
you may spend some time thinking about it, the writing should also take no
more than thirty to sixty minutes and should be 250 to 350 words or so. Below
are some questions that are often addressed in a Lifeview, just to get you started.
The key thing is to address those critical defining values and perspectives that
provide the basis for your understanding of life. Your Lifeview is what provides
your definition of what have been called “matters of ultimate concern.” It’s what
matters most to you.
Why are we here?
What is the meaning/purpose of life?
What is the relationship between the individual and others?
Where do family, country, and the rest of the world fit in?
What is good? What is evil?
Is there a higher power, God, or something transcendent, and if so, how does
this impact your life?
What is the role of joy, sorrow, justice, injustice, love, peace, strife in life?
We realize that these are somewhat philosophical questions, and we did just
mention the G-word. Some readers will see God as unimportant; others may
have wanted us to address this up front as the most important issue. You’ve
probably figured out by now that design is values-neutral, and we don’t take
sides. The questions, including the ones about God or spirituality, are given to
provoke your thinking, and it’s up to you to see which ones you want to try to



answer. They are not talking points for religious or political debates, and there
are no wrong answers. No wrong Lifeviews. The only way to do this incorrectly
is not to do it at all. Besides that, be curious and think like a designer. Add the
questions that work for you, make up your own, and see what you discover.
Here are Lena’s answers to the Lifeview questions:
I believe we are here to love—each other, ourselves, this wildly imperfect
world. I used to think life was about becoming someone better. Striving to
learn more so we can be more. Now I know it’s about remembering who
we really are beneath the performance, the survival instincts we mistake
for personality, the shame. We are stardust with a heartbeat. The purpose
of life, for me, is to grow more whole—by telling the truth, by forgiving
what I thought was unforgivable, by holding the tension between hope
and heartbreak. My life has had joy and also lots of sorrow, like everyone’s
has. Death. Divorce. Failure. Starting over again and again. I used to think
those things were the end of the world; now I just think of them as
necessary parts to the whole. I believe in a higher power—not one with a
beard and a gavel for judging but a Love so big it makes room for all of it.
Grace is my religion. Not the shiny, Sunday-best kind but the gritty grace
that meets us in a jail cell, a hospital bed, a messy kitchen, or a whispered
prayer for help. God used to be a distant concept. Not anymore. Now I
think God is in everyone and everything all at once. And we’re all
connected. My healing ripples outward. So does my harm. I try to choose
healing. I think this choice changes everything, from how I spend my time
to how I speak to my friends to how I show up with my family. I guess it’s
all about being present, letting go of things that don’t matter in the big
picture, and trusting that everything is going to work out. What is
something good now? Anything rooted in compassion. What is bad?
Anything that feels like fear or disconnection. I want my life now to be in
service to something bigger than me. I used to want more recognition and
validation and accomplishment; now I want more justice, truth,
connection, and health, of course.



Now it’s time to write down your answers.
Ready? Begin.
Calibrating Your Compass—Identifying Your Compass Values
Like any instrument your compass needs to be calibrated, fine-tuned for
accuracy. The first step of calibration is doing an integration check. Read over
your Lifeview and Workview and write down a few thoughts on each of the
following questions:
Where do your Workview and Lifeview complement each other?
Where do they clash?
What elements in one do and don’t have correlation in the other?
Does one drive the other? How?
Please take some time to write up your thoughts on the integration of your
two views. Our readers tell us that this is where they often get the biggest aha
moments, so give the integration some thought. In most cases, this reflection
will result in some editing of one or both of your views.
Having completed your integration check, it’s time for a relevancy check. Go
back and reread your My Current Story, then review your updated Workview
and Lifeview, this time asking:
Do these versions of my Workview and Lifeview adequately reflect my
current situation?
Is there something in My Current Story that’s missing here and warrants
attention in my Workview or Lifeview?
Make any final edits that these questions bring to your attention. Now your
My Current Story plus your Workview and Lifeview comprise an authentic,
integrated, and personally relevant compass.
To make your compass design readily usable in day-to-day life, we’ll take one
last calibration step to identify your Compass Values. A traditional compass
points in the direction of magnetic north, from which you can calculate true



north, the cardinal direction that orients the compass. Your core values define
what direction points “north” on your life compass. Studying all three of your
My Current Story, Workview, and Lifeview will reveal many different values—
some explicitly stated, some implied in context or between the lines. But at any
given moment in life, some values are more important, more relevant than
others. What we’re looking for are the top three to five values that most matter
to you at this time. These are your Compass Values. Of course, all your values
matter, but you don’t want to be overwhelmed. Coherency isn’t about
