perfection; it’s about being in alignment with what’s most important.
Prioritizing a short list of your Compass Values will make coherency clearer and
therefore more attainable. Expect your values short list to change over time,
which is fine and just means that you’re continuing to evolve as a person. But at
any point, you want to have a clear view and a firm grip on which way is north.
So keep your definition of north simple and concise. Your list of Compass Values
can’t include everything at once.
Let’s try an example to illustrate how to extract Compass Values from the
three key compass components: My Current Story, Workview, and Lifeview.
We’ll use Lena as our example.
We want to be clear—this isn’t about presenting an ideal or prescribing the
“right” way to live. Values are deeply personal. And while Lena’s values reflect
her life at this particular moment, yours will reflect your own priorities and
circumstances and background. Our only goal is to illustrate the process of
identifying Compass Values using a real and integrated story.
Compass Values Extraction Example for Lena
Let’s look at each of Lena’s three compass components to distill the values that
define her true north:
Lena’s My Current Story: Lena is in a season of unraveling. After years of
achieving, hustling, and proving, Lyme disease has called her inward. She’s let go
of building her company and external success markers and is now devoted to
healing, slowing down, writing, exercising, and exploring the possibility of a new
love. She lives in a place that gives her peace, she listens deeply, and she chooses



what aligns. Her work is no longer about striving—it’s about integrity, joy, and
love.
Lena’s Workview: Work is a sacred expression, not a hustle. It’s how she
channels love, hope, truth, and transformation into the world. Good work
connects, stretches, and serves. It’s honest. Money matters only to the extent
that it provides stability and space to do that meaningful work. If the work
doesn’t serve her soul or others, she’s no longer willing to force it.
Lena’s Lifeview: Life is about becoming whole—telling the truth and still
being loved. She believes in grace as a spiritual force, in forgiveness, in the
interconnectedness of all things. Joy and sorrow coexist. Justice is only true if
rooted in compassion. Connection, love, beauty, and repair are central.
Reading over her writing, we are able to look at each of Lena’s three elements
to find the Compass Value set that will succinctly define her true north.
Lena’s My Current Story is clearly focused on moving at a new pace—going
inward, slowing down, and paying attention to her health and well-being. The
word healing is evident in all she’s doing, and so that deserves to be on the list.
Both Lena’s Current Story and her Workview mention the word sacred, whether
it’s implicit in how she’s approaching the “unraveling” of an identity built
around striving or the very clearly stated idea that to her work is “sacred.”
Because the word features up front in both reflections, sacredness seems an
important value to add to the list. Another word that figures prominently in
Lena’s Lifeview and Workview is truth, so that is a clear value important to
Lena’s Current Story, whether it’s the truth of needing to slow down and focus
on her physical health or the prioritizing of work that’s rooted in what is true
and meaningful for her now. Connection is another value we can extract from her
Workview and Lifeview, and one that helps her consider where to spend her
limited time as she’s healing. And, finally, we have the value of grace, which is
both explicit and implicit in the three components of her compass. Grace for
herself, in admitting her new limits with her work, and as a spiritual value she
applies to those around her.
Of course, there are other values that could be extracted from Lena’s
reflections, but given her Current Story, Lifeview, and Workview, she agrees that
the five values listed below are what she’s prioritizing now.



From her reflections, we can compose a succinct list of Lena’s Compass
Values at this point in her life:
Healing
Grace
Truth
Connection
Sacredness
That list fits easily on a Post-it note with room to spare. At any moment and
in any situation, Lena can get her coherency bearings by reviewing these
Compass Values to determine if she’s on the right compass course or needs to
make an adjustment.
When your Workview and Lifeview sync with your Current Story, you steer
life with deliberate clarity. Think of a seasoned sailor: winds shift, storms roll in,
and sometimes you hug the shoreline, but a reliable compass always points true
north. Likewise, each course correction—whether a bold tack or a temporary
pause—remains intentional, keeping you aimed at a coherent, meaningful life no
matter what conditions arise.
Recalibrating Your Compass
Anytime you’re changing your situation, or pursuing a new thing, or wondering
what you’re doing at a particular job—stop. Before continuing, it’s a good idea
to check your compass and recalibrate it if necessary. Then you can take an
accurate reading on where you are and how you’re doing. Recalibrating your
compass involves nothing more than reviewing each of the three elements—My
Current Story, Workview, and Lifeview—and revising them to reflect the latest
changes in you, your thinking, and your circumstances. While there’s no right
rhythm, it makes sense to review these things at least annually and certainly
following any big shifts or moves. For Lena, that shift was illness. A year from
now, it might be full health, or a new creative project, or another move. When
that time comes, her Compass Values may shift as well—perhaps Curiosity or



Creativity rise, and Healing becomes embedded and integrated. Compass
recalibration is a good habit to sustain your practice for living a meaningful life.
Rotate your tires.
Change the battery in your smoke detector.
Double-check your Workview and Lifeview and make sure they align with
one another and your updated My Current Story.
Coherency Sightings
The positive psychology movement says that the number one exercise in
improving the quality of your life is to make a gratitude list. This is a simple
exercise where, at the end of each day, you spend a few minutes writing down
three things you were grateful for on that particular day. This is a great first step
in directing your attention to where and when you are experiencing things that
matter to you. There’s gold in this, but the gratitude exercise often only takes us
into the transactional world.
Think about it. Today, I was grateful that I got a free hamburger. I was
grateful to make that green light when I was running late. I was grateful to get the
kiss I was hoping my partner would give me when I got home. These are all really
transactions (yes—even the kiss, if you just focus on getting it instead of basking
in the affection flowing in it). They’re events. They are outcomes. And that’s
what we see most people write down when doing the gratitude exercise.
We’re going to take it up a level and look for coherency sightings, which have
greater potential for evoking meaning. Instead of just noticing what we’re
grateful for, we are going to pay attention to when we are having a lived
experience of connecting the dots of who I am, what I believe in, and what I’m
doing that reflect my Compass Values. Coherency sightings sound a little
different from gratitude sightings. Today, I had a chance to exercise generosity
when I picked up the tab for the guy behind me in line at the burger joint. Or: I was
glad to show affection today by bringing my partner some unexpected flowers after
work.
You can find the detailed instructions for coherency sightings in the Try Stuff
section at the end of this chapter. Coherency sightings are a simple but powerful



tool. We strongly recommend that you do them and do them regularly.
Coherency on the Job
The number one place where people report that they experience meaning in
their lives is at work. That may be a surprise to some (perhaps you thought it
would be at home, among family, or out in nature), but if you think about it for
a moment, it makes a lot of sense.
Work is the activity, place, and community where the overwhelming majority
of us expend our greatest energies and time (two thousand to three thousand
hours a year for most of us—nothing else comes close). It typically is the place of
our largest impact and our most substantial output. “Hey—aren’t we mostly in
the transactional world at work?” you ask. “How does work top the list of
meaning-making?”
Great questions. The workplace is indeed almost entirely transactional, so
where’s the meaning, and how do we extract it? The trick to doing this is
twofold. First, we need to focus on different things, and then we need to look at
those things differently.
Most people focus primarily, if not exclusively, on the impact of their work,
their outcomes—things like “Did our menu overhaul at our restaurant achieve
our goal of at least 50 percent gluten-free choices?” or “Did the children who
used our home reading app do better in school than those that didn’t?” These
things are important and are the types of outcome-oriented measures that
organizations are after and track religiously. And hitting those goals and those
KPIs (key performance indicators) does matter. Even though most of this book
is about helping you expand your meaning-making beyond impact to include
wonder, flow, coherence, and formative community, we still think impact is great
stuff and hope you succeed in making an impact. But making an impact isn’t
predictable, and even when it works, the big outcomes occur too seldom to
provide the kind of steady meaning diet that we’re all hungry for. So, let’s
complement impact by adding some coherency to our meaning-making diet at
work.
There’s a treasure trove of coherency sightings awaiting us all at work. All we
have to do is shift our attention from just looking at the what (the outcome) we



are working toward to the how of the actual interactions and activity we and our
coworkers are engaged in every day. Once we’ve turned our attention to the how
of our immediate work experience (which has cleverly moved us from the
transactional world into the flow world pulsing all around us at work), we can
start looking for moments where coherency is happening. For example, if you
have a Compass Value of putting others first, you would log a coherency
moment when you let a junior member of your team make the progress report
to the staff meeting. By affirming the coherency of how you’re doing your work,
you don’t have to wait for the impact of the completed project to make a
meaningful moment.
Work can become suddenly much more meaningful when we start
recognizing when it’s coherent for us and reflect on that experience to more fully
appreciate it. We acknowledge there are many places beyond work where you can
experience coherency, but we recommend doing lots of coherency sighting at
work, as it’s by far the biggest opportunity for most people, and it’s one that’s
largely untapped for most of us. Finding more meaning on the job is a huge getmore-out-of-life instead of try-to-cram-more-into-life opportunity.
Coherency Benefits
Doing coherency sightings as a practice, not just a one-time thing, brings lasting
benefits. First, we predict that you’ll notice moments of coherency more readily.
By having coherency in the back of your mind, you’re more likely to catch the
moments when they occur. Second, we predict that you’ll find that you are
starting to live a more coherent life and will do so seemingly effortlessly. Your
coherency won’t be increasing because you’re trying harder or are more
dedicated but simply because your raised level of awareness will naturally keep
you on the right compass heading. Skiers and mountain bikers know well that
you go where you look. So, if you want to ski the right track or bike the right line
—look there and you’ll go there. Look at the tree or the rut—you’ll go there,
too. Attention matters. When you’re looking for coherency, you go there. It’s
one more way to adopt the designer’s mindset of availability. When you adhere
to such a practice, you are training your attention, which makes you more



available to both having and noticing moments of coherency. And that’s exactly
the kind of meaningful moment-making we’re after.
Attending to your coherency also helps you gain more access to the flow
world. In the transactional world, life is all about production—achieving that
future goal or desired outcome. In the flow world, life is all about participation
—being fully present to and deeply engaged in the moment you and those
around you are living. Coherency is about how you are participating in life, not
just what you’ve produced. Coherency is available at any time; you don’t have to
wait for final results before celebrating it. Coherency sightings allow us to access
the flow world while still actively participating in the very busy transactional
world.
Coherency sightings are one of our favorite design tools for getting more out
of life. Why is that? Why focus on these sightings, and why are they so
important? Because moments of coherency are moments when you are living
out your best intentions. They are moments when you are becoming the person
you long to be—a person who is manifesting their Compass Values. In a
moment of coherency, you are realizing—in one small, scandalously particular
way—your better self. And no act of coherency is too small to be worthy of
notice! Coherency is one of those “ultimates” like beauty, justice, peace, love,
and compassion—ideals we aspire to but never fully attain. It’s only experienced
in little chunks of real-life particularity. If kindness is one of your Compass
Values, then simply giving your seat on the subway to the parent with a kid in
one arm and groceries in the other counts. Catching yourself in the act of being
coherent and celebrating those coherent moments when you are doing the right
thing is incredibly affirming and life-giving.
But what about incoherency? Shouldn’t we also be paying attention to when
we’re failing at this? We don’t think so. In our experience, positive reinforcement
is a far more powerful catalyst for growth than negative critique. Life is hard, and
we all make mistakes and fall short. We’re all inconsistent. It’s easy to get
discouraged and lose heart, or even become cynical. But discouragement and
cynicism won’t move you forward, won’t bring you more meaning, and won’t
help you bring joy to others. Most of us are oversupplied in criticism from
perhaps well-meaning people who seem dedicated to making sure we know what



we’re doing wrong (not to mention the flood of critique coming from within
our own heads). While we do recommend using your compass to make course
corrections when needed, our primary suggestion is to use your compass as an
affirmation tool to catch moments of coherency and celebrate them.
So, zero in on the moments when your head, heart, and hands lock into
alignment—those sparks of coherence are your true north. Log each one,
amplify it, and let the evidence of “getting it right” tug you forward faster than
any self-critique ever could. Build your compass, chart those wins, and let the
journey be joyful. We are cheering you on!
TRY STUFF
The two key activities recommended in this chapter are Build Your Compass and
Coherency Sightings Log, which are both described at length above in the body of the
chapter. Here’s a quick reminder checklist for each:
Exercise: Build Your Compass
1. Write your My Current Story (50 to 200 words)
2. Write your Workview Reflection and Lifeview Reflection (250 to 350 words each)
3. Calibrate your compass:
a. Review your My Current Story and check your Workview and Lifeview for
relevancy.
b. Do an integration check of your Workview and Lifeview.
c. Extract your Compass Values from your My Current Story, Workview, and Lifeview.
Exercise: Coherency Sightings Log
Here’s how to do the practice of capturing coherency sightings.
Step 1—Compass Values: Review your Lifeview and Workview exercises and your
My Current Story and confirm your Compass Values per the Compass Calibration
process described above—adjust if needed.
My Compass Values (with examples from Lena):
__________ (e.g., healing)
__________ (e.g., grace)
__________ (e.g., truth)
__________ (e.g., connection)



__________ (e.g., sacredness)
__________
Step 2—Log Coherency Sightings: Capture coherent moments in this log:
Date

Compass Value

Instance

What it means to
me

Regularly (either daily or weekly), notice the instances when you are experiencing
coherency because you are acting in alignment with your Compass Values. Keep a log
of the date, what you were doing, and what it meant to you.
Step 3—Review and Reflect: Periodically (weekly if you are doing this daily, monthly
if you are doing it weekly), review your log and journal on what you’re noticing:
What if any patterns are you noticing?
What Compass Values are you seeing more often? Less often?
What do you experience when you look back at your inventory of coherent
moments?
How is doing this practice affecting you?
Timing and Variations: We recommend most people do this practice on a weekly
basis over time to build a solid record of their coherency moments. However, you
could do it daily if you find it especially beneficial, or when you are just getting started
to get the hang of it and quickly build up a positive coherency inventory. You could
also do it less often—perhaps quarterly—as a way of taking stock of your life and
checking in with yourself. You can vary the practice format as well. Try taking
sightings on just one of your Compass Values (e.g., resilience) for a week and see
what you find. Rotate through all the values and then reflect on what you discover.

OceanofPDF.com



6

Finding Flow
Earlier in the book, we talked about the flow world and distinguished it from a
state of flow—that state when you’re so deeply immersed in an activity that time
dissolves, distractions fade, and every action feels effortless—like a pianist lost in
the music, an athlete moving with perfect rhythm, or a writer whose words pour
onto the page as if guided by an unseen force. Flow is a state of peak
performance and pure engagement where challenge meets skill in perfect
harmony. First introduced by psychologist Mihaly Csikszentmihalyi, the concept
of flow reveals how humans unlock their greatest potential when fully present in
the moment. But what exactly triggers this elusive state of being “in the zone,”
and how can we cultivate more of it in our daily lives? And if it’s true that we live
in both the transactional world and the flow world, can we balance the two to
bring more moments of meaning and purpose into our experience?
Our response to that question is an emphatic “Yes!”
Being in flow is terrific! Everyone wants more flow—in business, in sports, in
our personal lives. As we pointed out in chapter 3, the reason the flow state is
meaningful is because the purity of aliveness we experience in it makes us feel
more human. It releases in us what Joseph Campbell called the rapture of being
alive. Who doesn’t want more of that?! The only problem with flow—at least as
it’s conventionally understood—is that it’s a rare experience. It’s too elusive to
be something one can count on. In this chapter, we’ll reframe a few things and
chart the way to a much more accessible range of flow experiences. To do so, let’s



take another look at the conventional concept of the flow state, identify its
limitations, and design our way onto a bigger playing field.
In the Zone
Let’s begin by looking at how neuroscience understands the way flow works, and
what brings on the experience of being in the zone. The reason athletics and
focused, repetitive actions lend themselves to flow is that these skill-based flow
moments quiet the brain, allowing muscle memory and instinct to take over.
Neuroscience research makes a distinction between implicit functions that don’t
involve conscious thought and explicit functions that do. Explicit, conscious
brain activity is what’s happening in your achieving brain in the transactional
world—pretty much all the time. In fact, most of us think that having thoughts
—the self-aware mental activity of generating words to explicitly describe
something—is all there is to thinking. But the kind of thinking done by your
implicit cognition capacity isn’t language-based—it’s behavioral. Research has
shown that the implicit system utilizes and conveys intelligence without words
via the lived experience of using one’s skills.
It turns out that you don’t need words to think, and flow is a particular—and
rather delightful—form of a largely wordless experience. Science has confirmed
what design has long known—that there are lots of ways of thinking other than
verbal. The Design Program at Stanford has been teaching visual thinking for
decades, so this isn’t news to us. And we regularly explain design thinking as a
mechanism where we build to think—not where we talk or conceptualize to
think.
Seeing and doing are forms of thinking—components of the implicit types of
thinking that are not verbal but experiential. Current understanding recognizes
that the explicit (verbal and rule-based) and implicit (practice-based and
behavioral) thinking systems can and do operate coincidentally, especially when
you are learning something new.
The science tells us that flow occurs when we’re operating more in our
embodied, experiential modes of thinking rather than in our wordy, narrative
type of thinking. One of the reasons for this is that embodied experience is



almost always felt in the present moment, which is consistent with how things
work in the flow world.
This has also been demonstrated by brain scans. When we calm the decisionmaking part of the brain, the prefrontal cortex, we see increased activity in the
parts of the brain involved in synthesis and finding context. We also know that
other research indicates that when we quiet the prefrontal cortex and especially
the fight, flight, or freeze response, centered in the amygdala, we see increased
activity in parts of the brain that we now associate with the experience of awe,
wonder, and transcendence. (Which we think may be a hint at why being in flow
is so meaningful and delightful.)
Now that we’ve got a basic understanding of what flow is neurologically, let’s
look at where it comes from and how to access it.
The Conventional Flow Model Problem
The diagram below shows the flow channel as presented in Csikszentmihalyi’s
original 1990 book, Flow: The Psychology of Optimal Experience.

Description 6

In this model, the flow state is achievable when skill and challenge are in
balance during an activity. If your skill isn’t up to the task, you get nervous
about failing or don’t know how to proceed, and anxiety takes over. If you’re
over-skilled for the task, it doesn’t need your full attention and you get bored.
When skill and challenge are in balance, the activity pulls you fully into it but
doesn’t overstress you. When performing an activity within this narrow flow
channel, the conditions are such that the flow state of deep engagement may
occur (or it may not).

[Embedded visual 1 on PDF page 129 (779x571 px). Flow-channel graph: skill increases left-to-right and challenge increases bottom-to-top; a diagonal flow channel lies between boredom below and anxiety above.]



Being in flow, as conventionally understood, is all about attaining an
extremely high level of engagement in the activity you’re performing—an
engagement level that has the potential to become completely absorbing. This
high-level engagement happens across multiple modalities simultaneously:
mental, emotional, physical, and—if others are involved as in a team sport—
social. The degree of engagement is high precisely because the level of challenge
demands all the skill you can muster in the moment. The challenge pulls all your
faculties together to focus on this one thing that you’re doing. And it’s
happening in real time in the present moment. Flow is not just an intellectual or
emotional experience; it’s a fully human experience. So, in this type of intense
flow, which we call peak flow, you are having a pure experience of being alive. All
of you is involved (or at least lots of you is involved, and any parts of you that
aren’t involved are temporarily forgotten so that none of your attention is
wasted), and you are entirely in the present moment where the activity is taking
place.
In this type of flow state, you are experiencing what Maslow meant by selfactualization (i.e., being all that you can be).
“Wait a minute!” you retort. “Back in chapter 2, you guys said that Maslow’s
ideal of self-actualization was wrong and that we shouldn’t pursue that
unattainable, dead-end myth of ‘fulfillment.’ What’s going on here?!”
“Good point!” we respond. “Glad you asked.”
What’s really going on here is that flow is the place where self-actualization
and the scandal of particularity meet. Whoa—that’s a big idea—so let’s unpack
it.
Maslow’s original concept of self-actualization leading to fulfillment by one
becoming all that one can be is indeed wrong—dangerously wrong, in our
opinion. It’s wrong because all of us contain more aliveness than can be fully
expressed in one lifetime, so no matter how full a life you live, some of what you
can be—your ultimate human potential—won’t and can’t be realized. Ever.
This is where the scandal of particularity really shines, because you can fully
express yourself in a particular moment, which is what’s happening in an
experience of intense flow. Bill is an artist and experiences peak flow in his studio
regularly. He describes it like this:



When I’m standing in front of one of my large empty canvases [Bill likes
to paint big] and I’m trying to figure out how to start, what this painting
is about [Bill mostly paints abstract landscapes and environments], I first
have to fight off all the critical voices and noise in my head. Then, once
things quiet down, my curiosity picks up, and I start. When it is going
really well, the painting just happens. The brush feels right in my hand,
the paint has just the right squishiness, the images and colors seem to
“arrive” on the canvas in perfect order [i.e., Bill shifts from explicit
thinking in words and concepts to implicit thinking in practice and
experience]. I just stand there, helping the painting come into focus. It is
done when it needs to be done, and I step back, amazed at the mysterious
nature of what just happened. If that isn’t an altered state, a state of total
flow, I don’t know what is.
Even peak flow experiences like Bill’s are particular—meaning they involve
just a small portion of the whole person. While Bill is in flow painting, his guitar
skills and his ability to teach aren’t being expressed—but that’s not a problem,
because he doesn’t know that! All of his attention and every bit of the focus and
ability he needs are wrapped up in the act of painting. In that moment, the only
parts of Bill that are being used—and the only parts he’s even aware of—are the
particular ones the painting calls for.
In peak flow, you feel momentarily self-actualized and fully alive because you
are participating in that scandalously particular flow moment. The problem is
that there’s just not enough of this sort of flow available. According to
Csikszentmihalyi’s original theory, the opportunity for flow is limited to the
(rather narrow) flow channel shown in the last figure. But wait… there’s less! It
actually gets worse.
In his 1997 book, Finding Flow: The Psychology of Everyday Life,
Csikszentmihalyi describes additional mental and emotional challenges to being
in flow. In his updated model, the zone of activity where flow can occur shrinks
down further to just the upper-right corner of the flow diagram, as shown here.



Description 7

The only way to find real flow, according to this map, is to identify a highchallenge + high-skill activity, throw yourself into it wholeheartedly, and hope
that the flow state happens. No wonder flow seems so elusive for most people!
But it needn’t be that way. If we reframe our orientation to flow, we will discover
that lots of flow moments await us—continually bubbling up from the aquifer
of the ever-present flow world. The trick to gaining access to these untapped
flow moments is adopting a designer’s mindset—in this case, our favorite
designer’s mindset twofer: radical acceptance and availability.
The Flow Mindsets—Radical Acceptance and Availability
To make the experience of flow more accessible, we need to shift the source of
flow from outside in, relying on an external activity-challenge pair to hold you in
flow, to an inside-out orientation, relying on your mindset—in this case,
acceptance and availability—to free you to fully engage in what you’re doing. If
you look at the competing emotions in the previous diagram (e.g., boredom,
worry, etc.), you can see that flow is being prevented from occurring by the
intrusion of competitive thoughts and feelings. They sound something like
this…
When your skill level is above the challenge of your task…
Apathy says, “Oh, please. Do we really have to be doing this? What’s the
point?!”
Boredom says, “This is too easy! It’s dumb to waste our talent on this. Pick
something else!”

[Embedded visual 1 on PDF page 132 (921x701 px). OCR-readable text: = = Arousal / M4 / x) é j / o €& lS / D> 2 ZL / c 6 / ®O & / = / Ro / O Relaxation / Fs Boredom / ml / Low Medium High / Skill level]



Control says, “Well, okay, if we’re going to do this, how can we do it better
and faster this time? Let’s try to at least achieve more in doing it!”
When your skill level is below the challenge of your task…
Worry says, “Oh, I don’t like this. We’re in the wrong place—I’m afraid we
might blow it! Be very careful.”
Anxiety says, “There’s no way I can do this! I always fail at this sort of thing.
I’ll just make a mess of it.”
Stress says, “Whoa! This is exciting, but a little more than I bargained for—
what do I do now?”
These thoughts and emotions function like inbound intruders stealing your
attention, focus, and joy. They make it impossible for you to fully engage with
what you’re doing, and so prevent you from being in flow. If you listen carefully
to what these antagonistic voices are saying, they are all resisting acceptance of
the activity you’re performing. Boredom is resisting not being fully challenged.
Control is resisting just having a predictably simple experience without much
effort. Worry is resisting taking the risk of making a mistake. Apathy is resisting
even participating at all in something that demands less than your best. None of
those states of mind are accepting the task for what it simply is—something
perhaps not terribly challenging or possibly somewhat above your skill level.
But if you apply radical acceptance to any activity—radical by not accepting
any distractions due to a lesser or greater challenge or being a little over- or
under-skilled—you can successfully resist those intrusive reactions and in so
doing retain your full attention for the activity at hand. Radical acceptance
becomes a shield against distractions, thus preserving your potential for
achieving some degree of flow.
It looks like this:
The Radical Acceptance Flow Protection Shield



Description 8

Now we add availability—the ready-and-willing state of mind with a positive
bias toward action—to boost the deployment of our attention, the attention
that acceptance has defended from distraction. Together, they look like this:
The Radical Acceptance Flow Protection Shield and the Availability Flow Booster

Description 9

This diagram shows how acceptance and availability operate synergistically to
give you a positive advantage. Acceptance keeps common negative intrusions at
bay, availability kick-starts our engagement level, and the combination greatly
increases our chances of having a flow experience.
Time to Change the Channel
By employing radical acceptance and availability, we effectively widen the flow
channel and all but eliminate the boundaries to where we can access flow. The
expanded flow channel looks like this:

[Embedded visual 1 on PDF page 134 (1005x647 px). OCR-readable text: ey / cal Accept, ye / Your qos veg f SS / fail aoe \ Z aoe / Gain Q Z / Sy Zz / — ye / ~ om / ~ z / < 2 / Stop! = Flow = Watch out! / = Potential = / = ~ / = < / ~ S / 2; » Ss f / O ic NS Ap, / we? >, N bon’ Fj / om nt at ~S) tte, Nd / OF ow ’ (psy rst \n S Ways]

[Embedded visual 2 on PDF page 134 (1012x656 px). OCR-readable text: RS / psica! Acceptan- , Ze / "euiy fai GINO Kee’. / VA i aN / SS we, / YG she z= / > XE / ~~ { [KD i~) 3 = / = < y\ = / ain Flo = / WwW a / Stop! = . = Watch out! / =» = ‘ Potential i =]\e--— / Se X ay CC bs >) / © G 285 as aay Can; / RO Fo TMailaower 7“ borne tng, / OP ca 7a 5S "ayo]



Description 10

This enlarged flow channel gives you permission to pursue a wider range of
flow possibilities. There are still limitations. It is likely the case for most people
that a task that is well over their skill level will induce a level of stress and anxiety
that impedes flow. And some tasks that are far below our skill level will probably
still bring on flow-stopping boredom for most of us. Nevertheless, this
perspective significantly expands the potential for having life-affirming flow
experiences.
The conventional model of flow we’ve been discussing so far has in practice
overwhelmingly been limited to peak flow—flow induced by the intense focus
that accompanies the use of our highest skill level. Peak flow is terrific! It’s the
best in class and a truly wonderful experience. We heartily encourage you to get
yourself all the peak flow you can. We won’t spend much more time on peak
flow, because it is setting the bar high—too high for most of us most of the time
(Bill has spent hundreds of hours in that studio with brush in hand to get to
attain that peak experience, which only occurs once in a while).
Simple Flow
Our strategy is to set the bar low (or at least a little lower) so you grow your
faculty for flow over time. We call it simple flow, and it comes in two flavors: in
the moment and extended.
By simple, in-the-moment flow, we mean times of flow-like participation in
the present moment that can be had almost anywhere, anytime, if we are
practicing acceptance and availability. In chapter 3, we shared the two versions of
Fritz’s morning routine on the day of his big presentation—one lived entirely in
the transactional world, and one lived much more in the flow world. In the

[Embedded visual 1 on PDF page 135 (929x518 px). OCR-readable text: eee]



second version, Fritz was adopting the twofer mindsets of being available and
radically accepting his situation and tapping into the experience of in-themoment flow.
Anytime you are genuinely directing your attention to the flow world, we
consider it to be time spent in flow. A brief moment of simple flow isn’t as
intense or impactful as a whole afternoon spent in the zone of peak flow—but it
is a real and lovely experience that invites a legitimately meaningful moment.
Such a simple moment can be meaningful because in that moment you are being
self-transcendent. In chapter 1, we discussed how Maslow finally realized that
the path to meaning-making (versus fulfillment) was through self-transcendency
(versus being all you can be). Whenever you actively enter the flow world, you
become a participant who is recognizing and joining in with what is happening
in the world around you. That takes you beyond yourself and invites a meaningmaking moment, so let’s avail ourselves of all the flow we can.
When Fritz designed his morning well and engaged it with the right mindset,
he was able to catch glimpses of the flow world during his wake-up song, over
breakfast, on his drive to work, and especially walking through the park on the
way to his building. After parking his car, when that tree in full fall colors caught
his eye, he was quickly and deeply absorbed by the tree. He chose to give himself
to the moment (he applied the mindset of availability), which opened up the
flow world to him. He devoted his attention to his surroundings and allowed the
wonder of the colors to take hold of him. He felt the rush of emotion brought
on by beauty, which resulted not only in joy but in a fresh idea for his
presentation later that day—a bona fide inspiration. Fritz’s practice of
acceptance and availability evoked a morning full of simple flow moments that
—especially when strung together—gave him the experience of being more fully
alive, which felt genuinely meaningful.
If some flow is good, more is better, so now let’s design an extended
experience of simple flow. Extended simple flow is perhaps our favorite, because
it offers very close to the same intensity of flow experience as peak flow but is
much more easily accessed. Extended simple flow occurs low in the flow channel,
where the challenge is not very high at all. Even if you are a newbie or underskilled at the task, there isn’t much at stake, so it needn’t be anxiety-producing.



Let’s give it a try with a basic, everyday task—chopping onions for making soup.
(Bill loves chopping his way into flow.)
It’s a very simple activity—just chop up a half dozen whole onions and put
them in the big pot of soup stock that’s simmering on the stove. You just need
the onions to be diced into small chunks—not an exact size. The soup is for a
family event this weekend, and you have no other obligations tonight, so you’ve
got plenty of time and there’s no rush. Time and speed are not an issue. What
could be easier? Just you, a cutting board, a good kitchen knife, and some nice
onions (you wisely got Vidalias and chilled them overnight, so tearing up won’t
be an issue). You know the basics—peel off the outer skin, halve the onion so
you can lay it flat on the cut side, slice it into one-quarter- to one-third-inchthick slices, then dice those slices roughly into cube-shaped chunks. Nothing to
it, really—it will just take some time and a few hundred strokes of the knife.
This is the kind of task that rarely induces flow for most of us—in fact,
usually quite the opposite. This is just the sort of chore that just screams out for
multitasking. Maybe at least we can learn something or be entertained while
stuck chopping all these darn onions, so we turn on a podcast or an audiobook.
That’s not a bad way to chop onions, but it won’t bring on flow.
To transform this mundane chore into a simple flow experience, we must first
choose literally to “go with the flow” by accepting that what we’re doing now is
chopping onions. Period. That’s it. We are a single-minded, 100 percent focused,
dedicated, calm, and methodical onion chopper. No more, no less. Once we’ve
consciously accepted and committed to the task, then it’s time to get started.
First thing—let’s get set up. Now, get out the onions, clean off a good-size
cutting board, and pull out your best kitchen knife. Lay them all out on the
cutting board in an orderly manner and just look at everything. Take a good look
(which costs a whopping four seconds), then pick each item up in one hand and
feel it. Transfer it to the other hand, then back again, and replace it on the board.
Let your eyes and hands become familiar with these three things: onions, board,
knife. Get focused. These onions, this board, and this knife are your whole world
for the next ten to fifteen minutes. There is nothing else you need to attend to.
Nothing else need be on your mind. Every extraneous thought is deferred to
another time.



Spend just a moment directing your attention at each object and appreciate
what brought it to this moment in your kitchen. Those onions were grown by
farmers, harvested, transported, displayed at the store, picked out and brought
by you to this cutting board. These onions have known a lot of people and
traversed a long path. The cutting board has a similar story. Its dozen or more
laminated strips of wood come from at least three or four different types of trees
grown in you’ve no idea what far-flung places. How many years and steps have
gone by in the journey from seed to tree to lumber mill to woodshop on the way
to being crafted into a board for your use here? Lastly, the knife—oh my, but the
knife has traveled perhaps the farthest to get here tonight. Begun as ore deep in
the earth, mined, smelted, cast, forged, and ground, with a beautiful hardwood
handle added, and then the blade honed to a fine edge. Great skill and countless
steps in its manufacture (steps that harken back to the very beginning of
toolmaking) have brought this nicely weighted instrument that feels so good in
your hand to you right now. You suddenly realize that nearly every single morsel
of food that’s ever passed into your mouth has probably encountered a knife
somewhere in its lifetime. Life without knives is just unimaginable, you realize.
Wow. Knives are cool.
The one to three minutes that this little orientation ritual took is a beautiful
example of being as fully available as you can be simply to what’s in front of you.
It takes very little time, but it transforms your awareness and helps you zero in
your attention on just what you’re doing. This is how you build the protection
shield of acceptance we diagrammed that will help you to maintain your
attention to the task of chopping without yielding to the bombardment of
intrusive thoughts brought on by the impatient boredom of your achieving
brain. You are putting your awakened brain in charge of your attention in order
to more fully enter the flow world and gain access to the flow state. This is how
you use your designer’s mindset to become an artful moment-maker. Having
fully accepted your onion-chopping assignment and leaned deeply into it, you’re
ready to begin.
You peel off the outer skin to reveal the pale yellow Vidalia onion and begin
cutting. You halve it and start slicing. One slice, two, three… Then the first
intrusive thought arrives: Hey, did you remember to renew the car registration? It’s



due soon. But you’re ready and respond softly, Thank you for reminding me, but
I’m busy chopping now. I can renew online later. You keep slicing away. After the
fifteenth slice, you start to feel a rhythm in the motion. It almost has a hum to it.
You’re tempted to wonder, How does that happen? but you let it pass and just
attune yourself more closely to the slicing. You actually lean in, tilting your ear
to the board to hear the sound as the blade crunches through the meat of the
onion. Your hand starts noticing how different starting the cut and penetrating
the outer edge of the onion feels from completing the cut and moving through
the body of the onion, then stopping when the blade rocks down onto the
board. You let yourself become immersed in the symphony of images, sounds,
and feelings brought on by the slicing. You can’t catch it all, but without a
thought, you hold your breath to make sure you don’t miss anything.
After all the onions are sliced, it’s time to dice. You close your eyes for about
five seconds, trying to remember what those onions looked like four minutes
ago. Wow—they looked nothing like the pile of yellow half-round slices that
now cover most of your cutting board. Big changes for those onions—all due to
you and your partner, Mr. Knife. The dicing begins. Quite a different
movement. It takes a few tries to get your dance steps down. In no time, the pile
of little onion cubes starts mounting. They’ll soon be spilling all over the floor,
so you grab a bowl and scoop them up to clear some space on the board. The
onions feel totally different in your two bare hands from how they do under the
working edge of the knife blade. They’re a little bit cool and damp, but they’re
crisp, and your fingers can “see” the sharp corners of the cubes. It’s a nice feel.
The knife is keen enough to cleanly slice—not crush—the onions, and your
fingers notice. Back to dicing, and in what seems like no time, you’re done. The
bowl is full. You dump it into the stockpot. The whooshing plopping sound
they make as the avalanche of miniature onion icebergs hits the water is both
surprising and gratifying. And suddenly, it’s over. You toss the trimmings, wash
the bowl, wipe down the board, rinse the knife, and slide it back in the knife
block on the counter.
Seventeen minutes have gone by—but it seemed like an hour (or was it just
thirty seconds?). You aren’t a different person. Nothing really has changed in the
world, except for a few onions getting chopped into soup stock. You haven’t



really learned anything (except perhaps that you like your knife more than you
realized). You’re not really changed at all (except for some reason you are looking
forward to the upcoming family dinner more than you were before). Nothing
much at all has happened. And yet… you feel rather wonderful. Not running
down the street shouting wonderful, not even jumping up and down a few times
in the kitchen wonderful, but… pausing momentarily to look around the room
with a wry smile kind of wonderful. Rubbing your hands together and liking the
feel of them sort of wonderful. You’ve had a chance to simply engage fully in a
simple task that connected you to the real world in your real body doing
something real. You designed a simple flow moment and let yourself live into it,
and that released more of that huge reservoir of aliveness in you. And feeling that
aliveness affirms your humanity, which is inherently meaningful and
worthwhile.
When you return to that simple onion-chopping session a few days later
while doing your Seventh-Day Savoring exercise (coming up in chapter 7), you
notice that what you’re drawn back to is that seventeen minutes with the onions.
It was one of the best times of your week.
There is an old Zen proverb that describes this mindset:
Before enlightenment, chop wood, carry water.
After enlightenment, chop wood, carry water.
In-the-moment and extended simple flow opportunities are lying all over the
place. Whether it’s tidying your room, walking the dog, doing the dishes,
washing the car, buying groceries, folding laundry, even doing your taxes (okay
—neither of us has ever been in flow doing taxes, but we know people who
have). From a transactional-world perspective, you just want to get stuff over and
done with so you can move on to the next thing. Simple things can be
aggravating to the transactional mind because they do take some attention and
some time, but they don’t seem like they deserve either your time or attention. In
the transactional world, simple tasks are just a pain. In the flow world, they’re an
invitation to hidden joy.



We recommend that you allow yourself the delight of transforming simple
tasks regularly by doing them in the flow world with an accepting and available
mindset. If you just look, you’ll discover lots of simple flow moments hiding in
plain sight in your regular life, at home, and at work. Give it a try—we think
you’ll like having more flow be a part of your daily life.
There have been times when Bill has been operating in the flow world for
weeks at a time—simple and peak flow experiences back-to-back. Of course, he’s
also still taking care of the necessary transactions of eating, drinking, getting on
the train, and going to work. It happens to Dave, sometimes for hours, on his
little sailboat with a moderate wind and smooth water. It’s not hard for Dave to
sail in those conditions. It’s nothing like when he crews on a friend’s racing
sloop in big wind and a crowded pack of boats. But when Dave releases himself
fully into the experience, he goes into flow.
It takes some practice, but it’s doable. When you manage your mindset, any
activity—not just the peak ones—can lead to flow. The possibilities are virtually
endless.
Well, almost…
Not Quite Flow
Flow has sufficiently penetrated pop culture that it is sometimes conflated with
other types of experiences in which people become deeply absorbed or lose all
track of time. Some of these flow-adjacent experiences are complementary to
flow, but some are not, so they’re worth mentioning briefly, if only to avoid
confusion.
Mindfulness is often associated with flow, and rightly so. Mindfulness has
become a broad worldwide movement in both personal growth and
organizational development. It has its roots in multiple ancient wisdom and
religious traditions and has gained modern acceptance through practice and
research validation. Mindfulness practices all focus on bringing the individual
into the present moment in a calm and centered state of mind. The premise is
that a state of mindfulness reliably moves an individual toward an authentic
expression of their true and higher self. Accordingly, mindfulness is always



found in the flow world, and the condition of mindfulness reflects many of the
designer’s mindsets we’ve described here—particularly radical acceptance,
wonder, and fully engaged and calmly detached. The state of mind that
mindfulness practices seek to attain includes many of the classical attributes of
the flow state: deep absorption, no sense of the passage of time, and a feeling of
deep aliveness. Many practitioners will state simply that when they are in
mindfulness, they are in flow.
We aren’t going to argue with that claim, and we agree that mindfulness is a
good thing. Becoming proficient in any of the many mindfulness practices is a
definite advantage to someone using the ideas and tools in this book. Being more
mindful—having more control over your state of mind—is consistent with
becoming a better meaningful moment-maker. Some of the flow exercises we
suggest in Try Stuff at the end of this chapter have their origins in mindfulness
practices and traditions. That all having been said, we don’t consider
mindfulness and flow to be equivalent. For one thing, mindfulness is
approached via meditative practices, which are quiet and still—usually inactive
and done individually. Flow occurs typically during an active task or activity and
can be performed either alone or collaboratively. Mindfulness relies on your
attention being directed inward—removed from the outside world. Flow relies
on your attention being outward—engaged with the outside world.
We don’t think it is vitally important whether you conclude that mindfulness
is flow or is just complementary to flow. We do think it’s helpful to have a clear
understanding of what both mindfulness and flow are in order to more
effectively incorporate them into your life and daily practice.
Another case—the use of social media also has been associated with being in
flow. Indeed, heavy social media users by the millions report becoming fully
absorbed, totally engaged in what they’re doing, and losing all track of time
while using social media apps—which many claim as an experience of flow. This
is something with which we disagree—the immersive experience of social media
does not induce a healthy state of flow.
The massive influence of social media, especially in the lives of teens and
young adults, has garnered much attention and extensive research—much of it
focused on its negative, even addictive, impacts. While not attempting to fully



litigate this large and complex topic, we will point out one important difference
between flow and intense social media use: brain states. In her book Dopamine
Nation: Finding Balance in the Age of Indulgence, Stanford School of Medicine
researcher Anna Lembke, MD, discusses how social media over-activates the
addictive dopamine response cycle, resulting in compulsive behaviors that can
lead to depression. Scan research indicates that the dopamine cycle is
predominant during intense social media use. By contrast, the flow state has
been shown to produce alpha waves in the brain (associated with relaxed yet alert
state of being), which are absent in social media use. Flow does not induce
compulsive or addictive behavior, cause depression, bring about a lack of selfcontrol, or create social isolation. No negative outcomes have been validly
associated with the flow state.
Lastly, we will mention video gaming. The relationship between flow and
video gaming is controversial. Some argue that gaming (like active sports) can
produce a healthy state of flow. “Video games are one of the best ways to get into
flow,” said Kate Sweeny, professor of psychology at UC Riverside. Many video
gamers play together on teams, and synchronizing the experience of gameplay
through teamwork is an oft-cited flow experience by athletes in the “real world,”
so why not in the virtual one? Others disagree, pointing out video gaming’s
similarity to social media for potential negative outcomes and addictive
behaviors. The debate is far from resolved and includes a potent mix of opinion
and solid research. We take no specific position on this question other than to
recommend honest self-evaluation. Remember that design thinking is an
empirical approach that only works in reality. Accordingly, we encourage our
video-gaming readers to simply take an honest look at their gaming experience
and assess how it is and isn’t supporting flow states, and their goal of finding
more meaning and experiencing more authentic aliveness. You be the judge.
The flow world is accessible each and every moment, no matter what we are
doing. And in many ways, it’s more exciting than any video game could ever
hope to be.
TRY STUFF



Exercise: Magic Trees (Practicing in-the-Moment Awareness)
Flow at any level and in any form depends on a high level of awareness of the present
moment. In peak flow, the high degree of skill needed to meet the high level of
challenge demands your maximum awareness and so helps establish the flow state.
In less demanding situations, you depend on your mindset to guide you toward flow.
This simple exercise helps you to practice targeting and concentrating your
awareness in order to deeply engage the present moment.
Step 1: Go out in nature or a park with trees on a nice sunny day when it’s calm or
there’s just a hint of breeze (do not do this on a windy day). Find a comfortable place
to sit that affords you a good view of a landscape with full view of a number of trees
(bring a folding chair if you can).
Step 2: Sit down and get comfortable. Find a sitting position that you can maintain
for five to ten minutes. Scan the still life panorama in front of you back and forth
slowly a few times.
Step 3: Let your eyes come to rest on one tree—one with some sky visible through
and behind it. Watch it closely for at least a full minute. Fully take it into view. See all
of it.
Step 4: If you have not already noticed it, look for small movements of the leaves or
branches in the breeze. Keep staring until you find and recognize the motion. [This is
the magic moment. What appeared perfectly still one moment is suddenly full of tiny
but undeniable motion.1]
Step 5: Watch the movement for at least a minute. Catch all the different directions
and pathways of motion. Notice how different leaves or branches move differently
and the varying range of movement as the air shifts.
Step 6: Close your eyes for five seconds, then open them again and try to see the
same tree as being motionless—just the way you first saw it (at the beginning of step
3). Notice how you can’t do it—you can’t see a static tree any longer. The motion has
become inescapable and relentless.
Step 7: Reflect on your experience. Try to recall what it was like when you first saw
the tree (motionlessly) and how you see it now. Notice how much more in the
moment, how much more a part of the actual reality you are sitting in you have
become by noticing the small but magical movement in the trees.
Step 8: For a few days after the exercise, in any of a wide range of typical situations
(sharing a meal with someone, attending a meeting, etc.), suddenly invite yourself to
“see the movement in the trees!”—meaning, look more deeply and enter the moment
more fully. See what you notice and journal on it.
Step 9: Repeat as often as you like to keep your awareness at a high state of
readiness.
Exercise: Flow Walking Meditation
There is evidence that movement is a simpler path to mindfulness than being still. So,
for this exercise, we will give a variation on the Zen walking meditation ritual, best
done in some beautiful natural place—the beach, a lake, the woods, a mountaintop, or
a city park. The goal is to slow down enough to take only mindful, in-the-moment
steps and to fully embrace a moment-to-moment reality.
Step 1—Choose Your Setting: Pick a peaceful, natural place where you can walk
slowly without interruption—a forest path, beach, quiet trail, or a calm city park.



Step 2—Set Your Intention: Before you begin, pause. Take a deep breath and
silently set an intention: I’m here to connect with the present moment, one step at a
time.
Step 3—Walk Slowly and Silently: Begin walking at half your normal pace. Slow
everything down. No need to force a rhythm—let your breath and your steps find each
other naturally.
Step 4—Focus on Sensation: Feel your foot lift, move through the air, and make
contact with the ground. Notice how your weight shifts. Tune in to the textures
underfoot, the air on your skin, the sounds around you.
Step 5—Let Go of Goals: You’re not walking to get somewhere. You’re walking to be
somewhere. If your mind wanders, gently bring it back to the next step. And then the
next. Stop and re-ground your awareness if you get too distracted; start walking again
when you are ready.
Step 6—Use Your Breath as an Anchor: Inhale as you take a few steps. Exhale as
you take a few more. Let your breath keep you tethered to the moment. If thoughts
come, thank them, release them—and return to your breath and next step.
Step 7—Stay for Ten to Twenty Minutes: You can do this practice for as little as ten
minutes or stretch it longer. The goal isn’t duration—it’s depth. When you feel
complete, stop walking, close your eyes, and take three deep, grateful breaths.
Step 8—Reflect Briefly: Afterward, ask yourself: How do I feel now compared to
before I began? Don’t analyze—just notice.
Exercise: Take a Sniffari (Sniff Walk)
Canine behavior specialists suggest that fifteen to twenty minutes of daily olfactory
exploration provides a dog mental stimulation comparable to an hour of physical
exercise, significantly reduces stress, enhances cognitive function, and fulfills crucial
natural instincts. Dogs are olfactory animals, and giving them the chance to broadly
activate their scent-based brains by letting them stop and take in whatever smells
they desire at their own pace makes them happy and enhances well-being. Humans
are largely sight-based animals, but we have other senses as well. We do ourselves a
favor to give our senses a chance to show us more of the world (and ourselves), and
in so doing help us to engage the flow world right in front of us and experience more
aliveness.
Step 1: Plan a twenty-minute walk with no distractions, preferably in an outdoor
area where a variety of smells may be encountered, such as a park or garden, the
mountains, the ocean, or even an industrial area, shopping mall, or urban downtown.
(As your dog will tell you, it doesn’t have to be nice smells—just interesting ones, but
no noxious or toxic smells, please.)
Step 2: Decide where your walk is going to start. Stand still at your “starting line,”
and close your eyes and just breathe normally for a minute. Let your attention shift
from your eyes to your nose. Start paying attention to what your nose is picking up. It’s
time to think like a dog.
Step 3: Once you’ve got scent at the front of your awareness, start walking slowly
while breathing easily and sniffing the air looking for any distinctive smells. As soon
as you pick up a new scent, stop and let your nose zoom in on it to try to fully capture
the smell. If you can name the smell, great, but no problem if you can’t. Just try to
embed the memory of the scent before you move on.



Step 4: Repeat step 3 as many times as you can in fifteen minutes. Be sure to
linger at each scent to try to fully capture each smell and the nuances between
different smells. Don’t rush.
Step 5: After about fifteen minutes, reverse direction and return to your starting line
the same way you came, only now walking “normally” using sight-based awareness
(sight-walking is much faster than sniff-walking, so you won’t need as long to get
back). As you retrace your steps, try to recall the different smells as you pass by each
place where you’d lingered and sniffed. See if you can both “see” and “smell”
simultaneously as you walk back (which demands a high level of engagement with
your present moment).
Step 6: When you get back to where you started, sit down (on a bench, in your car,
or just on the ground), close your eyes, and savor your walk for one to two minutes.
Take note of how you feel and your sense of aliveness and well-being. Make an entry
in your journal if you maintain one.
Variations: Try this exercise using sound or even touch. We caution against doing it
with taste out in the wild, but you might be able to do it with taste at a farmers market
or a street fair with lots of food vendors. Just give yourself a chance to experience
being more human by using more of your human faculties.
WARNING—Don’t Attempt to Keep Up with Your Dog: If you have a dog, you may be
tempted to take your dog on a sniffari (a great idea) and see if you can just go along
smelling the scents that your dog is sniffing out (a lousy idea). A dog’s sense of smell
is ten thousand to one hundred thousand times more sensitive than yours, and dogs
can sniff five to ten times per second—five times faster than you. So… don’t go there.
We’re just unable to keep up with what our dogs are smelling.
Exercise: Peak Flow Reflection
We can’t really give you an exercise to achieve peak flow. Peak flow requires you to
perform an activity that demands your maximum skill to respond to a high level of
challenge. That’s something we can encourage you to seek, but we can’t prescribe it
for you. You’ve got to find that activity and develop that level of skill yourself. Again—
we’re big advocates of peak flow and encourage you to develop activities where you
experience it. After hundreds of hours invested in the studio, Bill regularly can achieve
peak flow with his brush in his hand working on a big canvas. After thousands of
miles of careful skill-building, Dave can drop totally into the zone on his motorcycle
zipping through serpentine turns on tight mountain highways. So please do find your
all-absorbing, skill-demanding activity and let the peak flow flow.
While we can’t give you an exercise to achieve peak flow, we can give you an
exercise to get more out of it or even reprise it, which brings us to the Peak Flow
Reflection:
Step 1: Recall a time when you experienced a high level of peak flow while doing
something that demanded your full skill and attention. (It can be anything—sports,
performing music, working on a totally absorbing project, etc.)
Step 2: Remember the experience, and as much as possible, don’t just remember it
but reenter it and relive it again, as close to the original experience as you can. (In
some instances, you can actually reenter the flow state if you let your imagination fully
take you into the experience. This is great when it happens, but don’t expect it
regularly.)



Step 3: As you relive it, slow it down in your memory and let yourself experience it
even more deeply than you were able to when it actually happened at real-time speed.
(This exercise is a variation on the Seventh-Day Savoring exercise in chapter 7.
Savoring is a powerful tool that can be used in a variety of ways.)
Step 4: Write your relived reflection (preferably by hand).
Step 5: Read the reflection over to yourself, once silently and then a second time
out loud. Notice any differences between those two readings.
Step 6: Later that day (or within a day or two), read it aloud to a friend, partner, or
family member. Notice how the story feels when read to someone else compared to
just reading it to yourself.
Step 7: Add any notable observations from the readings in steps 5 and 6 to your
written reflection.
Step 8: Lastly, just take a moment to hold that flow moment and enjoy it one more
time—more fully than ever.
Exercise: Flip the World Switch
See the Flip the World Switch exercise in chapter 3. It provides a quick and easy
technique for making a simple flow moment in almost any situation.
Exercise: Just This (Extended Simple Flow)
This exercise provides the step-by-step process for making an extended simple flow
moment out of doing “just this” as illustrated by Bill’s onion-chopping example.
Preparation
Step 1—Define the Task: Select what task/activity you’re going to do. Be specific.
Choose a good “just this” activity that is simple and leaves room for you to engage
with it thoroughly and without undue thought or stress.
Step 2—Set the Stage: Gather all the needed equipment (if any) and have it ready to
go at the appointed time for your activity. Be sure you have access to the space
necessary to do it. Prearrange things so nothing impedes the activity once it begins.
Step 3—Make Time and Clear the Decks: Schedule time for the activity. Leave plenty
of time so you needn’t rush and your activity won’t conflict with anything else you or
others may be doing. Anticipate and eliminate possible interruptions so that once the
activity begins you can do it nonstop without disruption.
Execution
Step 1—Get Ready, Get Set: At the appointed time and place, arrange whatever
materials and tools will be needed for the activity (e.g., onions, cutting board, knife,
counter space, apron, bowl).
Step 2—Go with the Flow: As you start the activity, close your eyes for a moment and
dedicate yourself fully to be in this moment and participate in doing just this and
nothing else. (This is where you erect your shield of acceptance that you’ll use to hold
off intrusive thoughts and resist distraction.)



Step 3—Stop, Look, and Appreciate: Before you begin the activity, let your eyes and
hands gaze at your setup and appreciate what was involved in bringing everything
together for this moment. Let that appreciation focus your attention and activate your
availability to lean into what you’re about to do.
Step 4—Start: Begin the activity. Give it a little time to get your rhythm going.
Step 5—Be the Ball and Get into It: Intensify your focus as the activity continues.
Think less about what you’re doing and more just be what you’re doing. (This is what
sports psychology means by “be the ball”—don’t think about what you’re doing; just
attend to the doing of it.)
Step 6—Direct Your Spare Attention—Take the Sensory Tour: The activity is simple, so
you will likely be unable to put all your attention into just doing it. Accept that fact and
be available to direct your spare attention in a way that enhances flow potential. For
example, don’t ask yourself, “Am I using the knife the best way?” Ask instead, “How
exactly does the knife feel throughout the slicing motion?” To direct spare attention to
what you’re doing (instead of criticizing or analyzing it), “take the sensory tour.” Allow
each appropriate sense to take in what’s happening. How does the knife feel? How do
the onions look and smell? What is the sound on the cutting board?
Step 7—Finish: Continue the activity until it’s done (either the task is completed or the
allocated time is expired).
Step 8—Stop, Look, and Appreciate: Repeat step 3, now reflecting on the completed
activity and any results thereof (e.g., diced onions simmering in the pot).
Step 9—Celebrate Your “Just This” Particularity: Cap off the experience by
celebrating the simple joy of having participated in this particular activity. Clap your
hands or congratulate yourself out loud—or, if possible, share it briefly with someone
else.

1

Dave has been doing this exercise for decades and has never not found the moment
when the trees seemingly suddenly come to life to be anything other than miraculous.
It always astonishes him once he sees the movement how he could have ever missed
it—and how clearly more attentive he is when he’s fully aware of the motion.

7

Designing Formative Communities
There’s something powerful about being known—not just your name but your
hopes, your fears, your story. Now imagine being in a roomful of people who
understand what you’re striving for, what’s weighing on you, and what lights
you up. People who are genuinely glad to see you, who want to hear what’s on
your mind, and who are ready to support you if they can. That kind of
connection isn’t just rare—it’s transformative.
This magical place where everyone knows your name and is happy to see you?
It’s what we call a formative community.
For more than seven years, Dave has worked with the Distinguished Careers
Institute at Stanford. DCI, as it’s called, is where a cohort of thirty-five to fortyfive people anywhere from the ages of forty-five to eighty-five come together for
a year to explore ideas and think deeply about their post-career lives. The
participants (referred to as fellows) are distinguished in their careers, whether in
business or education or activism, and come from a wide range of backgrounds
and cultures. Some have money, and some don’t. Some have advanced degrees,
and some don’t. It’s a diverse group. Over the course of the year, they take lots of
classes, meet Stanford professors and students, and participate in workshops, but
cohort after cohort report that the most powerful experience of the year they
spend together isn’t about the classes, or workshops, or brilliant professors they
get to interact with.
It’s about the community they form. And year after year, they all report
they’ve never had anything like it.



Not ever.
Which boggles the mind when you think that many of them have built
incredibly successful lives and organizations. They’ve been a part of building
healthy corporate cultures. They have large networks of friends, personally and
professionally. Most of them have families they are close to. These are people
who have lots of people in their big, full lives—lives they self-report as being
deeply networked and well supported for decades.
Asking his “tell me more” question, year after year, allowed Dave to discover
what it is that these DCI fellows are finding in their Stanford cohort that is so
different from all their other highly successful community experiences. Based on
this work, we’ve concluded that people typically form groups for one of three
very different reasons: to have a good time together (a social community); to get
something done together (a collaborative community); or to become better
together (a formative community). It turned out that while the DCI fellows had
lots of experience in vibrant social and collaborative communities, most of these
people had never been in a formative community until they came to DCI. And
they loved it.
What distinguishes a formative community is that it’s not transactional.
Collaborative communities have a clear goal—hit that sales target, complete that
project on time, improve that group of students’ test scores. Working hard
together on a shared goal is very life-affirming and satisfying and a necessary part
of almost everyone’s lives. For many people, the bonds formed with
collaborators with whom they’ve labored long and hard on big challenges—like
starting a company or building a house or serving in the military—are the
strongest relationships of their lives.
Social communities exist for the members to enjoy one another’s company
and share experiences. While the ultimate purpose of the group may be
relational, the practical focus is frequently on the actual events shared (e.g., the
dinner out, the road trip, the movie night, which are each a type of transaction).
At the end of most social community gatherings, the participants know if the
activity “worked” or not depending on whether everyone “had a good time.”
Such social communities are wonderful, and sharing and enjoying life together
with others is an essential and meaningful aspect of life. However, the time



invested, while enjoyable and even life-giving, may not leave the participants
better. And that’s where a formative community comes in.
A formative community is distinct, offering unique benefits—something the
DCI fellows discovered repeatedly. Most groups center around shared content,
like technology in a software company or screenwriting in a film class. Formative
communities, however, focus on shared intent—the collective aim to become
more human. This emphasis transcends career paths, expertise, or interests,
fostering diverse communities across ages and backgrounds.
DCI fellows pursued vastly different passions, from economic justice to AI to
poetry, yet they weren’t united by a transactional or performance-based goal.
Instead, they shared a commitment to helping one another grow into more
authentic versions of themselves. Their purpose wasn’t to “win” at DCI but to
support one another in leading purposeful lives. This formative community
existed solely for that reason—and its impact was profound.
Simply put, a formative community is one where members help shape one
another. The critical elements of a formative community are:
Everyone has made a core commitment to growing their way forward to
become more coherently and authentically human, and to help others do the
same.
There are regular and consistent interactions—the group spends time
together communicating and sharing experiences.
There is a shared construct or methodology that guides the process the
community uses to sustain itself.
The group persists for a sufficient period of time to form trusted
relationships.
Getting More, Becoming More
A primary claim of this book is that more meaning is waiting for you right there
in the life you are already living. Our suggestion is that by applying a designer’s
mindset and instituting some of these practices, you can get more out of life
without having to cram more into it. The chief purpose of a formative



community is to do just that—help the community members get more out of
life. The foundational regimen that enables a formative community to deliver on
that promise is a simple three-step practice: Engage-Reflect-Storytell (E-R-S):

Description 11

This is the practice that all members of a formative community group
perform on an ongoing basis and which informs the community’s conversation.
Engage simply means go out in the world and do stuff. Go to work, feed the
kids, have some flow, read a book, put on your wonder glasses, take your
neighbor some flowers, do the dishes.
Reflect is the next step in the process. Set aside time, typically weekly, to
reflect on what you’ve been doing. Review your week and journal about your
experiences, especially those that shine a little brighter in your memory. Perhaps
during the week some questions came up, and maybe you had an insight or a
moment of wonder—write it down. You don’t have to be long-winded or
profound; you just have to write down an honest, regular reflection.
Reflection is the critical step. Without it, nothing that follows will work.
With it, everything falls into place. While we’re not sure we agree with Socrates
that “the unexamined life is not worth living,” we do know for sure that regular
reflection is crucial to getting more out of life and building a successful
formative community. Why? Because a formative community meeting is like a
potluck dinner—everybody needs to bring a dish to share so everyone gets fed.
The “dish” each member brings is their reflection, which isn’t hard to prepare—
it only takes a few minutes a week, but it makes all the difference. And once
prepared, you share it by telling your story.
Storytell is the next step in this circular process. The story we’re
recommending you tell is simply the storyline of your ongoing reflection. A

[Embedded visual 1 on PDF page 152 (694x581 px). OCR-readable text: ag]



formative community is the place where that story gets told and nurtured. It’s a
place not only where others hear you but where you can hear yourself. When
you hear your own story as you share it with others who are receiving it
graciously and you see your story being regarded in the welcoming faces of your
compatriots, your story becomes more meaningful and real to you. This
community-based practice of Engage-Reflect-Storytell empowers the designer’s
mindset of create your world. When you actively take responsibility for what the
unfolding story of your life is, your story becomes richer and your world more
interesting. Telling that story in community is an important part of making it
real. We are social animals, and we can’t experience the fullness of ourselves by
ourselves. The stories of wonder, coherence, and flow that you tell and the
questions about life you disclose become more real and more valuable through
the group’s validation. The listeners benefit as well. Listening empathically to
another person’s stories of meaning-making moments invites us into something
precious in that person’s life—it makes us more human. It’s so simple, and it’s so
transformative.
If a formative community group did nothing more than regularly perform
the Engage-Reflect-Storytell practice, it would be a thoroughly worthwhile
experience. But it can be so much more.
Remember the Be-Do-Become model and the definition of the human
person as a becoming from chapter 2? A formative community is simply a group
of becomers walking alongside one another in their journey of becoming. Just
what is it we’re becoming? Simply our more authentic, more coherent, more alive
selves. We’ll never be done becoming. Isn’t that great?! That means that life is
going to stay interesting forever, as long as we pay attention and stick to our
practice of designing a more meaningful way to live in community.
Living the Question
For some people, life is about rehearsing their answers—figuring out how to
effectively apply what they understand and what they know in whatever
situation they find themselves. For others, life is about living their questions—
discerning what new inquiry may move them along to the next lesson that life is



inviting them to learn. People in a formative community are intrigued to
discover and explore those next questions and next learnings. They’re not only
open and available; they are also actively looking for the next invitation. They
like to enjoy life now as much as anyone (in fact, they’re experts at getting more
out of the present moment), but they don’t stop there. Their commitment to
“living the question” is what fuels them and keeps them going on the journey of
becoming. We call the particular question you are living into at any particular
time your “focus question.”
Your focus question is the question that life is posing to you now about who
you are becoming during this season of your life. The time frame of a focus
question can be as short as a few months to as long as a few years or even a
decade. The idea of a focus question is a new one for most people—and best
understood by example.
Meet Harmony. At forty-nine, she was an accomplished international
executive and entrepreneur with a long track record of building things that
matter. After years in the fast-paced start-up world, she took a leadership role at a
mission-driven US nonprofit—a way to stay grounded while her two sons
finished high school. The plan was to press Pause, catch her breath, and be
present for this final stretch of hands-on parenting. But as her youngest prepared
to leave for college and the house grew quieter, something stirred. A new idea. A
