spark. The beginning of what just might be her next big leap.
Harmony found herself at a crossroads, facing a major life and career
question: Should she go all in on her new start-up idea—and return to those
sixty-plus-hour workweeks—or stay in her fulfilling (but less stimulating) thirtyfive-to-forty-hour-a-week corporate role? With her youngest about to leave for
college, she knew she’d soon have more time. But where would she invest it—
into her career, or into her homelife, especially her marriage? Her relationship
with Dale was solid, but after two decades of kids and careers, it had often taken
a back seat.
On the surface, the choice seemed straightforward: Stay the course or leap
into the start-up. But the truth ran deeper. The clock was ticking—if she didn’t
move on her idea soon, someone else might. Nearing fifty, Harmony felt at the
top of her professional game, and if she was ever going to build something new,



now was the moment. At the same time, she also felt a deep pull toward her
marriage, knowing this next season—post-parenting—was often a make-orbreak stretch for couples. She wanted to create something meaningful with Dale,
something fresh and intentional. But she also couldn’t shake the feeling that this
new company idea might be a once-in-a-lifetime opportunity. And that tension
—between building something new professionally or investing more deeply in
her personal life—was exactly where she stood.
What to do?
Harmony’s situation helps us discern an important distinction between two
separate things: a presenting problem and a focus question. Let’s take them one
at a time. A presenting problem is just that—a problem that life has presented to
you that demands a solution. Presenting problems are usually transactional in
nature and can vary anywhere from minor to life-changing in scope and impact,
and almost always feel rather urgent (if not emergent).
Most of us upon encountering such a problem want nothing more than to
solve it, and the sooner, the better. In Harmony’s case, the presenting problem
was simple: which career path to take—stick with her nonprofit corporate job or
launch the start-up. The way to resolve that problem would be to analyze which
is the better option by working through all the relevant issues: Which one’s more
interesting? Which one has a bigger impact in the world? Which one makes more
money? Which one is a better use of my skills? Which one could be deferred to a
later time more easily? Which one is more likely to succeed? And so on. These are
all appropriate considerations, but they are largely transactional issues and
represent transactional-world thinking. While “Which job?” is an important
thing to resolve, that problem-solving question won’t help Harmony get at the
bigger life issues at stake here.
Focus questions get at not just what’s the best option or how something will
work but where you are headed and who you are becoming. Stepping back and
looking at her situation through the lens of “Who am I becoming?” completely
reframed Harmony’s thinking. She realized that if she was going to dedicate up
to the next ten years of her life to her next career move, then she’d be exiting this
job at sixty. It struck her that she may very well not take another big-career job at
that age, so this next decision could be the biggest career decision of the rest of



her life. That realization opened up what issue was actually most at stake for her
in this decision, which wasn’t her career. Harmony knew that she tended to
throw herself 110 percent into her work, so where she worked, with whom she
worked, and what she was working on all had a profound impact on her
personal development and growth. That meant that who she would be by the
age of sixty would be hugely influenced by what type of work she did in the
coming decade. These reflections formed Harmony’s focus question to be, “In
this next crucial decade of my life, how do I want my career to contribute to who
I will be at sixty?” Having that question foremost in her mind for the next six
months helped focus her attention on what really mattered. It enabled her to
assess what her work life meant to her and identify what she loved and didn’t
love on the intense days at work that were more start-up-like. It reminded her to
tune in to her marriage and imagine what she wanted with Dale. As is often the
case, there was no right or wrong answer—just very different ways to live. She of
course continued to do her due diligence on her options, but her focus question
kept her attention on what truly mattered.
In one of her formative community meetings, someone made the
observation, “You know, you keep talking about who you’ll be at sixty. I’m
wondering, given what a priority you say the marriage is for you in all this, what
if your question was, ‘What will we be like at sixty?’” That feedback nudged
Harmony to better hone her focus question (which is just the sort of
contribution a formative community can make). It led her to ask Dale more
about how he felt about the start-up option—and not just what he’d like to do
together if they had more time. To her surprise, Dale said, “Actually, I think it’d
be okay if you did it. Sure, I’d love to spend more time with you now that the
kids are out of the house. And start-ups are hard—really hard—but my favorite
thing is to see you shine. You are just amazing when you’re creating something,
and the truth is, you love this new idea of yours. No, I won’t like seeing less of
you for the next few years, but the version of you I will get to see is worth the
wait. I’d like to enter our sixties married to the happiest version of Harmony
there is. It’s up to you, but I’m with you either way.”
In Harmony’s example, her presenting problem (which career path to take)
and her focus question (who she wants to be at sixty) were closely linked, which



is often the case but not always. The presenting problem facing Greg, one of our
former students, was which grad school admission to accept—the one from the
top-rated private school with no financial assistance or the one from the public
school with a generous scholarship. Which school to attend was an important
problem to solve, an important transactional question to answer, but it wasn’t
his focus question. As part of his grad school evaluation, he’d spoken with lots of
recent alumni who were five to ten years into their careers. What he heard in
those conversations that made the biggest impression on him had very little to
do with which graduate program the person had attended and had everything to
do with the quality of mentoring they’d received during and following grad
school. Many of the alumni he met described how they would not be the person
they had become were it not for the support and wise input of their mentors. So,
his focus question was, “What is mentoring uniquely able to teach me, and how
do I find good mentoring?” Living thoughtfully into that question over the next
few years proved to have a profound impact on his life, especially in terms of
forming his professional ethics and decision-making process. Meanwhile, he
solved the transactional problem of which school to attend based on finances.
Once he concluded that what he most valued in his education came not from
the classroom but from mentorship, he was free to simply get the most costeffective, competent training available, so he chose the public school for its
affordability. Greg’s example paints a clear distinction between an important
transactional problem to be solved (which grad school to attend)—which, while
important, is not formative—and a genuine focus question (what mentoring
could mean for him)—which, when lived into, can and will be powerfully
formative. Presenting problems deserve to be solved, but they do not deserve to
take all our attention, and far too often, they do exactly that. Most of Greg’s
classmates spent all their energy on which grad school to attend, assuming that
picking the right school was all they had to do to “make it.” They were just
solving their next problem. Greg was taking charge of forming his life.
Choosing to pursue a life of becoming—a life of ongoing personal growth,
learning, and maturation—takes effort and focus. That’s what a focus question
is there for—simply to help us keep looking and moving ahead by not only
solving problems but by becoming our better selves.



The Double Agenda
If what we’re recommending is that you identify your focus question, live into
it, and use it as a key tool in sustaining an effective formative community, then
why are we discussing all these presenting problems as well? Because, just like
Harmony and Greg, we all have them. Problems are unavoidable and will
constantly vie for as much attention as they can get. The result is that problems
can easily hijack your attention away from your focus question. Socrates
reminded us to live the examined life, but he didn’t have to remind us to solve
our problems, because problems constantly remind us of themselves. We are
making the distinction between your presenting problems (found in your
transactional-world life) and your focus question (found in your flow-world life)
so you don’t lose sight of your focus question. Focus questions only rarely have a
deadline, like in Harmony’s case, so they are easily deferred to another day and
soon forgotten. It’s easy to feel like the big problem is the only thing we can
afford to be thinking about, but if we let problem-solving consume our lives,
we’ll end up years later the same person we always were—just with a stockpile of
solved problems. If you want to become a better, more interesting, more
authentic version of yourself, have a more meaning-full life, you need to preserve
some time and energy for your formation. Becoming is never urgent, though it is
crucial. Focus questions almost never have a deadline, though they are always
timely. The only way your formation will ever get any attention is by choosing to
make it a priority. That said, we can’t ignore the problems either—presenting
problems are important and demand to be addressed. Which is why all formative
community groups need to serve a double agenda: formation and problemsolving.
The primary purpose of a formative group is to help the members get more
meaning out of their current lives and support one another’s ongoing becoming
projects. In addition, the group can be a great resource to provide support and
insight for problem-solving when necessary. It’s only natural to bring life’s
challenges to these trusted people who you know have your best interests at
heart. The key is to not let the problem-solving and advice-giving dominate the
conversation. If that happens, your formative community may unintentionally



turn into a collaborative community—one gathered around the task of helping
to get one another’s presenting problems solved. That’s not a bad thing! Having
a team of dedicated problem-solvers committed to making your life better can be
wonderful. But it’s not the same as a formative community in which we
explicitly are committed to furthering one another’s becoming our better selves,
not just our solved selves. So, do help one another to solve those unavoidable
problems that arise, but stay vigilant to protect and nurture each other’s
becoming and progress in addressing your focus questions. Don’t keep falling
for the beguiling next problem. Life has an endless supply of them, and they’re
not all as urgent as they claim, so tell some of them they’ll have to wait. Expect
the right balance between the formative conversation and the solution-seeking
conversation to shift depending on the members’ circumstances and manage
accordingly. As long as the formative conversation is kept front and center in the
group’s practice and never abandoned, the group will continue to be a
meaningful experience. Groups that slide off into all problem-solving are
helpful, but they’re not meaningful. Helpful is fine, but it’s more meaning and
purpose that we’re really after, so let’s keep our eyes on the prize.
Welcome to the Collective
Dave has seen how valuable formative community was for the people
participating in the Stanford DCI fellowship program. But those people took an
entire year off to think about their future, take classes, roam the campus, and
have lots of engaging conversations. Could regular busy people build formative
community, too? Our work with a DCI alum, Baden, showed us that the answer
to that question is a resounding yes.
After completing the DCI program, Baden’s formative community group
continued meeting on Zoom, because they all valued the experience so much.
Baden loved it and was sure that lots of people could benefit from being in such
a group, even if they couldn’t take a year off at Stanford. Baden was involved in
entrepreneurship and venture investing, which brought him into contact with
lots of different people. He kept asking anyone who would listen, “Are you
interested in talking about where your life is going and what you’re learning?



Would you want to be in a community that focused on those sorts of things?”
And many of them did. So, Baden invited them to be a part of a new community
that he just called the Collective. He didn’t push it; he was patient and wanted to
see how many and what kinds of people were interested. After a few years, there
were over three hundred people in the Collective, and very different kinds of
people from all over the world: college kids, retirees, bankers, tech geeks,
accountants, small-business owners, big corporate execs, singles, parents—you
name it. And they were all people who wanted to grow and learn from one
another and find more meaning and purpose. The Collective started slowly—
putting on a few webinars, hosting some regional meetups, and so on.
Eventually, Baden came to Dave and asked him to run a six-month pilot
program of small formative community groups of Collective members. Dave
jumped at the chance to see if formative communities worked as well for farflung, busy working people as for DCI fellows.
Baden put Collective members in intergenerational groups of five or six
people who didn’t know one another, spanning a wide range of ages, walks of
life, and cultural backgrounds. All they had in common was the desire to try
being in a formative community to get more out of their lives and progress as
growing, becoming people. The participants were all trained on the Designer’s
Way mindsets and the key ideas in this book. Dave assigned everyone to do their
compass (chapter 5) and to start their Engage-Reflect-Storytell (E-R-S) practice
by doing regular reflection journaling. In each group’s first meeting, members
shared their compass and the story of why they joined the group and what they
were hoping for out of the experience. Everyone found that conversation very
stimulating and was ready to come back for more. Next, he assigned them to
write their focus question and discuss with their groups how they chose their
question and what “living into their becoming” meant to them. As the weeks
progressed, Dave encouraged the participants to do the Coherency Sightings Log
(chapter 5) regularly, to try the Put On Your Wonder Glasses exercise (chapter 4)
and do Sudden Savorings (in the Try Stuff section of this chapter) to
complement their ongoing E-R-S practice.
It took a while to get comfortable with the formative agenda of “just”
noticing more and getting more out of life and not collaborating to achieve



something or make decisions. Most of the groups noticed how presenting
problems came up regularly and could sometimes dominate the conversation.
Eventually, all the groups developed a solid rhythm and balance and discovered
that telling their reflection stories first and putting the problem-solving at the
end was an effective approach. An interesting trend became clear over time. Both
the problem-solving and formative, reflective conversations were engaging, and
either would easily take all the group’s time if allowed. However, when problemsolving was given extra discussion time, the ideas didn’t get much better, they
just took more time. When focus questions, reflection, and becoming were given
more time, the conversation got deeper and more satisfying. The conclusion was
that deep conversation gets deeper, and shallow (even important) conversation
stays shallow. The moral of that story: Identify worthy problem-solving topics
up front, but discuss them last after prioritizing time for formative conversation.
As long as the groups maintained a healthy diet of formative time, the experience
was life-giving and meaningful. People in the pilot started reporting how the
community was impacting their lives. For instance…
Alain, a hard-charging MBA, was in the midst of a thorny process of taking
over the family business from his dad. He was having a difficult time getting his
dad to step back. Then things changed unexpectedly when he did the compass
exercise and started the practice of taking coherency sightings. He noticed that
while his father was quite different from himself, his dad had clear values and ran
the company very coherently. That made Alain more grateful for his dad and
more appreciative of the legacy of integrity his dad had built. What the
coherency sightings did was redirect some of Alain’s attention, which was
entirely focused on transactional concerns about the company, toward the flow
world of what was actually going on with his dad and the staff. Without saying a
word, that shift in Alain’s mindset changed not only what he was seeing and
noticing but changed how he was being present to his father and the employees.
It eased the tensions that had been building up between Alain and his dad.
Then, without being asked, his dad started to loosen his grip on the reins and
began handing over responsibilities to Alain.
Rona, an independent financial adviser who had been hard at work building
her business for twenty years, wondered if she’d finally grown the firm enough



and no longer had to constantly try to expand. She decided that her focus
question was, “If I accepted that I am successful—that I have enough—what
freedom would that give me?” A few group meetings after sharing that focus
question, she told this story:
At the end of the day last Wednesday, I got a call from one of my more
difficult clients. I’ve never really enjoyed working with him, but for years,
I’ve given him extra attention in hopes of growing his account. As I picked
up the phone, my focus question came to mind. Suddenly, I knew. I knew
that the freedom I wanted to exercise was the freedom not to work with
this client directly. I politely told him that it was good to hear from him
but I couldn’t talk because I was leaving the office for the day. Then I told
him that I was doing a reorganization and I would no longer be handling
his account. Sarah on my staff would be taking care of him from now on,
and she would get back to him next week. Then I said goodbye and hung
up. Then I cried. I had never done that before in my entire career—taken
care of myself instead of the client—and it was about time. Then I took
myself out to dinner.
Pax was a third-year college student. He wasn’t sure what his focus question
should be, so he decided just to reflect for a few weeks on “What am I
becoming?”—a question he’d never asked himself before. What he concluded
was that at nineteen, he was already becoming a failure. There always seemed
more to do than he could get done, and he was pretty sure things would stay that
way, because it didn’t look like life after college would be any easier. The voice
inside his head barked, I’ll never achieve my way out of this! While relating this
story to his group, he realized that “what I need to become is more forgiving of
my limitations,” so he started working on applying the designer’s mindset of
radical acceptance, including accepting himself. In very short order, he was
enjoying life more than he had in years.
Harmonic Resonance
Those are the sorts of stories we hear all the time about the transformations, big
and small, that can happen in a formative community. In a matter of weeks,



people in Baden’s small group pilot project were helping one another have more
meaningful lives and to live into becoming their better selves. One reason a
formative community has this sort of impact in people’s lives relies on
something we call harmonic resonance.
Scientifically speaking, if two things in proximity share the same natural
frequency and one of them is vibrating, the other one will start vibrating, too.
For example, two tuning forks will vibrate in sympathy if they are tuned to the
same note, the same frequency.
In a formative community, the “frequency” that we’re trying to get the group
to vibrate at is human coherency and authenticity—each individual acting and
becoming more fully themselves. Whether it’s in sharing a small but meaningful
moment of flow or announcing a breakthrough insight related to a focus
question, those stories produce the resonant frequency of someone being more
in tune with the world, others, and themselves. The sound of someone else’s
authentic resonance can animate our own. This is why it is the intent
(authenticity and coherence), not the content (e.g., politics, climate change, or
parenting teenagers), of a formative community that makes it a powerful
experience. It wasn’t important that Alain’s, Rona’s, and Pax’s stories of growing
and becoming were so different in nature. What mattered was being in
community with people sharing their progress in living more meaningfully.
The Long Game
A common question that arises is: “How long can we expect to keep this up?”
Just how long can a formative community continue to be useful and stimulating
to its participants, particularly when it doesn’t have any clear transactional end
goal in mind? Well, with the right people and the right commitment—for
decades. We know this to be true, as we have both been in such a group almost
all our adult lives.
A few decades ago, Bill joined a group of men who get together every Sunday
to talk about being a man in the modern era. Bill knows the exact date because it
was the same year that his son Ben was born, 1994. The coincidence is no
accident: Bill joined this group of men, most of whom are older than he was,



seeking advice on how to be a good father and raise a healthy son. This formative
community has been life changing for Bill.
Dave formed his group TD3 (Tom and 3 Daves) in 1974 before graduating
college. Their purpose was to support one another in coherently living out their
values until the day they die. TD3 celebrated their fiftieth anniversary with a
weeklong retreat during the year we were working on this book.
With nearly a century of experience between us in these kinds of groups, we
speak with a deep and highly informed conviction that a strong formative
community can go the distance with you in life. Both of us will say that our
participation in these groups is one of the single most meaningful and impactful
things we have ever done. If you are fortunate enough to be a part of building
such a group, it can make a tremendous difference in your life.
It’s well worth it. So, let’s get started.
TRY STUFF: PART 1—DESIGNING A FORMATIVE COMMUNITY
Starting a formative community group isn’t as hard as you might think, and… it’s fun!
While a group of four to six is nice, a group of three works beautifully. So, all you need
to do is find just two other people who are willing to give it a try. (You can make a pair
work, but a threesome is much more dynamic, so try for at least three people if
possible.) Remember, your group members don’t have to have a shared interest or
activity (e.g., golf, pasta-making, or Japanese sci-fi novels), or be in a similar life
situation (e.g., married with kids, in college, recently retired), or be similar ages; they
just need a shared desire to get more meaning out of life and a willingness to reflect
on their experiences and participate authentically in community. It’s great if they’re
geographically close enough that you can meet in person, but it’s not critical. Most of
the formative communities we know meet on Zoom very successfully.
We suggest a low-key approach, taking one step at a time. There’s no need to rush
or push it—take your time and enjoy the process. First, just list all the people you know
who might be willing to have these sorts of conversations. If you’re like most people
we’ve met, a few likely candidates will quickly bubble up to the top of your list. Then
over the next few weeks, be on the lookout for an opportunity with each of those
people individually to mention that you’re thinking of starting a formative community
group to discuss what makes life more worthwhile and share meaningful experiences,
and are wondering if they’d be willing to give it a try. Tell them that it’s not a big
commitment; you’re just going to prototype it for a while and see how it goes. We
recommend three to six months with meetings of sixty to ninety minutes every two or
three weeks. It’s hard to build trust if your meetings are four or more weeks apart, and
meeting every week is too demanding for most people. An every two- or three-week
frequency is the sweet spot for most groups. A good prototype should last for at least
six meetings (nine is better) to afford the group enough time for community to form



and develop a rhythm to your practice. If you’re clear about your initial invitation and
the commitment isn’t too big and scary, we think you’ve got a very good chance of at
least finding two other people who are willing to join you for your prototype. Once
you’ve got your participants, it’s time to start meeting.
Meeting #1: Get Organized: In your first meeting—which is great to do as a dinner if
you’re all local—your goal is just to get to know one another, review what the group is
for and how it works, and confirm who’s going to participate. Be clear what the
members need to do for the group to succeed and what the initial assignments are,
and answer any questions (as best you can). After everyone is clear what’s involved,
ask if they’re game to be in the prototype group, and if two or more say yes—you’ve
got a group!
Lastly, set the schedule for your meetings, which is usually the hardest part. It’s far
easier to do scheduling in person than by email, so do your best to have a schedule in
place before that first organizing meeting ends. We can’t overemphasize the
importance of committing to the schedule. Everyone is busy, but making time to meet
is critical. If participation in the early weeks is weak and spotty, the group formation
will suffer and may never recover.
Start-Up Homework Assignment: You want to establish some common ground
right away. You want everyone to arrive at the next meeting with two things:
their compass (see chapter 5)
their focus question (or a few alternative draft focus questions for group
discussion)
Meeting #2: Lay the Foundation: This meeting is dedicated to hearing one
another’s compasses and discussing focus questions. The goal is that everyone
learns what matters to each member and understands where everyone is in their
journey of becoming. This meeting provides a crucial foundation that enables the
members to actually become a formative community—one that supports each
member authentically. With the initial assignments completed and shared, the group
should now begin the Engage-Reflect-Storytell practice that will be the bread-andbutter of future meetings. Remind the members that your meetings are “potlucks,”
where success depends on everyone bringing something to share from their E-R-S
practice.
Meeting #3 and Thereafter: By the third meeting, the group rhythm and momentum
should be building. You can work from the agenda provided below but expect that
your group will evolve how it spends its time. Just be sure to watch out for everyday
problems encroaching and taking too much time away from the formative community
and focus question conversation. Make a habit of regularly checking in with the group
on what’s working and what needs adjustment.
Last Prototype Meeting: At the last meeting of the community for your group
prototype, take stock of how the experience has been for everyone, and discuss where
you want to go from here. Does the group want to continue, and if so, for how long? Be
flexible; each group handles future commitments differently. One of the DCI alumni
groups committed right from the start to continue meeting every week, indefinitely.
Another group of early-career young adults that Dave led would only commit to six



weeks at a time, but did so over twenty-five times and stayed together for nearly three
years.
Formative Community Meeting Agenda
This agenda covers all the elements that may arise in a formative community
meeting. It presumes the meeting runs ninety minutes and is easily adjusted for
anything from one to two hours. Not all elements would appear in all meetings;
notably, the focus question update may not happen in every meeting, and problemsolving occurs only as needed and not dominantly.
Check-In (1–2 minutes each, 3–10 minutes total)
Three words that characterize how I am as I arrive today.
Any individual requests for what to address at today’s meeting (e.g., a problemsolving request or an important update).
Engage-Reflect-Storytell Sharing (20–30 minutes)
Each member shares one to three key reflections they captured since the last
meeting and what insights or questions arose from them.
While listening during this time, each member is keeping track of:
Things said to which you’d like to ask, “Tell us more about…”
Things surfaced which seem interesting to discuss together
Things that are confusing or new to you that you’d like to understand better
When everyone has completed one cycle of “storytelling” their reflections, the
invitation is made: “Who heard something they’d like to hear more about from a
member or discuss further as a group?”
If there are a number of such inquiries, the group simply picks where to go next
and how much time to give each theme.
Enjoy the discussion, keeping track of the use of time.
Focus Question Updates (10–20 minutes)
The following prompt is provided to the entire group to open this part of the
discussion: “How is our focus question impacting our lives? What do you hope
may come of living into this question well in the coming weeks and months?”
Any member who has an update restates their focus question and gives their
update, including any of the following:
How they have been “living their question”



How they have been living their question
New insights on their focus question
Progress, regress, hopes, or issues related to their growth in this area
Possibility of modifying the focus question or starting a new one
Group discussion with the sharing member(s) as deemed useful
Problem-Solving (0–15 minutes)
Ask if any members have a “presenting problem” that they’d like to tell the group
about or for which they’d like to obtain assistance or feedback.
Determine how much time to allocate to problem-solving. (This can also be done
during check-in to be aware at the start of possible time demands for the
meeting.)
Discuss as helpful to the members.
Community Status Check (10 mins—do every few meetings)
Discuss how the group is doing: what’s working, what’s not, what if any changes to
make.
Check Out (2–5 minutes)
Confirm schedule and logistics for the next few meetings. Adjust meeting
appointments as needed.
Each member departs with “As I go today, what I’m taking away is… [ ] and what I’m
looking forward to is… [ ].”

TRY STUFF: PART 2—EXERCISES FOR FORMATIVE
COMMUNITY MEMBERS
Here we provide a number of exercises that members of formative communities can
use individually to complement the core Engage-Reflect-Storytell practice described in
this chapter. You may choose to make some of these exercises part of your regular
practice or you may use them occasionally to provide value and variety to sustain you
in your pursuit of getting more out of your life and your ongoing journey of becoming.
Exercise: Reflection Component of the Engage-Reflect-Storytell Practice (E-R-S)
Engage-Reflect-Storytell as described in this chapter is the baseline practice for
formative community members. It’s what the individual members regularly journal on



as they pay attention to their lives to recognize meaningful moments day-to-day. A
wide variety of things might appear in this reflection journal, including:
Flow moments, including peak flow experiences or simple flow (extended and
momentary)
Put on Your Wonder Glasses observations (or any Wonder experiences)
Mystery noticings
Coherency Sightings Log
Savorings—either the Seventh-Day or the Sudden variety (see below)
Focus question insights or encounters
Mindset moments—times when you were able to apply any of the five designer’s
mindsets in a way that made a difference, or insights about how they work for you:
wonder
availability
radical acceptance
fully engaged and calmly detached
create your world
and especially our favorite twofer: radical acceptance and availability
Exercise: Seventh-Day Savoring
Here’s a simple four-step exercise you can do weekly. We recommend making it a
regular practice to get the most out of it.
Retreat
Find a quiet spot where you can sit comfortably for five to ten minutes, either at a
table or with a surface you can write on (preferably by hand, but typing is okay if
you prefer).
Close your eyes and just breathe for a moment. Take at least three or four full,
calming breaths to slow yourself down and be glad that you’re alive and have this
quiet moment to yourself.
Review
Now, while still keeping your eyes closed, let the last seven days flow before your
mind’s eye. As you do so, look for two to four moments in your week that you are
attracted to and grateful for in retrospect.



Note: Beware of being “attracted” to problems, conflicts, forgotten to-do items, and
the like. The mind loves to get caught up in that stuff. When that stuff appears
(and it will), just tell yourself, I’ll get to that another time, and let it go. Don’t fight it
or attempt to resolve it—both of which will steal all your attention. Just
acknowledge it, then let it go and return to your reflection. And yes, this takes
some practice to get the hang of.
As you review your week, being attentive to those two to four moments, very briefly
jot down just one or two words for each such moment that stands out, so you
won’t lose it. Cheerful grocer or Finished essay or Appeased boss.
Reflect and Savor
When you’ve got your list, look it over.
Fully savor each of those moments again—getting the most out of them. Try to not
only remember but reenter the moment. The idea of savoring is to get more out of
the experience by giving it more time and attention than you could when it
happened. Savoring is a great form of celebrating the particularity of an
experience.
If one moment particularly pulls your attention, feel free to journal a bit further
about the experience. Nothing long or dramatic—just an entry to capture that
experience.
Reinforce and Retain
Then reinforce your reflection by saying to yourself something like, I’m really glad
for these things. With these in mind, it was a good week.
This step is putting the create your world mindset to work to improve the quality of
your experience of your life. That’s it! And it really does take only five to ten
minutes.
Bonus Step—Insights
If you notice that any of the moments you identified are offering an insight or a
learning you want to capture, write that down, too.
Insights don’t always happen, but they’re lovely when they do, so be sure and get
yours when they’re available.
Bonus Step—Tell Your Story
One way of getting the most out of your life is by giving it away—and you don’t
have to wait for your formative community meeting to do it.
If others in your household are doing this exercise, too, you can share your
reflections.



Most of us get asked, “How’s it going?” pretty often. You could insert a story from
your Seventh-Day Reflection in your answer. “It’s going pretty well, actually. Why,
just last week, the checker at the grocery store ran out to the parking lot to return
the credit card I’d dropped. How great is that?”
Exercise: Sudden Savoring
After you’ve done the Seventh-Day Savoring exercise a number of times and have
developed some facility for “savoring,” try Sudden Savoring. Sudden Savoring just
means doing a quick but deep dive into savoring a moment right at the time it occurs.
It may seem difficult, but once you get the feel for it, it’s surprisingly easy. And it feels
great!
Kevin has been a cherished mentor to Dave for decades. Kevin, in his nineties as
we wrote this book, was widely appreciated as a very kind and wise person. Many
people in addition to Dave would seek Kevin’s help, so Kevin spent a lot of time having
a coffee with someone struggling with being fired, getting divorced, receiving a cancer
diagnosis, or just being terribly confused or discouraged. Somewhere during those
many difficult coffee conversations, Kevin would famously pause, look the person in
the eyes, and say, “Gosh, I dunno. That sounds like a very tough situation. I’m not sure
what to say, but I do have one question. [pause] How’s the coffee?” The person would
bewilderingly say, “What?!” And Kevin would repeat, “How’s the coffee?… The coffee
right there in that cup, the one in your hand. How is it?” Kevin would persist until they
stopped, slowly took a sip, and actually noticed what the coffee tasted like. And it
tasted good—usually far better than they had yet noticed. Kevin was tricking people
into a Sudden Savoring, which had two immediate benefits every time. First, it gave
them a chance to enjoy their coffee. It created a little moment of joy—a scandalously
particular moment—amid a difficult time. Second, it grounded them and brought them
back to the flow world of the present moment, where the big problem always seemed
less scary.
It’s nice when someone named Kevin is there to remind you to savor the moment
you’re in, but you can remind yourself (and you needn’t wait for a crisis to do it). Hint:
Sudden Savoring is a technique to create an in-the-moment simple flow experience
that’s worth remembering. They can be found in all kinds of situations like:
Over a cup of coffee
Petting the dog
Someone letting you go ahead of them in the checkout line
Smelling the tree as you step out of the car
Noticing the spotless glass as you pull it out of the dishwasher
Hugging your mom, partner, child, friend, neighbor, or someone else
Reading a beautiful sentence in a book



All that you have to do is recognize that you’re enjoying something. As soon as you
do, tell yourself, Oh! I love this! Don’t miss it! and immediately stop and close your eyes
for two to three seconds—really, that’s all it takes—and give your complete attention to
that one thing. Let that coffee taste or pine scent or soft fur wash all over and through
you for just that brief moment and take a snapshot of it in your body that you can pull
out later for further reflection.
That’s it! Once you start doing Sudden Savorings, you’ll do them all the time.

OceanofPDF.com



8

Ages and Stages
People often ask if our approach to meaning-making changes based on one’s
“stage of life.” Is it easier to find meaning and purpose later in life? How is it
different in your teens and twenties? What do we need to do if we want to build
an interesting and purposeful life over the long haul?
Forget “perfect timing.” Meaning is a design project you can start today. Just
remember: The questions that preoccupy you at twenty won’t be the same at
seventy. Life unfolds in seasons, so pay attention to the season you find yourself
in and be aware of and curious about what’s coming next.
Staying aware of your life stage lets you put the designer’s mindsets in
context, and that can be a big help to living and enjoying a more meaningful,
purposeful life in any and every stage. So here are some stage-related tips we’ve
picked up along the way that we hope will help you to get the most out of life as
it unfolds.
We affirm the widely held perspective—detailed well in two excellent stage-oflife books: David Brooks’s Second Mountain and Richard Rohr’s Falling
Upward—that life essentially falls into two different halves. The first half of life
is spent finding your place in the world and crafting an identity—building a
strong container for your life, as Rohr puts it. In the second half, you shift from
building a self to giving that self away by identifying what’s actually in that
container you’ve carefully constructed and pouring it back out to the world. Put
in Maslow’s terms, the first half is about pursuing self-actualization, and the



second half is moving into self-transcendence—first we build a self, then we
transcend it.
The Odyssey Years
During the first half of life, the decade from roughly eighteen to twenty-eight
defines what psychologists call emerging adulthood and we call the Odyssey Years.
How you navigate these years is an important part of your first half of life.
Luckily, because we get to teach Designing Your Life at Stanford, we get to
spend a lot of time with some terrific college students who are right in the
middle of these powerful, dynamic, and sometimes turbulent years.
We encourage our students to spend their Odyssey Years exploring the world
and deepening their understanding of themselves, their relationships, and what
matters most. This time of life is when the young adult is being formed,
culminating in the full development of the prefrontal cortex (PFC)—the part of
the brain responsible for planning, problem-solving, decision-making, and, most
importantly, self-control.
In terms of meaning and purposefulness, emerging adults often desperately
want to discover their passion (and you know how we feel about the passion
problem). While a few twentysomethings find a passion early, most people
remain on that search well into their thirties or forties, which we remind them is
totally normal, and even then doesn’t guarantee finding that “one true passion.”
Most people have multiple passions that evolve over the years, and frequently
arise only after working in a field long enough to grow enough appreciation for
it to become a passion. Passion’s great—but don’t wait for it.
These are also the years of intensely forming one’s convictions. It’s not only
okay, it’s necessary to have strong opinions and to care very deeply about things
as an emerging adult. Those strong feelings are the on-ramp to the coherent life
—a life organized around living out your values. While those values and
concerns will change over time, now’s the time for becoming committed to the
value-centered, coherent life.
A number of important appetites are grown in this time of life, and most
important among them are curiosity, wonder, and coherency. The Odyssey Years



are the perfect time to curate your curiosity, which is the doorway to wonder.
Allow yourself to dabble in all manner of things and learn to be a learner. Let
yourself be easily amazed and make wonder a habit. If you exit your twenties
having become an expert at putting on your wonder glasses, that capability will
pay huge lifelong dividends in accessing the flow world and meaningful
moments.
A warning is that the opposite can also be true—this is a time when
skepticism and negative mindsets can take hold. If you enter your thirties with
no compass for wayfinding, have lost your curiosity, and have become a stranger
to wonder, a harsh road may await you. Powerful systems (like business,
government, academia, and medicine) run the transactional world. If you enter
that world without your own compass, the system will gladly provide you one of
its own making. Following someone else’s compass makes you a passenger of
your life, not the author of it. If you exit emerging adulthood having lost track
of wonder, you may never find your way back to it decades later when you really
need it and can finally revel in the fullness of it in the second half of life.
Investing in curiosity, wonder, and coherence during the Odyssey Years will
lay a strong foundation on which to build a meaningful life for many decades to
come. So lean deeply into curiosity, search out experiences of wonder, and strive
to articulate your values and live coherently by them. These are the big tasks of
becoming during this season and, if attended to well, will help you get the most
out of those years and the years to come.
The First Half
After the Odyssey Years, the rest of the first-half years are full of challenges. It is
during this stage that Richard Rohr talks about building your life container.
This is the container that defines who you are and where you stand in the world.
Basically, it’s what you’re all about—the combination of your story, your
Workview, and your Lifeview (chapter 5). Crafting that container is hard,
necessary work. Much if not most of that work is done in the transactional
world as you build your work and career and assemble your network of friends,
colleagues, and family. The emphasis is on getting stuff done and making



something of your life (however you define that). And it takes time. That
transactional emphasis tends to come with a nonstop sense of urgency. It seems
like everything takes too long, so we are constantly pushing to speed things up.
But a lot is happening in these first-half years with work, career, family,
relationships, children, community, and naming your mission and goals for life.
These things take a few decades to complete, so pace yourself. Don’t let the
urgency distract you from the joy of this season. These very productive years can
be deeply gratifying and meaningful, if you remember to pay attention and catch
the many wonder-full moments when they appear. You’ll pass this way but once,
so remember to make the most of it.
The encouragement we want to give to those of you in the first half of life is
to keep moving and not get lost or stuck. We wrote this book to address a
concern that we kept hearing again and again from people in their thirties and
forties—that they were working hard, and even successfully, at designing and
building their lives, but they still weren’t experiencing the kind of meaning and
purposefulness in those lives that they were hoping for. As we heard their stories,
it sounded to us like they were accidentally (though quite understandably)
getting stuck in their first-half-of-life thinking. For modern people in a postinternet, AI-accelerated world, the first-half-of-life project can easily become allconsuming and all transactional. The first half’s hyper-focus on producing and
achieving has made impact the primary if not sole form of meaning-making
many people recognize. Being busy isn’t the problem. As we’ve seen it, losing
sight that there is anything else in life is the problem.
There’s more at stake here than just the sadness of missing out on the gifts of
the flow world during these years. This relentless focus on constant production
morphs from a merely lopsided lifestyle trend to an ideological conviction.
Serious people living worthwhile lives are getting stuff done, and the best and
brightest are using technology to do more faster. The mantra of more faster starts
to become a mindset—even a kind of moral code—that perniciously works its
way into every domain of modern life. Worst of all, this mindset is insatiable, so
it carries its adherents along year after year, well into the second half of life and in
so doing delays or even prevents the rest of the journey of becoming fully
human. If you’re learning the same lessons at sixty that you learned at forty,



there’s a good chance you overran the finish line of the first half and just kept
going. Keeping a more balanced perspective isn’t hard to do, but it is easy to
forget. That’s where some well-chosen practices from this book and
participating in a supportive formative community can make a world of
difference.
During these go-go years of work and possibly family, just remember to
design a life that includes regular access to the flow world, that makes room for
wonder, and appreciates coherency along the way. You don’t need to dedicate
lots of time to these things—just enough to keep your awakened brain awake
and celebrate the scandal of particularity. Many of the exercises in the preceding
chapters can be done in no time at all, and you’ll find that small investments of
time and attention given to the flow world can reap huge psychic benefits. Try it
—we guarantee you’ll feel the difference in no time.
The second recommendation we have for this first-half stage is to get your
compass calibrated and form the habit of living coherently. While the Odyssey
Years are the time to start forming your Compass Values, it’s in the thick of these
first-half years that your compass gets calibrated against the push and pull of
reality in adult life. Now is the time when necessity will very likely demand some
compromises be made. Perhaps creativity is important to you, but the only job
you could find in the media world was account management. Is creatively
managing your customer relationships and promoting creative media (but not
actually making it yourself) good enough, or do you feel you need to find a
different job altogether? These aren’t easy questions, but they’re the ones we face
in the first half. When they appear, take them head-on and work it through so
that you have confidence in your own coherency. In our work, when someone at
the age of thirty-eight or forty-five suddenly looks up and finally admits to
themselves they’ve lost their way, exclaiming, “Omigosh! How did I get here?!”
it’s usually because they abandoned their compass early on—or never calibrated
it in the first place. Having a set of values is easy; applying them coherently in the
real world can be harder. Don’t shy away from the task or put it off to next time.
Get your compass calibrated and take coherency sightings regularly, especially in
your thirties and forties.



This is also a great time to invest in a formative community and to find
mentors to help you navigate the tough choices when they arise. Both of us
remain deeply grateful to have had great mentors who helped us stay on track
when things got confusing during our early careers and young family years.
The Second Half
The second half of life is about moving from self-actualization into selftranscendence—the peak of Maslow’s revised pyramid. This follows the rule,
“First we must build the Self, so that we can transcend the Self.” Going back
decades if not centuries, much has been written about the second half of life,
and never more than as the huge baby boomer generation began entering their
sixties and seventies (with Gen X and millennials coming up close behind). If
you are entering this phase of life, we encourage you to sample the many books,
TED Talks, podcasts, workshops, and articles on the subject and discover what
resources are most helpful to you. While this is not a comprehensive treatment,
we do want to highlight some of the unique gifts and design opportunities to be
found in this stage.
When we’re talking about the second half of adult life, we’re talking about
the life that awaits us after a big shift in the human journey of becoming—the
shift from role to soul. In Connie Zweig’s lovely book The Inner Work of Age:
Shifting from Role to Soul, she describes the special invitation of late life as an
opportunity
to discover an advanced stage of human development that is hidden in
plain sight—the shift from role to soul. This phrase was coined by
spiritual teacher Ram Dass, a Harvard psychologist who returned from
India in the 1960s and became a renowned guide and bestselling author.
He describes this shift in identity from the active roles that we have
fulfilled during our lives to something deeper, something connected to a
spiritual essence that has inherent value and does not depend on our
productivity, accomplishments, or self-image. Ram Dass calls this spiritual
essence loving awareness. Whether we call it soul, Spirit, Higher Self, or



God, when we begin to identify with That, we begin to become who we
really are. With this next stage of development, we can unearth the
treasures of late life.
Not everyone chooses to make this shift, and while it usually happens in
one’s fifties, it can start anywhere from thirty-five to eighty-five, depending on
the mindset and personality of the individual. The famous psychiatrist and
researcher Carl Jung spoke extensively about this change. He called it going from
life’s “morning” to life’s “afternoon.”
Thoroughly unprepared, we take the step into the afternoon of life. Worse
still, we take this step with the false presupposition that our truths and
our ideals will serve us [as before] going forward. But we cannot live the
afternoon of life according to the program of life’s morning, for what was
great in the morning will be little at evening and what in the morning was
true, at evening will have become a lie.
Jung suggests that second-half living is different from the first-half living, so
the tools and tricks that worked in life’s morning probably won’t work as well in
the afternoon of life. Remember philosopher James Carse’s discussion of finite
and infinite games—where we play finite games by the rules to win versus
playing with the rules in infinite games to get to keep playing? Using Carse to
explain Jung, what we’re saying is that the first half of life is playing a finite game
(winning the project of building the container of your life) while the second half
of life is an infinite game (staying in the never-ending game of enjoying each
particular moment for itself). Going from being an effective finite game player to
an artful infinite game player is what the big midlife shift is all about. That may
sound a bit daunting, but if you think on it for a moment, it’s wonderfully
encouraging news. Carse and Jung and countless wisdom traditions before them
are telling us that the second half of life is a refreshingly new chapter with new
gifts to give and new experiences of meaning and purposefulness in store for us.
It stands to reason that a new approach is needed to make the most of these new
discoveries.



Our first tip: Don’t stumble your way into second-half living. (Skip the
midlife crisis.) Stumbling into midlife happens when we have become so
preoccupied with first-half living that we ignore the signals that the game is
changing. Signals like your body is past its natural peak; you’re the one at work
with more answers than questions; your kids are going through puberty (it’s one
of life’s ironies that we go through a second puberty just as our kids are going
through their first); and “winning” or “succeeding” just doesn’t pack the same
punch it used to. When these things start appearing in life, don’t turn a blind eye
and just hit the gas to accelerate into your old life (by going to the clubs more, or
buying a new wardrobe, or trading up to a faster car). Lean into this new
perspective and listen to what your life is starting to tell you.
The secret to doing midlife well is simple—it’s our old designer’s mindset
friends: acceptance and availability. If we accept that the second half is coming
and make ourselves available to it and open to learn from it, we can make the
most of it. If we just try to keep doing what we’ve done before, we’ll get stuck in
the first half and find it more and more frustrating. If you keep a little curiosity,
wonder, coherence, and community going, you’ll be well positioned to skip the
crisis and sail into your second half.
The chief feature of the well-lived second-half life is freedom. With the
container-building project of your first-half self largely completed; you are now
free to put your attention on other things. You are still involved in the
transactional world and probably very busy, but in this stage, you more and
more get to pick what you say yes and no to. If we come to our second-half life
with a healthy trust in ourselves and fully accepting the life that we’re in—
successes, losses, compromises, joys, and all—our capacity to notice and pay
attention to our experience of life in the moment (and not just our progress on
our goals) takes a big leap forward. That capacity can become a superpower.
Our second tip: Get good at using your flow world–vision superpower. Like
Superman’s superpower of X-ray vision, second-half people have the superpower
of flow-world vision. In chapter 3, we talked about “How to Be in Two Places at
Once” and provided suggestions for how to quickly transition from the
transactional world to the flow world and back again. This is much easier to do
in your second-half life because your maturity and wisdom enhance and focus



your vision. You can see the flow world much more easily because you have put
the transactional world in its place—it isn’t taking up all your attention and
awareness. Remember that the flow world is always happening everywhere.
Every moment has a flow moment available in it. With this superpower, you
experience the reality of the flow world coursing along just below the surface of
the transactional world, and you are able to access it anytime you want.
Dave’s friend Foster is a perfect example of someone in this stage of life.
Foster works hard at living in the flow world as much as he can and is able to
keep his awareness of and connection to the flow world even while being busy in
the transactional world. Foster calls his experience of the flow world “eternity”
(i.e., the never-ending now of the present moment). Dave recently asked Foster
about how it was going. “Oh, I’m just having a great time being an eternal
person these days,” said Foster. “I’m enjoying trying to do eternity all the time
now.”
Foster isn’t a monk sitting under a rock in the desert; he’s a small-business
owner in his fifties, with a wife and four kids in high school and college. He goes
to work at his industrial hearing testing company every day and schedules the
mobile testing trucks on their routes. He deals with happy customers and upset
customers. He makes sure to be available for his kids’ after-school sports
competitions, and he swings by the grocery store for some eggs on the way
home. Foster is a regular guy with a regular life. He just happens to live it almost
continuously in the flow world, which is his version of eternity.
Foster uses the designer’s mindsets, especially radically accepting his life, so
that he is fully engaged and calmly detached from whatever he is doing. He
worked hard to build the container of his life in his thirties and forties. He has
run his business for twenty-plus years, so he knows what he’s doing in his career.
He’s worked on his marriage and being a dad for a couple of decades and is
definitely hitting his stride in his family life. He’s done the work of life’s first half
and, most importantly, accepts the life that he’s built—which frees him to live
his life much more deeply and meaningfully because he’s no longer distracted by
having anything to prove.
Now, don’t get us wrong. Foster is a pretty advanced second-half kind of guy.
Dave considers Foster one of only two or three genuinely enlightened people



he’s ever known personally, but Foster will tell you it’s not really that hard or
that complicated. Radically accept your life, look for and see the flow world right
in front of you, choose to be available to it, and voilà—you’ve let your flow
world–vision superpower free you to master the art of being in two places at
once—the transactional world and the flow world.
Foster is a perfect example of someone who really knows how “to get more
out of, not cram more into” his life. This is the single biggest learning and
awareness of this stage of life, and one reason that study after study shows that
well-adjusted second half of lifers are the happiest people.
Our final second-half-of-life tip: Embrace elderhood. Dedicate yourself to
acquiring the wisdom that comes with being an elder. Take full responsibility for
maximizing the learnings and growth uniquely available in the latter years of the
second half. One good way to engage that opportunity well is to ask big focus
questions as you begin this part of life’s journey.
As an example, for their fifty-year anniversary retreat, Dave’s TD3 formative
community developed a set of focus questions that they used to launch their
shift to elderhood as they entered their seventies:
As you enter elderhood, what do you need to let go of (roles, children,
powers, etc.)?
At this stage, how much is enough (money, professional contribution,
service, etc.)?
What in you is most resisting this change, and what in you is most welcoming
it?
What invitation is life extending to you as you become an elder?
On each day of their five-day retreat, one of the guys led the group in a deep
discussion of one of these four questions, then on the last day, they all had to
answer, “Now, what are you actually going to do? What will you prototype as
you shift into your elderhood well, and how can we support you as you do it?”
Bill’s men’s group (all of whose kids have long ago grown and gone) is asking
similar questions, and so are the hundreds of DCI fellows that Dave has worked
with over the years. This kind of deep conversation can make a huge impact on



everyone, and choosing the right questions to focus on can make a tremendous
difference. Great questions lead to transformative answers.
All of which leads us back to this chapter’s original question. Yes, the stages
of life matter for all the reasons listed, but living a meaningful life can happen at
all ages and stages.
Parenting and Partnering
While parenting and partnering aren’t “life stages,” per se, they are very
important parts of life for many people. We’re not experts in these fields, but
after interacting with hundreds of couples in our life design work over two
decades (not to mention having over two dozen kids and grandkids between us),
we do have a few suggestions about how to apply the tools and ideas of this book
in these important relationships.
For parents, our top recommendation is to focus on helping your kids enjoy
and savor childhood, not try to speed them into adulthood. Put another way,
avoid the Transactional World Trickle-Down Effect. Parents of young children
are themselves right in the middle of the first half of life and busily—very busily
—working hard to successfully navigate the many demands of the transactional
world. They want to compete effectively and win as many of the finite games life
has them playing as possible. It’s only natural for them to direct their kids’ lives
the same way they’re directing their own. While quite understandable, it’s not
helpful. That tendency has produced the most performance-oriented and
success-driven children in the history of mankind, and it’s been a disaster for
childhood. Children are naturally at home in the flow world. They thrive in the
present moment and are at ease with wonder. The essential activity of the flow
world is play, and children are terrific at play (the flow world is just one big
infinite game). If we put children in the right environment and let them do what
comes naturally, the simple flow of childhood happens.
In his highly acclaimed book The Anxious Generation, NYU social
psychologist Jonathan Haidt makes the important distinction between a screenbased childhood, a recent invention with myriad negative effects, and a playbased childhood, which enables healthy individual and social growth and well-



being. It’s a stunning conclusion that the centerpiece of Haidt’s corrective for
what ails modern childhood is the absence of play. We couldn’t agree more. Play
puts a child in the flow world. The flow world evokes much of what makes us
human, so play is a parent’s best friend. Resist the Transactional World TrickleDown Effect that, left unchecked, pushes every aspect of every person’s life—at
any and every age—toward more and more production.
Defend your child’s right to be a child. Defend your child’s right to be in the
flow world.
And don’t just transport your kids to the flow world and back; go with them.
There’s a huge difference between just visiting your child’s world and entering
into it with them. It’s easy to let the transactional world try to edge its way in,
but playing with your kids is way more fun for both of you and far more
formative for the child. What’s not to like about that?! And if you take this
approach, your child won’t be the only beneficiary. You’ll receive a lovely and
unexpected gift from your child—the Children’s Flow World Trickle-Up Effect.
Having lots of flow-world experiences with your children isn’t just good for
them; it’s good for you, too, as it enhances your own capacity for participating in
the flow world. There’s no experience of parenting quite as sweet as letting your
child grow you into a better person. In healthy families, we’re all part of one
another’s becoming.
For couples, whether partnership or marriage, our suggestion is simply that
you strive to make your relationship a formative community. This may seem so
obvious as not to be worthy of mention, but it won’t happen on its own, and as
it’s an incredibly powerful strategy, it warrants some attention. There are lots of
popular myths and memes about love. We’ll point out two in particular that
stand in stark contrast to formative community. The first archetype, which has
fueled countless love songs, sings a familiar refrain, “I love you just the way you
are, darling. Please… don’t ever change!” This romantic sentiment is entirely
understandable. When your epic quest for love has finally found your heart’s
desire and you’ve fallen madly in love, of course you want that magic moment to
last forever!
The second example is its opposite—where one person is not in love with
who the other person is but loves who they could become if only a few critical



changes take place. Life will be wonderful just as soon as he starts reading better
books and has something interesting to say to my clever friends, or as soon as she
plays better tennis and learns to love camping. And so begins the (invariably
doomed) relationship-long project to fix or improve the lacking partner.
These two classic love memes may be clichés, but that fact hasn’t stemmed
their popularity. Both these misperceptions make the same mistake. They take a
static, frozen-in-time view of the other person, whether in the present or the
future, that denies and eventually competes and conflicts with that person’s
ongoing becoming. While most real-life couples’ relationships are much better
than either of these caricatures, the flaw in these clichés points out a critical
aspect of healthy love—the importance of fully embracing growth and
becoming. Many couples have a solid social relationship in which they quite
enjoy each other’s company, and a collaborative relationship in which they help
one another in building a life and family, but too seldom do couples truly attain
a formative relationship committed to one another’s becoming.
Growing a genuinely formative relationship goes beyond simply accepting
that things will change over time and dealing with those changes as they appear.
A formative partnership does not merely accept change; it expects change and
even catalyzes it. Formative love is grounded first and foremost not in my view
and hopes of you or in your view and hopes of me, but in our view and hopes of
Us. A formative love trusts that the love that arises between two people actually
forms a new thing—an Us. This Us is love’s gift and emerges authentically from
the best of each person.
The invitation to a formative couple is to nurture the becoming of each
individual wholeheartedly and without reservation and in so doing nurture the
becoming of Us. This may sound a bit elusive, but we suspect you’ve seen what
we’re talking about. We’ve all been in the presence of couples who, when they’re
together, create a space, an atmosphere, that is inviting, open, and loving. A
space that the couple is at home within and where you feel welcomed as well.
That’s the kind of Us we’re talking about. And while growing such a
relationship is clearly a lifelong aspiration, we think it’s more accessible than
most couples realize.



The way of building a formative couple partnership or marriage follows the
exact same process and tools as we outlined for a formative community in the
last chapter. It depends on each person committing to their own ongoing
becoming by paying attention to their lives and actively living into focus
questions that keep them moving and maturing. It includes regularly
communicating with one another what we’re learning, what we’re wondering
about, and how we’re growing. It doesn’t take a lot of time. It does take a
mindset committed to one’s partner’s growth and becoming and a discipline of
personal reflection and communication. We’re confident that developing the
habit of sustaining a formative community conversation as a couple will reap
deep benefits in supporting the growth and becoming of your Us.
TRY STUFF: TOOL TIPS FOR DIFFERENT STAGES
This chapter provides insights on how the ideas and tools from the book apply in
different stages of life. We aren’t adding any new tools here, but we do have tips for
where select tools provided in the prior chapters apply at different stages of life. Here
is a checklist of tips:
Your Compass (chapter 5)
Developing your compass, especially articulating your Compass Values list, is
important during the Odyssey Years to establish the habit of coherency.
Compass Calibration (chapter 5)
During the first half of life, regularly recalibrate your compass, especially as life’s
demands and obligations require renegotiating compromises.
Sharing how your compasses are evolving is a good way for couples to engage
in formative community conversations that grow the foundation of their Us.
Put on Your Wonder Glasses and Museum Magic: Curating Your Curiosity (chapter
4)
Doing these exercises in the Odyssey Years can help preserve the vibrancy of
your curiosity and availability to wonder as you transition from childhood into
adulthood where the transactional world may try to dull these sensibilities.
In the second half of life, these exercises can become particularly life-giving
with your broadened perspective on the world (and more time to enjoy them).



Coherency Sightings Log (chapter 5)
This is an important practice in your first half, to help keep you focused on living
an authentic life and experiencing the joy of your (many!) coherent moments.
Flip the World Switch (chapter 3)
Use this technique regularly in the first half as a way of ensuring your availability
to the flow world, despite being immersed in the transactional world constantly.
In the second half of life, strive to be able to access this maneuver at almost
any time. Work to see how long you can sustain being flipped into the flow
world while still maintaining your activity in the transactional world (e.g., during
a meeting with your tax accountant or while washing the car) as a way of
learning to be in two worlds at the same time as much as possible.
This trick is a great way to quickly enter into the present moment with your
child.
Got To–Get To Reversal (chapter 2) and Sudden Savoring (chapter 7)
These two quick and readily accessible tools are a good way for parents to
transform many of the seeming chore-like requirements of parenthood into lifegiving moments (e.g., I get to push my child on this swing).
They’re also both great tools in the first half of life for quick flow-world momentmaking to refresh you during a long transactionally demanding day.
Take a Sniffari (chapter 6)
Parents—don’t miss this terrific family-outing activity!
Second half of lifers—this makes a great exercise for those more regular long
walks you’ve finally got the time for.
Focus Question (chapter 7)
Supporting one another’s thoughtfully discerned focus question is a powerful
support to a couple wanting to build a formative community.
Engage-Reflect-Storytell Practice, E-R-S (chapter 7)
This practice is highly recommended lifelong. It’s our essential response to
Socrates’s wise counsel to live the examined life—a key aspect of “getting more
out of life without having to cram more into it.”



OceanofPDF.com



CONCLUSION

Living a Meaningful Life
If you’ve made it this far, congratulations—you’ve just completed a crash course
in what might be the most important design challenge of your life: how to solve
the wicked problem of living a meaningful life.
We’ve offered you a radical proposition: Meaning isn’t something you find;
it’s something you design. Not someday. Not after you land the dream job or
unlock the ultimate purpose. But now. Daily. In the midst of real constraints,
real people, and real-life messiness. It’s deeply human work to make meaning—
not in the big picture but in the granular, the particular. Not in theory but in
action. We’ve explored new mindsets, dismantled dysfunctional beliefs, and
practiced reframes that shift everything.
We can all live a meaningful life right here, right now. We don’t need to wait
for more time or better circumstances (or even more or better books). None of
our lives will ever be perfect, but meaning-making happens in the way we attend
to the million imperfect and particular moments that make up our hours, days,
weeks, months, years, and decades.
Together, we’ve covered a lot of ground—from curiosity to wonder, from
transactions to flow, from an achievement-driven life to one that maximizes
meaning-making. We have explored a point of view of the radical acceptance of
reality. We understand that we can be fully engaged in this radical reality while at
the same time be calmly detached. We’ve encouraged you to grab every
opportunity you can to make moments of wonder, coherence, and flow, and to
build formative communities that enrich your life and the lives of your beloveds.
If you put the ideas and tools you’ve just acquired to use, we are confident that



you can and will begin to experience the joy of getting more out of life and the
freedom of no longer having to cram more into it.
Remember that there is no finish line. There is no done. There is no enough.
The risk with a book like this is that it can sound like we’ve got it all figured out.
That if you just understand these concepts and implement these tools,
everything will be great and you will dance happily into all your coming sunsets
with joy in your heart and meaning emanating from your soul. That hasn’t quite
happened for us, and it probably won’t happen for you.
And that’s fine.
Remember the scandal of particularity? The crazy truth that all ultimate
things like beauty, truth, and love are never fully realized and can only be
experienced in small partial particularities like a birthday cake, a kind letter, or a
well-timed kiss? Well… here’s one last example of it.
Your life.
Your life is a particularity—one small, specific instantiation of a much larger
and more mystical reality called You. There is no such thing as completely doing
You. That’s why Maslow’s first idea that self-actualization, defined as being all
you can be as the apex of human development, was wrong. By radically
accepting the limitations of our finite lives, including the relentless and necessary
demands put on us by the transactional world we can’t live without, we are freed
to enjoy what is right in front of us—to get more out of life without
compulsively having to keep cramming more and more and more into life.
We know you want to make an impact, to make things better. Better for your
family, your community, for yourself and your coworkers, for the climate and
the planet, and for all of us. We encourage you to keep working hard at making
things better. We hope you are successful, but regardless of how well or how fast
or how successful you are at making things better—our most fervent hope for
you is that you know more meaning and joy now, right where you are.
Which brings us right back to where we started—the perennial problem of
meaning and purpose. In chapter 1, we told you we are not in the business of
trying to resolve what the meaning of life is, nor can we tell you how to find your
one true purpose. We said that this is a design book by two designers, and those



aren’t design questions—they’re existential, philosophical questions. That’s all
true, and we stand by that position.
But it’s not the whole story.
The real problem with those questions isn’t that they’re too hard; it’s that
they define life as a transaction (i.e., they’re the wrong question). They treat the
meaning and purpose of life as solvable problems that deserve to be answered
once and for all, and that’s not what either of us (or most wisdom tradition
leaders or the Harvard Study of Adult Development or probably your
grandmother) believe. Life is not a transaction that we can win. There is no
finish line. Life is simply the continual flow of particular experiences and
engagements in which we participate. We really do believe that every one of us
holds the possibility of continual change and lifelong growth—we are all a
becoming. That means that your future self will know things and value things
and see things in ways your present self can’t even imagine. So how could there
ever be a final answer to the ultimate meaning or purpose of your life if there is
no final YOU? There is no ultimate real you. There is only the ever-increasingly
revealed you.
Live right now as meaningfully as you can by accepting reality and being
available to wonder and flow and being as coherent as you can by living
purposefully day by day with the support and encouragement of a formative
community. It not only makes life more enjoyable—it moves you along the way
toward your better self, the one capable of even more meaning and purpose. We
both have come to believe that meaning is indeed found in engaging in the
journey of becoming, and purpose is fulfilled in becoming fully alive in each
unfolding moment. The meaning and purpose we all share, but each express
uniquely, is to become fully human—deeply engaged in doing worthwhile
things in the transactional world and deeply participating in the fullness of the
flow world.
If you add up all the ideas and mindsets and tools that we’ve given you in this
book and assemble them into a life practice, what you have is what philosophers
call a way—a course or mode of living life. We almost titled this book The
Designer’s Way but chose not to because that sounds like we’re telling you that
our way is the right way and ought to be your way. And that’s wrong. What



carries you forward in this never-ending person-making project is your way, and
the point of this book is to get you to design your own way. The Hagakure, an
early 1700s book of samurai wisdom, has this to say about “ways”:
It is bad when one thing becomes two. One should not look for anything
else in the Way of the Samurai. It is the same for anything that is called a
Way.
Therefore, it is inconsistent to hear something of the Way of
Confucius or the Way of the Buddha, and say that this is the Way of the
Samurai.
If one understands things in this manner, he should be able to hear
about all Ways and be more and more in accord with his own.
We share this to make clear that we are not recommending that you live by
the way of design or the way of Bill or the way of Dave. Each of us must find our
way. We can learn from others and even participate in an established way, like
Buddhism or Christianity or samurai Bushido, but ultimately, we each must
choose our way and make our way through life by it.
