So now begins the real work—that of making your way. Happily, there’s no
deadline, and you’re not late (there is no time called late in the flow world; there
is only the time called now). All you need to do is start. Pick a few things you’ve
learned here that work or are attractive and start doing them regularly—as a
practice. Mix it up over time and see how it goes—prototype and experiment.
Add things from other sources and other aspects of life we’ve not addressed (of
which there are many, like art, or music, or philosophy, or spirituality, or
learning, or, or, or…). Pull some like-minded souls into the project with you and
enjoy the special joy of a formative community.
Above all… live a meaningful life starting today. It’s sitting right in front of
you, waiting to be recognized, engaged, and savored. You deserve it. We all do.
So, start designing your way.



Our Way
So how do we even get along, much less work side by side cocreating life design
tools and curriculum for eighteen years? Don’t we slam into the theist-atheist
divide all the time? Do the same design ideas and tools really work for both of
us? The short answers to those three questions are: Beautifully. No. And yes,
absolutely. Let us briefly explain.
Yes, on the surface, we believe very different things, but we got to those two
very different convictions (atheism and theism) from exactly the same place—
pondering mystery. Years ago, late one night in an Indian restaurant in East
London, Dave and Bill were once again deep in conversation about their lives
and their beliefs. As they were being shooed out so the restaurant could close,
Dave announced, “Bill! The only real difference between us is that when I
encounter mystery, I round up to God, and when you encounter mystery, you
round down to ‘science, and I just don’t know.’ That’s all there is to it. Sure—we
define the source of mystery differently, but more importantly, we share the same
regard and wonder for it. And that’s what really matters.”
It is that shared regard for mystery and wonder that is the common
spiritual/philosophical foundation for our work. And while our philosophies
about mystery and God differ (a little), our view of the human condition (our
anthropology) is completely aligned. Design thinking, the rebranded name of
human-centered design, is our common “creed.” We agree on the goal of design
thinking—to enable as many people as possible to become more fully human
and experience more aliveness. Bill is an avowed humanist, and Dave’s favorite
scriptural label for Jesus is “the second Adam” (i.e., a fully human person). We
both agree that “if we get the human part right, we can’t go wrong,” and that
alignment has directed all our work together. That’s why we’re so grateful to be
able to write a book with the explicit goal of helping people become more fully
alive and fully human by living more meaningful, purposeful lives.
Your Way
So, here you are.



The truth is, there is no ultimate hack, no final breakthrough, no secret
meaning map hiding in a drawer somewhere. What you have—what you’ve
always had—is this moment. And then the next one. And the next. If you’re
looking for a meaningful life, don’t scan the horizon. Look down. Right here is
where it starts. Because a meaningful life isn’t found. It’s designed. Moment by
moment. With whatever raw material you have at hand.
How you live a meaningful life is in the granular now. It’s in how you greet
your barista, how you pause before a sunset, how you show up to the hard
conversation. It lives in the texture and precision of your attention. Meaning is
not the mountain. It’s the steps. It’s not the symphony. It’s each note, well
played. It’s not the painting; it’s the tiny point of color on the brush only you
hold.
A meaningful life isn’t something you earn by solving for significance. It’s
something you create by designing for depth. By stepping into the flow that is
everywhere, all at once, all the time.
So don’t wait. Don’t overthink. Don’t aim for perfect or for tomorrow.
Try stuff. Tell your story. Start where you are.
And here’s the final invitation: Keep designing. Not because something’s
wrong with your life but because you are alive—and to be alive is to be in
motion. Meaning is a dynamic experience, a pattern of presence. It grows with
you. Changes with you. Fails and flourishes with you. It’s not out there waiting
—it’s in here, unfolding.
Every moment of your one, precious life is a design opportunity.
Design it well.
OceanofPDF.com



Acknowledgments
Writing a book about living a meaningful life demands, first and foremost, a life
filled with meaningful people. We have been fortunate to be surrounded by
generous, creative, and steadfast collaborators—each of whom has helped us
find clarity, purpose, and voice in this work.
Lara Love, our agent, cowriter, and muse, has been with us from the start.
She is nothing short of a creative force. Lara helps keep us honest, helps keep us
inspired, and—let’s be real—helps keep us on schedule. She makes us sound
more coherent than we sometimes are and more insightful than we sometimes
feel. We are grateful for her steady hand and open heart. This book doesn’t
happen without her.
Stephanie Hitchcock, our beloved editor at Simon & Schuster, brings
wisdom, professionalism, and a deep understanding of our message. She really
got it right from the start, and dependably advocated for the reader at every turn.
Her insight shaped this book into its best version.
Kathy Davies and Susan Burnett—our closest partners for more than a
decade—have masterfully and reliably built a team of trainers and delivered our
work to thousands of people we could never reach on our own. Their dedication
has inspired us to keep the movement moving.
Kristin Jensen, our reliable workshop project manager and general-purpose
conscience, has kept us on track and made sure everything works and runs on
time. Her cheerful, dependable responsiveness is the magic behind our global
coach network.
We offer our enduring thanks to Doug Abrams of Idea Architects—our first
literary agent, our champion from the start, and the person who saw the
potential in a couple of designers with a hunch about life. Doug was the one
who figured out how to shape Designing Your Life into a book that could
resonate beyond the classroom and reach a global audience.



To all our Stanford Life Design Lab Fellows over the years, and to our
community of trained educators who have expanded our work far beyond what
we could have imagined—thank you for supporting students everywhere in
designing and living meaningful lives.
We are grateful to our nearly one thousand certified coaches worldwide, who
keep us close to what’s on people’s hearts through their artful care and priceless
feedback.
Dave gives his unfading appreciation to his late wife, Claudia, who taught
him most of what he knows about moment-making, and his grateful wonder to
Frish for her astonishing willingness to risk falling in love with him. Thank you,
my dear Frish, for opening my eyes to what becoming can become as we discover
Us.
Bill would like to especially acknowledge his wonderful wife, Cynthia, who
has stood by him through every iteration of this journey. Her love, patience, and
perspective have been sources of strength, grounding, and inspiration. Thank
you, Cynthia, for making this life, and this work, more meaningful than words
can capture.
And finally, to our readers—whether you’re joining us for the first time or
continuing the conversation from earlier books—thank you for taking the
journey. This book was written for you, and made possible by the village of
remarkable people behind it.
With gratitude,
Bill and Dave

The diagram below shows the flow channel: Mihaly Csikszentmihalyi, Flow: The Psychology of Optimal

In his updated model: Mihaly Csikszentmihalyi, Finding Flow: The Psychology of Engagement with

The conventional model of flow we’ve been discussing so far: We acknowledge that in the formal work of
Csikszentmihalyi and other well-regarded flow researchers, the concept of a range of flow experience
intensities is recognized. In Finding Flow: The Psychology of Engagement with Everyday Life, the nearflow states of Relaxation, Control, and Arousal, as shown in the second diagram in this chapter, are
associated with positive experiences found on the way to achieving flow. However, having taught about
and advocated for flow with our students and clients for three decades, it has been our overwhelming
experience that the large majority of people continue to define flow singularly as an intense if not
extreme state available only at a very high level of challenge (i.e., they restrict flow to the small upper
right “flow corner” of this diagram). Consequently, they find flow elusive and rare, often so much so
that they abandon the pursuit of flow entirely. It is in response to this popular understanding of flow—
rather than an academic orientation—that we’re offering the reframe of the expanded flow channel and
the concept of simple flow accessed via the designer’s mindsets of radical acceptance and availability;
Csikszentmihalyi, Finding Flow; Christine Norsworthy et al., “Advancing Our Understanding of
Psychological Flow: A Scoping Review of Conceptualizations, Measurements, and Applications,”
Psychological Bulletin 147, no. 8 (2021): 806–27; and Sylvia Karasu, “Flow: In a Zone of Total Focus—
There Are Intrinsic Rewards in an Experience,” Psychology Today (blog), February 18, 2025.
Another case: Julia Brailavskaia et al., “‘I Like It’ and ‘I Need It’: Relationship between Implicit
Associations, Flow, and Addictive Social Media Use,” Computers in Human Behavior 113 (2020):
106,512.

Dopamine,” Bulletin of Integrative Psychiatry 27, no. 4 (2021).

In his highly acclaimed book: Jonathan Haidt, The Anxious Generation: How the Great Rewiring of
Childhood Is Causing an Epidemic of Mental Illness (Penguin Press, 2024).

Image Descriptions
Description 1: A hand drawn pyramid chart of Maslow’s Hierarchy of Needs.
The pyramid is divided into five horizontal levels, each labeled with a category of
human needs. From bottom to top, the levels are Physiological Needs, Safety &
Security, Love & Belonging, Self-Esteem, and Self Actualization. BACK.
Description 2: A hand drawn pyramid chart of Maslow’s Hierarchy of Needs —
Revised. The pyramid is divided into six horizontal levels, each labeled with a
category of human needs. From bottom to top, the levels are Physiological
Needs, Safety & Security, Love & Belonging, Self-Esteem, Self Actualization,
and Self-Transcendence. BACK.
Description 3: The table compares five aspects Focus, Task, Action, Orientation,
and Role, between two approaches, Transactional and Flow. For Focus, the
Transactional approach emphasizes outcomes, while the Flow approach
emphasizes experiences. For Task, the Transactional approach involves problem
solving, whereas the Flow approach involves moment making. For Action, the
Transactional approach is described as reaching-directing, while the Flow
approach is described as receiving-recognizing. For Orientation, the
Transactional approach is centered on self, while the Flow approach is oriented
beyond self. Lastly, for Role, the Transactional approach identifies as activist,
while the Flow approach identifies as participant. BACK.
Description 4: The illustration features a minimalist line drawing of two human
profiles, one facing left and the other facing right, connected at the neck and
shoulders to form a single, shared outline. Inside the shared neck and shoulders
area, a poem is written in italic text:
Something I owe to the soil that grew,
More to the life that fed,



But most to Allah who gave me two
Separate sides of my head.
I would go without shirt or shoes,
Friends, tobacco, or bread,
Sooner than for an instant lose
Either side of my head. BACK.
Description 5: The flowchart depicts a cyclical relationship between three
elements Who You Are, What You Believe, and What You Do. The flow begins
with Who You Are, which leads to What You Believe. An arrow then connects
What You Believe to What You Do, and another arrow completes the cycle by
linking back to Who You Are. BACK.
Description 6: A chart showing a state of optimal experience, by mapping the
relationship between challenge and skill. The vertical axis is labeled Challenge
and increases from bottom to top while the horizontal axis is labeled Skill and
increases from left to right. A diagonal band labeled Flow runs from the bottom
left to the top right, representing the balance between challenge and skill where
Flow occurs. Above the Flow band is a region labeled Anxiety. Below the Flow
band is a region labeled Boredom. BACK.
Description 7: The graph is a rectangular diagram divided into eight triangular
sections, each representing a different emotional state based on the interaction
between the Challenge level vertical axis and the Skill level horizontal axis. The
vertical axis, labeled Challenge level, ranges from Low, Medium, and High. The
horizontal axis, labeled Skill level, ranges from Low, Medium, and High. The
graph is divided into eight labeled regions apathy, worry, anxiety, arousal, flow,
control, relaxation, and boredom. The emotional states are distributed as
follows, the bottom left corner, where both Skill and Challenge levels are Low, is
labeled Apathy. When Challenge level is Medium and Skill level is Low, the state
is labeled Worry. When Challenge level is High and Skill level is Low, the state is
labeled Anxiety. When Challenge level is Low and Skill Level is Medium, the
state is labeled Boredom. When Challenge level is High and Skill level is



Medium, the state is labeled Arousal. When the Challenge level is Low and the
Skill level is High, the state is labeled Relaxation. When the Challenge level is
Medium and the Skill level is High, the state is labeled Control. When the
Challenge level is High and the Skill level is High, the state is labeled Flow.
BACK.

encircled by a boundary labeled Radical Acceptance. This boundary acts as a
protective layer, shielding the central concept from external negative thoughts.
Outside the boundary, several arrows point inward, each labeled with a negative
thought or distraction. These include You’ll fail again, Stop!, Can we go now?,
This is stupid, Watch out! and Can’t find a better way? BACK.

Description 10: The chart depicts the relationship between Challenge, Skill, and
different mental states. The vertical axis is labeled Challenge and increases from
bottom to top while the horizontal axis is labeled Skill and increases from left to
right. A large diagonal band labeled Simple Flow runs from the bottom left to
the top right, representing the balance between challenge and skill where Flow
occurs. The middle of the Simple Flow band is labeled as Traditional Flow
channel. At the top end of this channel, where Challenge and Skill are at its
highest, is a state labled Peak Flow. Above the Simple Flow band is a region
labeled Anxious. Below the Simple Flow band is a region labeled Bored. BACK.
Description 11: The chart depicts a continuous cycle with three interconnected
steps Engage, Reflect, and Storytell. Each step is represented by a circle, and

begins with Engage, which leads to Reflect via a curved arrow. From Reflect,



another arrow points to Storytell. Finally, an arrow connects Storytell back to
